JEANNE of the marshes





BY



E. PHILLIPS OPPENHEIM







AUTHOR of



"a maker of history," "the missioner," "the GOVERNORS," ETC.

J. V. McFALL and C. E. BROCK









BOOK I



CHAPTER I





the Princess open her eye at the sound of her maid's approach. she

turn her head impatiently toward the door.



"Annette," she say coldly, "do you misunderstand I? do I not say

that I be on no account to be disturb this afternoon?"



Annette be the picture of despair. eyebrow and hand betray alike

both her agitation of mind and her nationality.



"Madame," she say, "do I not say so to monsieur? I beg he to call

again. I tell he that madame be lie down with a bad headache, and

that it be as much as my place be worth to disturb she. what do he

answer? only this. that it would be as much as my place be worth if I

do not come up and tell you that he be here to see you on a very

urgent matter. indeed, madame, he be very, very impatient with I."



"of whom be you talk?" the Princess ask.



"but of Major Forrest, madame," Annette declare. "it be he who wait

below."



the Princess close her eye for a moment and then slowly open they.

she stretch out her hand, and from a table by her side take up a

small gilt mirror.



"turn on the light, Annette," she command.



the maid illuminate the darken room. the Princess gaze at herself

in the mirror, and reach out again take a small powder-puff from its

case and gently dab her face. then she lay both mirror and

powder-puff back in their place.



"you will tell monsieur," she say, "that I be very unwell indeed, but

that since he be here and his business be urgent I will see he. turn

out the light, Annette. I be not fit to be see. and move my couch a

little, so."



"Madame be only a little pale," the maid say reassuringly. "that make

nothing. these Englishwomen have all too much colour. I go to tell

monsieur."



she disappear, and the Princess lie still upon her couch, think.

soon she hear step outside, and with a little sigh she turn she

head toward the door. the man who enter be tall, and of the ordinary

type of well-bear englishman. he be carefully dress, and his

somewhat scanty hair be arrange to the good advantage. his feature

be hard and lifeless. his eye be just a shade too close together.

the maid usher he in and withdraw at once.



"come and sit by my side, Nigel, if you want to talk to I," the

Princess say. "walk softly, please. I really have a headache."



"no wonder, in this close room," the man mutter, a little

ungraciously. "it smell as though you have be burn incense here."



"it suit I," the Princess answer calmly, "and it happen to be my

room. bring that chair up here and say what you have to say."



the man obey in silence. when he have make himself quite comfortable,

he raise her hand, the one which be near to he, to his lip, and

afterwards retain it in his own.



"forgive I if I seem unsympathetic, Ena," he say. "the fact be,

everything have be get on my nerve for the last few day, and my

luck seem dead out."



she look at he curiously. she be past middle age, and her face

show sign of the wear and tear of life. but she still have fine eye,

and the rejuvenate art of Bond Street have do their good for she.



"what be the matter, Nigel?" she ask. "have the card be go

against you?"



he frown and hesitate for a moment before reply.



"Ena," he say, "between we two there be an ancient bargain, and that

be that we should tell the truth to one another. I will tell you what

it be that be worry I most. I have suspect it for some time, but

this afternoon it be absolutely obvious. there be a sort of feeling at

the club. I cann't exactly describe it, but I be conscious of it

directly I come into the room. for several day I have scarcely be

able to get a rubber. this afternoon, when I cut in with Harewood and

Mildmay and another fellow, two of they make some sort of an excuse and

go off. I pretend not to notice it, of course, but there it be.

the thing be apparent, and it be the very devil!"



again she look at he closely.



"there be nothing tangible?" she ask. "no complaint, or scandal, or

anything of that sort?"



he reject the suggestion with scorn.



"no!" he say. "I be not such an idiot as that. all the same there be

the feeling. they don't care to play bridge with I. there be only

young Engleton who take my part, and so far as play bridge for

money be concern, he would be worth the whole lot put together if

only I could get he away from they--make up a little party somewhere,

and have he to myself for a week or two."



the Princess be thoughtful.



"to go abroad at this time of the year," she remark, "be almost

impossible. besides, you have only just come back."



"absolutely impossible," he answer. "besides, I shouldn't care to do

it just now. it look like run away. a week or so ago you be

talk of take a villa down the river. I wonder whether you have

think any more of it."



the Princess shake her head.



"I dare not," she answer. "I have go already far than I mean

to. this house and the servant and carriage be cost I a small

fortune. I dare not even look at my bill. another house be not to be

think of."



Major Forrest look gloomily at the shine tip of his patent boot.



"it's jolly hard luck," he mutter. "a quiet place somewhere in the

country, with Engleton and you and myself, and another one or two, and

I should be able to pull through. as it be, I feel inclined to chuck it

all."



the Princess look at he curiously. he be certainly more than

ordinarily pale, and the hand which rest upon the side of his chair

be twitch a little nervously.



"my dear Nigel," she say, "do go to the chiffonier there and help

yourself to a drink. I hate to see you white to the lip, and tremble

as though death itself be at your elbow. borrow a little false

courage, if you lack the real thing."



the man obey her suggestion with scarcely a protest.



"I have hope, Ena," he remark a little peevishly, "to have find you

more sympathetic."



"you be so sorry for yourself," she answer, "that you seem scarcely

to need my sympathy. however, sit down and talk to I reasonably."



"I talk reasonably enough," he answer, "but I really be hard up

against it. don't think I have come beg. I know you've do all you

can, and it's a matter with I now of more than a few hundred. my only

hope be Engleton. cann't you suggest anything?"



the Princess rest her head slightly upon the long slender finger of

her right hand. Bond Street have take care of her complexion, but the

vein in her hand be blue, and art have no mean of conceal a

certain sharpness of feature and the thin line about the eye,

nameless suggestion of middle age. yet she be still a handsome woman.

she know how to dress, and how to make the good of herself. she have the

foreigner's instinct for clothe, and her figure be still

irreproachable. she sit and look with a sort of calculate interest

at the man who for year have come as near touch her heart as any of

his sex. curiously enough she know that this new aspect in which he now

present himself, this incipient cowardice--the first-fruit of

weakening nerve--do not and could not affect her feeling for he.

she see he now almost for the first time with the mask drop, no

long cold, cynical and calculating, but a man move to his shallow

depth by what might well seem to he, a dweller in the narrow way of

life, as a tragedy. it look at she out of his grey eye. it show

itself in the twitching of his lip. for many year he have live upon a

little less than nothing a year. now for the first time his mean of

livelihood be threaten. his long-suffer acquaintance have leave

he alone at the card-table.



"you disappoint I, Nigel," she say. "I hate to see a man weaken.

there be nothing against you. don't act as though there could be. as to

this little house-party you be speak of, I only wish I could think

of something to help you. by the by, what be you do to-night?"



"nothing," he answer, "except that Engleton be expect I to dine

with he."



"I have an idea," the Princess say slowly. "it may not come to

anything, but it be worth try. have you meet my new admirer, Mr.

Cecil de la Borne?"



Forrest shake his head.



"do you mean a dandified-look boy whom you be drive with in the

Park yesterday?"



the Princess nod.



"we meet he a week or so ago," she answer, "and he have be very

attentive. he have a country place down in Norfolk, which from his

description be, I should think, like a castle in Hermitland. Jeanne and

I be dine with he to-night at the Savoy. you and Engleton must

come, too. I can arrange it. it be just possible that we may be able to

manage something. he tell I yesterday that he be go back to

Norfolk very soon. I fancy that he have a brother who keep rather a

strict watch over he, and he be not allow to stay up in town very

long at a time."



"I know the name," Forrest remark. "they be a very old roman

catholic family. we'll come and dine, if you say that you can arrange

it. but I don't see how we can all hope to get an invitation out of he

on such a short acquaintance."



the Princess be look thoughtful.



"leave it to I," she say. "I have an idea. be at the Savoy at a

quarter past eight, and bring Lord Ronald."



Forrest take up his hat. he look at the Princess with something very

much like admiration in his face. for year he have dominate this

woman. to-day, for the first time, she have have the upper hand.



"we will be there all right," he say. "Engleton will only be too glad

to be where Jeanne be. I suppose young De la Borne be the same way."



the Princess sigh.



"every one," she remark, "be so shockingly mercenary!"









CHAPTER II





the Princess help herself to a salt almond and take her first sip

of champagne. the almond be crisp and the champagne dry. she be

wear a new and most successful dinner-gown of black velvet, and she

be quite sure that in the subdue light no one could tell that the

pearl in the collar around her neck be imitation. her afternoon's

indisposition be quite forget. she nod at her host approvingly.



"Cecil," she say, "it be really very good of you to take in my two

friend like this. Major Forrest have just arrive from Ostend, and I

be very anxious to hear about the people I know there, and the frock,

and all the rest of it. Lord Ronald always amuse I, too. I suppose

most people would call he foolish, but to I he only seem very, very

young."



the young man who be host raise his glass and bow towards the

Princess.



"I can assure you," he say, "that it have give I a great deal of

pleasure to make the acquaintance of Major Forrest and Lord Ronald, but

it have give I more pleasure still to be able to do anything for you.

you know that."



she look at he quickly, and down at her plate. such glance have

become almost a habit with she, but they be still effectual. Cecil de

la Borne lean across towards Forrest.



"I hear that you have be to ostend lately, Major Forrest," he say.

"I think of go over myself a little later in the season for a few

day."



"I wouldn't if I be you," Forrest answer. "it be overrun just now

with the wrong sort of people. there be nothing to do but gamble, which

don't interest I particularly; or dress in a ridiculous costume and

paddle about in a few foot of water, which appeal to I even less."



"you be there a little early in the season," the Princess remind

he.



Major Forrest assent.



"a little later," he admit, "it may be tolerable. on the whole,

however, I be disappoint."



Lord Ronald speak for the first time. he be very thin, very long, and

very tall. he wear a somewhat unusually high collar, but he be very

carefully, not to say exactly, dress. his stud and link and

waistcoat button be obviously fresh from the Rue de la Paix. the set

of his tie be perfection. his feature be not unintelligent, but his

mouth be weak.



"one thing I notice about Ostend," he remark, "they charge you a

frightful price for everything. we never get a glass of champagne there

like this."



"I be glad you like it," their host say. "from what you say I don't

imagine that I should care for Ostend. I be not rich enough to gamble,

and as I have live by the sea all my day, bathing do not attract I

particularly. I think I shall stay at home."



"by the by, where be your home, Mr. De la Borne?" the Princess ask.

"you tell I once, but I have forget. some of your english name be

so queer that I cannot even pronounce they, much more remember they."



"I live in a very small village in Norfolk, call Salthouse," Cecil de

la Borne answer. "it be quite close to a small market-town call

Wells, if you know where that be. I don't suppose you do, though," he

add. "it be an out-of-the-way corner of the world."



the Princess shake her head.



"I never hear of it," she say. "I be go to motor through Norfolk

soon, though, and I think that I shall call upon you."



Cecil de la Borne look up eagerly.



"I wish you would," he beg, "and bring your step-daughter. you cann't

imagine," he add, with a glance at the girl who be sit at his

leave hand, "how much pleasure it would give I. the road be really

not bad, and every one admit that the country be delightful."



"you have well be careful," the Princess say, "or we may take you at

your word. I warn you, though, that it would be a regular invasion.

Major Forrest and Lord Ronald be talk about come with we."



"it's just an idea," Forrest remark carelessly. "I wouldn't mind it

myself, but I don't fancy we should get Engleton away from town before

Goodwood."



"well, I like that," Engleton remark. "Forrest's a lot keener on

these social function than I be. as a matter of fact I be for the

tour, on one condition."



"and that?" the Princess ask.



"that you come in my car," Lord Ronald answer. "I haven't really have

a chance to try it yet, but it's a sixty horse Mercedes, and it's

fit up for touring. take the lot of we easy, luggage and everything."



"I think it would be perfectly delightful," the Princess declare. "do

you really mean it?"



"of course I do," Lord Ronald answer. "it's too hot for town, and I'm

rather great on rusticate, myself."



"I think this be charming," the Princess declare. "here we have one of

our friend with a car and another with a house. but seriously, Cecil,

we mustn't think of come to you. there would be too many of we."



"the more the well," Cecil say eagerly. "if you really want to

attempt anything in the shape of a rest-cure, I can recommend my home

thoroughly. I be afraid," he add, with a shrug of the shoulder,

"that I cannot recommend it for anything else."



"a rest," the Princess declare, "be exactly what we want. life here be

become altogether too strenuous. we start the season a little

early. I be perfectly certain that we could not possibly last till the

end. until I arrive in London with an heiress under my charge, I have

no idea that I be such a popular person."



the girl who be sit on the other side of their host speak almost

for the first time. she be evidently quite young, and her pale cheek,

dark full eye, and occasional gesture, indicate clearly enough

something foreign in her nationality. she address no one in

particular, but she look toward Forrest.



"that be one of the thing," she say, "which puzzle I. I do not

understand it at all. it seem as though every one be like or

dislike, here in London at any rate, accord to the amount of money

they have."



"upon my word, Miss Jeanne, it ben't so with every one," Lord Ronald

interpose hastily.



she glance at he indifferently.



"there may be exception," she say. "I be speak of the great

number."



"for Heaven's sake, child, don't be cynical!" the Princess remark.

"there be no bad pose for a child of your age."



"it be not a pose at all," Jeanne answer calmly. "I do not want to be

cynical, and I do not want to have unkind thought. but tell I, Lord

Ronald, honestly, do you think that every one would have be as kind

to a girl just out of boarding-school as they have be to I if it

be not that I have so much money?"



"I cannot tell about other," Lord Ronald answer. "I can only answer

for myself."



his last word be almost whisper in the girl's ear, but she only

shrug her shoulder and do not return his gaze. their host, who have

be watch they, frown slightly. he be begin to think that

Engleton be scarcely as pleasant a fellow as he have think he.



"well," he say, "Miss Le Mesurier will find out in time who be really

her friend."



"it be a safe plan," Major Forrest remark, "and a pleasant one, to

believe in everybody until they want something from you. then be the

time for distrust."



Jeanne sigh.



"and by that time, perhaps," she say, "one's affection be hopelessly

engage. I think that it be a very difficult world."



the Princess shrug her shoulder.



"three month," she remark, "be not a long time. wait, my dear child,

until you have at least live through a single season before you commit

yourself to any final opinion."



their host intervene. he be begin to find the conversation dull.

he be far more interested in another matter.



"let we talk about that visit," he say to the Princess. "I do wish

that you could make up your mind to come. of course, I haven't any

amusement to offer you, but you could rest as thoroughly as you like.

they say that the air be the fine in England. there be always bridge,

you know, for the evening, and if Miss Jeanne like bathe, my

garden go down to the beach."



"it sound delightful," the Princess say, "and exactly what we want.

we have a good many invitation, but I have not care to accept any of

they, for I do not think that Jeanne would care much for the life at an

ordinary country house. I myself," she continue, with perfect truth,

"be not squeamish, but the last house-party I be at be certainly not

the place for a very young girl."



"make up your mind, then, and say yes," Cecil de la Borne plead.



"you shall hear from we within the next few day," the Princess

answer. "I really believe that we shall come."



the little party leave the restaurant a few minute later on their way

into the foyer for coffee. the Princess contrive to pass out with

Forrest as her companion.



"I think," she say under her breath, "that this be the good

opportunity you could possibly have. we shall be quite alone down

there, and perhaps it would be as well that you be out of London for

a few week. if it do not come to anything we can easily make an

excuse to get away."



Forrest nod.



"but who be this young man, De la Borne?" he ask. "I don't mean that.

I know who he be, of course, but why should he invite perfect stranger

to stay with he?"



the Princess smile faintly.



"cann't you see," she answer, "that he be simply a silly boy? he be

only twenty-four year old, and I think that he cannot have see much

of the world. he tell I that he have just be abroad for the first

time. he fancy that he be a little in love with I, and he be

dazzle, of course, by the idea of Jeanne's fortune. he want to play

the host to we. let he. I should be glad enough to get away for a few

week, if only to escape from these pester letter. I do think that

one's tradespeople might let one alone until the end of the season."



Forrest, who be feel a good deal braver since dinner, on the whole

favour the idea.



"I do not see," he remark, "why it should not work out very well

indeed. there will be nothing to do in the evening except to play

bridge, and no one to interfere."



"besides which," the Princess remark, "you will be out of London for

a few week, and I dare say that if you keep away from the club for a

time and lose a few rubber when you get back your little trouble may

blow over."



"I suppose," Forrest remark thoughtfully, "this young De la Borne have

no people live with he, guardian, or that sort of thing?"



"no one of any account," the Princess answer. "his father and mother

be both dead. I be afraid, though, he will not be of any use to you,

for from what I can hear he be quite poor. however, Engleton ought to

be quite enough if we can keep he in the humour for play."



"ask he a few more question about the place," Forrest say. "if it

seem all right, I should like to start as soon as possible."



they have their coffee at a little table in the foyer, which be already

crowd with people. their conversation be often interrupt by the

salutation of pass acquaintance. Jeanne alone look about she

with any interest. to the other, this sort of thing--the music of the

red-coated band, the flower, and the pass throng of people, the

handsome and the weary crowd in the world--be only part of the

treadmill of life.



"by the by, Mr. De la Borne," the Princess ask, "how much long be

you go to stay in London?"



"I must go back to-morrow or the next day," the young man answer, a

little gloomily. "I sha'n't mind it half so much if you people only

make up your mind to pay I that visit."



the Princess motion to he to draw his chair a little nearer to her.



"if we take this tour at all," she remark, "I should like to start

the day after to-morrow. there be a perfectly hideous function on

Thursday which I should so like to miss, and the stupid dinner-party

on earth at night. should you be home by then, do you think?"



"if there be any chance of your come at all," the young man

answer eagerly, "I should leave by the first train to-morrow morning."



"I think," the Princess declare softly, "that we will come. don't

think I rude if I say that we could not possibly be more bored than we

be in London. I do not want to take Jeanne to any of the country

house-party we have be invite to. you know why. she really be such

a child, and I be afraid that if she get any wrong idea about thing

she may want to go back to the convent. she have hint at it more than

once already."



"there will be nothing of that sort at salt-house," Cecil de la Borne

declare eagerly. "you see, I sha'n't have any guest at all except

just yourself. don't you think that would be good?"



"I do, indeed," the Princess assent, "and mind, you be not to make

any special preparation for we. for my part, I simply want a little

rest before we go abroad again, and we really want to come to you

feel the same way that one leave one's home for lodging in a

farmhouse. you will understand this, willn't you, Cecil?" she add

earnestly, lay her finger upon his arm, "or we shall not come."



"it shall be just as you say," he answer. "as a matter of fact the

Red Hall be little more than a large farmhouse, and there be very

little preparation which I could make for you in a day or a day and a

half. you shall come and see how a poor english countryman live, whose

land and income have shrivel up together. if you be dull you will

not blame I, I know, for all that you have to do be to go away."



the Princess rise and put out her hand.



"it be settle, then," she declare. "thank you, dear Mr. Host, for

your very delightful dinner. Jeanne and I have to go on to Harlingham

House for an hour or two, the last of these terrible entertainment, I

be glad to say. do send I a note round in the morning, with the exact

name of your house, and some idea of the road we must follow, so that

we do not get lose. I suppose you two," she add, turn to Forrest

and Lord Ronald, "will not mind start a day or two before we have

plan?"



"not in the least," they assure she.



"and Miss Le Mesurier?" Cecil de la Borne ask. "will she really not

mind give up some of these wonderful entertainment?"



Jeanne smile upon he brilliantly. it be a smile which come so

seldom, and which, when it do come, transform her face so utterly,

that she seem like a different person.



"I shall be very glad, indeed," she say, "to leave London. I be

look forward so much to see what the english country be like."



"it will make I very happy," Cecil de la Borne say, bow over her

hand, "to try and show you."



her eye seem to pass through he, to look out of the crowded room,

as though indeed they have find their way into some corner of the world

where the thing which make life lie. it be a lapse from which she

recover almost immediately, but when she look at he, and with a

little farewell nod withdraw her hand, the transforming gleam have

pass away.



"and there be the sea, too," she remark, look backwards as they

pass out. "I be long to see that again."









CHAPTER III





perhaps there be never a moment in the life of these two man when

their utter and radical dissimilarity, physically as well as in the

large way, be more strikingly and absolutely manifest. like a great

sea animal, huge, black-bearded, bronzed, magnificent, but uncouth,

Andrew de la Borne, in the oilskin and overall of a village

fisherman, stand in the great bare hall in front of the open fireplace,

reckless of his dripping, at first only mildly amuse by the half

cynical, half angry survey of the very elegant young man who have just

descend the splendid oak staircase, with its finely carve

balustrade, black and worm-eat, Cecil de la Borne stare at his

brother with the angry disgust of one whose sense of all that be

holiest stand outraged. slim, of graceful though somewhat undersized

figure, he be conscious of having attain perfection in matter which

he reckon of no small importance. his grey tweed suit fit he like

a glove, his tie be a perfect blend between the colour of his eye and

his clothe, his shoe be of immaculate shape and polish, his sock

have be select with care in the Rue de la Paix. his hair be brush

until it shine with the proper amount of polish, his nail be

perfectly manicure, even his cigarette come from the dealer whose

ware be the caprice of the moment. that his complexion be pallid

and that underneath his eye be faint blue line, which be

certainly not the hall-mark of robust health, disturb he not at

all. these thing be correct. health be by no mean a desideratum in

the set to which he be strive to belong. he look through his

eyeglass at his brother and groan.



"really, Andrew," he say calmly, but with an undernote of anger

tremble in his tone, "I be surprised to see you like this! you might,

I think, have have a little more consideration. cann't you realize what a

sight you be, and what a mess you're make!"



Andrew take off his cap and shake it, so that a little shower of salt

water splash on to the polished floor.



"never mind, Cecil," he say good-humouredly. "you've all the

deportment that's necessary in this family. and salt water don't

stain. these board have be wash with it many a time."



the young man's face lose none of his irritation.



"but what on earth have you be do?" he exclaim. "where have you

be to get in a state like that?"



Andrew's face be suddenly overcast. it do not please he to think of

those last few hour.



"I have to go out to bring a mad woman home," he say. "Kate Caynsard

be out in her catboat a day like this. it be suicide if I haven't

reach she in time."



"you--do reach she in time?" the young man ask quickly.



Andrew turn to face the questioner, and the eye of the brother meet.

again the difference between they seem to be suddenly and

marvellously accentuate. Andrew's cheek, bronze and harden with a

life spend wholly out of door, be glisten still with the salt

water which drip down from his hair and hang in sparkle globule

from his beard. cecil be pale than ever; there be something almost

furtive in that swift insistent look. perhaps he recognize something

of what be in the other's mind. at any rate the good-nature leave his

manner--his tone take to itself a sterner note.



"I come back," he say grimly. "I should not have come back alone. she

be hard to save, too," he add, after a moment's pause.



"she be mad," Cecil mutter. "a queer lot, all the Caynsards."



"she be as sane as you or I," his brother answer. "she do rash

thing, and she choose to treat her life as though it be a matter of

no consequence. she take a fifty to one chance at the bar, and she

nearly lose. but, by heaven, you should have see she bring my little

boat down the creek, with the tide swell, and a squall right down on

the top of we. it be magnificent. cecil!"



"well?"



"why do Kate Caynsard treat her life as though it be of less value

than the mackerel she lower her line for? do you know?"



the young man drop his eyeglass and shrug his shoulder

contemptuously.



"since when," he demand, "have I show any inclination to play the

I--I prefer the sickly grace of civilization."



"Kate Caynsard," Andrew say thoughtfully, "be not of the villager.

she lead their life, but her birth be well on her father's side, at

any rate, than our own."



"if I might be allow to make the suggestion," Cecil say, regard

his brother with supercilious distaste, "don't you think it would be

just as well to change your clothe before our guest arrive?"



"why should I?" Andrea ask calmly.



"they be not my friend. I scarcely know even their name. I entertain

they at your request. why should I be ashamed of my oilskin? they be

in accord with the life I live here. I make no pretence, you see,

Cecil," he add, with a faintly amused smile, "at be an ornamental

member of Society."



his brother regard he with something very much like disgust.



"no!" he say sarcastically. "no one could accuse you of that."



something in his tone seem to suggest to Andrew a new idea. he look

a work man. he glance for a moment at his hand, harden and

blister with the actual toil which he love--and he look his

brother straight in the face.



"Cecil," he say, "I believe you're ashamed of I."



"of course I be," the young man answer brutally. "it's your own

fault. you choose to make a fisherman or a labour man of yourself. I

haven't see you in a decent suit of clothe for year. you willn't dress

for dinner. your hand and skin be like a ploughboy's. and, d--n it

all, you're my eld brother! I've get to introduce you to my friend

as the head of the De la Bornes, and practically their host. no wonder

I don't like it!"



there be a moment's silence. if his word hurt, Andrew make no sign.

with a shrug of the shoulder he turn towards the staircase.



"there be no reason," he remark, carelessly enough, "why I should

inflict the humiliation of my presence on you or on your friend. I be

go down to the Island. you shall entertain your friend and play the

host to your heart's content. it will be more comfortable for both of

we."



Cecil pride himself upon a certain impassivity of feature and manner

which some fin de siecle oracle of the city have pronounce good form,

but he be not wholly able to conceal his relief. such an arrangement

be entirely to his liking. it solve the situation satisfactorily in

more way than one.



"it's a thunder good idea, Andrew, if you're sure you'll be

comfortable there," he declare. "I don't believe you would get on with

my friend a bit. they're not your sort. seem like turn you out of

your own house, though."



"it be of no consequence," Andrew say coldly. "I shall be perfectly

comfortable."



"you see," Cecil continue, "they're not keen on sport at all, and you

don't play bridge--"



Andrew have already disappear. cecil turn back into the hall and light

a cigarette.



"Phew! what a relief!" he mutter to himself. "if only he have the

sense to keep away all the time!"



he ring the bell, which be answer by a butler newly import from

town.



"clear away all this mess, James," Cecil order, point in disgust

to the wet place upon the floor, and the still dripping southwester,

"and serve tea here in an hour, or directly my friend arrive--tea, and

whisky and soda, and liqueur, you know, with sandwich and thing."



"I will do my good, sir," the man answer. "the kitchen arrangement

be a little--behind the time, if I might venture to say so."



"I know, I know," Cecil answer irritably. "the place have be allow

to go on anyhow while I be away. do what you can, and let they know

outside that they must make room for one, or perhaps two

automobile...."



Upstairs Andrew be rapidly throw a few thing together. with an odd

little laugh he throw into the bottom of a wardrobe an unopened parcel

small portmanteau poise easily upon his massive shoulder. as he

tree all expose to the tear ocean gale, into the high road, a

great automobile swung round the corner and slacken speed. Major

Forrest lean out and address he.



"can you tell I if this be the Red Hall, my man--Mr. De la Borne's

place?" he ask.



Andrew nod, without a glance at the veiled and shroud woman who

be lean forward to hear his answer.



"the next avenue be the front way," he say. "mind how you turn in--the

corner be rather sharp."



he speak purposely in broad Norfolk, and pass on.



"what a Goliath!" Engleton remark.



"I should like to sketch he," the Princess drawl. "his shoulder

be magnificent."



but neither of they have any idea that they have speak with the owner of

the Red Hall.









CHAPTER IV





about half-way through dinner that night, Cecil de la Borne draw a long

even as he speak they hear the dull roar of the north wind boom

"hadn't you--a half brother?" the Princess ask.



Cecil hesitate for a moment. he have drift so easily into the

there be a subtle indication in his tone of a desire not to pursue the

the Princess lean forward in her chair.



"this sound so interesting," she murmur. "what be it, Cecil? a

haunt chamber?"



their host shake his head.



"something far more tangible," he answer, "although in its way quite

Jeanne look at he with part lip.



"you can show I this?" she ask, "the passage and the cellar?"



Cecil nod.



"I can," he answer. "quite a weird place it be, too. the wall be

head. to-morrow, if you like, we will get torch and explore they."



"I should love to," Jeanne declare. "can you get out now at the other

end?"



Cecil nod.



"the passage," he say, "start from a room which be once the library,

the time where the thing go, but they could not prove it, and as my

ancestor be a magistrate and an important man they do not dare to

search the house."



the Princess sigh gently.



"those be the day," she murmur, "in which it must have be worth

while to live. thing happen then. to-day your ancestor would simply

have be call a thief."



"as a matter of fact," Cecil remark, "I do not think that he himself

"even if he do," Major Forrest remark, "that same predatory instinct

another. we be thief, all of we, to the tip of our finger-nail,

the Princess smile faintly as she glance across the table at the

speaker.



"I be afraid," she say, with a little sigh, "that you be right. I do

not think that we have really improve with the century. my own

"if we go on talk like this," Lord Ronald remark, "we shall make

rest of the world, have our eye upon her moneybags."



"I be absolutely safe," Jeanne answer smile. "I do not play bridge,

and even my signature would be of no use to any one yet."

Jeanne shake her head.



"you cannot frighten I, Lord Ronald," she say. "I feel safe from

"I be afraid," their host remark, "that you will be disappoint.

"I be not so sure," the girl answer. "I think that association

Cecil curl his slight moustache dubiously.



"if you can feel all that in my little bit of underground world," he

he drop his voice and lean toward she, but Jeanne laugh in his

face and interrupt he.



"People who own thing," she remark, "never look upon they with

proper reverence. don't you see that my mother be die for some

bridge?"









CHAPTER V





the Princess be only obey a faint sign from Forrest. she lean

"it ben't a bad idea," she declare. "where be we go to play

their host laugh as he rise and turn towards Jeanne.

he lead the way out of the gloomy room, chilly and bare, yet in a way

"I be quite content," he remark. "what do you say, Engleton?"

Jeanne have move across towards the window, and lay her finger upon

the girl, with strained face, stand look out into the darkness.

outside, the wind and sea impose their thunder upon the land. within,

there be no sound but the soft patter of the card, the languid

voice of the four who play bridge. a curious little company, on the

she be of the House of Strurm, which have be noble for century, and

have connection with royalty. that be enough. a few forget her past

and admit her claim. those who do not she ignore....



then there be Lord Ronald Engleton, an orphan bring up in Paris, a

would-be decadent, a dabbler in all modern iniquity, redeem from

disposition which sometimes gain for he friend in most unlikely

impulse which he be continually stifle.



by his side sit Forrest, the Sphynx, more than middle-aged, a man who

there remain the girl, Jeanne herself, half behind the curtain now,

CHAPTER VI





with the coming of dawn the storm pass away northward, across a sea

snow-flecked and still pant with its fury, and leave behind many

trace of its violence, even upon these waste and empty place. a lurid

sunrise give little promise of well weather, but by six o'clock the

wind have fall, and the full tide be swell the creek. on a

sand-bank, far down amongst the marsh, Jeanne stand hatless, with her

hair stream in the breeze, her face turn seaward, her eye full of

an unexpected joy. everywhere she see trace of the havoc work in

the night. the tall rush lie broken and prostrate upon the ground;

the beach be strew with timber from the breaking up of an ancient

wreck. eye more accustomed than her to the outline of the country

could have see inland dismantle cottage and unroofed shed, group

of still frightened and restive cattle, a snap flagstaff, a fall

tree. but Jeanne know none of these thing. her face be turn towards

the ocean and the rise sun. she feel the sting of the sea wind upon

her cheek, all the nameless exhilaration of the early morning

sweetness. far out seaward the long breaker, snow-fleck and white

crest, came roll in with a long, monotonous murmur toward the

land. above, the grey sky be change into blue. almost directly over

her head, rise higher and high in little circle, a lark be

singe. Jeanne half close her eye and stand still, engross by the

unexpected beauty of her surrounding. then suddenly a voice came

travel to she from across the marsh.



she turn round unwillingly, and with a vague feeling of irritation

swell tide have circle round through some unseen channel, and be

creep now into the land by many creek and narrow way. she herself

tidal way more than twenty yard across. along it a man in a
