wait for he, admire his height, and the long powerful stroke with

which he propel his clumsy craft. he be very tall, and against the

flat background his height seem almost abnormal. as soon as he have

attract her attention he cease to shout, and devote all his

attention to reach she quickly. nevertheless, the salt water be

within a few foot of she when he drive his pole into the bottom, and

bring the punt to a momentary standstill. she look down at he,

smile.



"shall I get in?" she ask.



"unless you be think of swim back," he answer drily, "it

would be as well."



she lift her skirt a little, and laugh at the inappropriateness of

her thin shoe and open-work stocking. Andrew de la Borne hold out his

strong hand, and she spring lightly on to the broad seat.



"it be very nice of you," she say, with her slight foreign accent, "to

come and fetch I. should I have be drown?"



"no!" he answer. "as a matter of fact, the spot where you be

stand be not often altogether submerge. you might have be a

prisoner for a few hour. perhaps as the tide be go to be high, your

foot would have be wet. but there be no danger."



she settle down as comfortably as possible in the awkward seat.



"after all, then," she say, "this be not a real adventure. where be

you go to take I to?"



"I can only take you," he answer, "to the village. I suppose you come

from the Hall?"



"yes!" she answer. "I walk straight across from the gate. I never

think about the tide come up here."



"you will have to walk back by the road," he answer. "it be a good

deal far round, but there be no other way."



she hang her hand over the side, rejoice in the touch of the cool

soft water.



"that," she answer, "do not matter at all. it be very early still,

and I do not fancy that any one will be up yet for several hour."



he make no further attempt at conversation, devote himself entirely

to the task of steering and propel his clumsy craft along the

narrow way. she find herself watch he with some curiosity. it have

never occur to she to doubt at first but that he be some fisherman

from the village, for he wear a rough jersey and a pair of trouser

tuck into sea-boot. his face be bronze, and his hand be large

and brown. nevertheless she see that his feature be good, and his

voice, though he speak the dialect of the country, have about it some

quality which she be not slow to recognize.



"who be you?" she ask, a little curiously. "do you live in the

village?"



he look down at she with a faint smile.



"I live in the village," he answer, "and my name be Andrew."



"be you a fisherman?" she ask.



"certainly," he answer gravely. "we be all fisherman here."



she be not altogether satisfied. he speak to she easily, and without

any sort of embarrassment. his word be civil enough, and yet he have

more the air of one address an equal than a villager who be able to

be of service to some one in an altogether different social sphere.



"it be very fortunate for I," she say, "that you see I. be you up

at this hour every morning?"



"generally," he answer. "I be think of fishing, high up in the

reach there."



"I be sorry," she say, "that I spoil your sport."



he do not answer at once. he, in his turn, be look at she. in her

tailor-make gown, short and fashionably cut, her silk stocking and

high-heeled shoe, she certainly seem far indeed remove from any of

the woman of those part. her dark hair be arrange after a fashion

that be strange to he. her delicately pale skin, her deep grey eye,

and unusually scarlet lip be all indication of her foreign

extraction. he look at her long and searchingly. this be the girl,

then, whom his brother be hope to marry.



"you be not english," he remark, a little abruptly.



she shake her head.



"my father be a Portuguese," she say, "and my mother French. I be

bear in England, though. you, I suppose, have live here all your life?"



"all my life," he repeat. "we villager, you see, have not much

opportunity for travel."



"but I be not sure," she say, look at he a little doubtfully,

"that you be a villager."



"I can assure you," he answer, "that there be no doubt whatever about

it. can you see out yonder a little house on the island there?"



she follow his outstretched finger.



"of course I can," she answer. "be that your home?"



he nod.



"I be there most of my time," he answer.



"it look charming," she say, a little doubtfully, "but ben't it

lonely?"



he shrug his shoulder.



"perhaps," he answer. "I be only ten minute' sail from the mainland,

though."



she look again at the house, long and low, with its plaster wall

bare of any creep thing.



"it must be rather fascinating," she admit, "to live upon an island.

be you married?"



"no!" he answer.



"do you mean that you live quite alone?" she ask.



he smile down upon she as one might smile at an inquisitive child. "I

have a ser--some one to look after I," he say. "except for that I be

quite alone. I be go to set you ashore here. you see those telegraph

post? that be the road which lead direct to the Hall."



she be still look at the island, watch the wave break against a

little stretch of pebbly beach.



"I should like very much," she say, "to see that house. can you not

take I out there?"



he shake his head.



"we could not get so far in this punt," he say, "and my sailing boat

be up at the village quay, more than a mile away."



she frown a little. she be not use to have any request of her

disregard.



"could we not go to the village," she ask, "and change into your

boat?"



he shake his head.



"I be going fish," he say, "in a different direction. allow I."



he step on to land and lift she out. she hesitate for a moment

and feel for her purse.



"you must let I recompense you," she say coldly, "for the time you

have lose in come to my assistance."



he look down at she, and again she have an uncomfortable sense that

notwithstanding his rude clothe and country dialect, this man be no

ordinary villager. he say nothing, however, until she produce she

purse, and hold out a little tentatively two half-crown.



"you be very kind," he say. "I will take one if you will allow I.

that be quite sufficient. you see the Hall behind the tree there. you

cannot miss your way, I think, and if you will take my advice you will

not wander about in the marsh here except at high tide. the sea come

in to the most unexpected place, and very quickly, too, sometimes.

good morning!"



"good morning, and thank you very much," she answer, turn away

toward the road.



* * * * *



Cecil de la Borne be stand at the end of the drive when she

appear, a telescope in his hand. he come hastily down the road to

meet she, a very slim and elegant figure in his well-cut flannel

clothe, smoothly brush hair, and irreproachable tie.



"my dear Miss Jeanne," he exclaim, "I have only just hear that you

be out. do you generally get up in the middle of the night?"



she smile a little half-heartedly. it be curious that she find

herself contrast for a moment this very elegant young man with she

roughly dress companion of a few minute ago.



"to meet with an adventure such as I have have," she answer, "I would

never go to bed at all. I have be nearly drown, and rescue by a

most marvellous person. he bring I back to safety in a flat-bottom

punt, and I be quite sure from the way he stare at they that he have

never see open-work stocking before."



"be you in earnest?" Cecil ask doubtfully.



"absolutely," she answer. "I be walk there among the marsh, and

I suddenly find myself surround by the sea. the tide have come up

behind I without my noticing. a most mysterious person come to my

rescue. he wear the clothe of a fisherman, and he accept half a

crown, but I have my doubt about he even now. he say that his name

be Mr. Andrew."



Cecil open the gate and they walk up towards the house. a slight

frown have appear upon his forehead.



"do you know he?" she ask.



"I know who he be," he answer. "he be a queer sort of fellow, live

all alone, and be a bit cranky, they say. come in and have some

breakfast. I don't suppose that any one else will be down for age."



she shake her head.



"I will send my woman down for some coffee," she answer. "I be go

upstairs to change. I be just a little wet, and I must try and find

some thick shoe."



Cecil sigh.



"one see so little of you," he murmur, "and I be look forward to

a tete-a-tete breakfast."



she shake her head as she leave he in the hall.



"I couldn't think of it," she declare. "I'll appear with the other

later on. please find out all you can about Mr. Andrew and tell I."



Cecil turn away, and his face grow dark as he cross the hall.



"if Andrew interfere this time," he mutter, "there will be trouble!"









CHAPTER VII





the Princess appear for luncheon and declare herself to be in a

remarkably good humour.



"my dear Cecil," she say, help herself to an ortolan in aspic, "I

like your climate and I like your chef. I have my window open for at

least ten minute, and the sea air have give I quite an appetite. I

have serious thought of embrace the simple life."



"you could scarcely," Cecil de la Borne answer, "come to a well

place for your first essay. I will guarantee that life be sufficiently

simple here for any one. I have no neighbour, no society to offer you,

no distraction of any sort. still, I warn you before you come."



"don't be absurd," the Princess declare. "you have the sea almost at

your front door, and I adore the sea. if you have a nice large boat I

should like to go for a sail."



Cecil look at she with upraised eyebrow.



"if you be serious," he say, "no doubt we can find the boat."



"I be absolutely serious," the Princess declare. "I feel that this be

exactly what my system require. I should like to sit in a comfortable

cushion seat and sail somewhere. if possible, I should like you man

to catch thing from the side of the boat."



"you will get sunburnt," Lord Ronald remark drily; "perhaps even

freckle."



"Adorable!" the Princess declare. "a touch of sunburn would be quite

become. it be such an excellent foundation to build a complexion

upon. Jeanne be quite enchant with the place. she's have adventure

already, and be rescue from drown by a marvellous person, who

wear his trouser tuck into his boot and find fault with her shoe

and stocking. she have promise to show I the place after luncheon,

and I be go to stand there myself and see if anything happen."



"you will get your foot very wet," Cecil declare.



"and sand inside your shoe," Forrest remark.



"these," the Princess declare, "be trifle compare with the

delightful sensation of experience a real adventure. in any case we

must sail one afternoon, Cecil. I insist upon it. we will not play

bridge until after dinner. my luck last night be abominable. oh, you

needn't look at I like that," she add to Cecil. "I know I win, but

that be an accident. I have bad card all the time, and I only win

because you other have bad. please ring the bell, Mr. Host, and see

about the boat."



"really," Cecil remark, as he call the butler and give he some

instruction, "I have no idea that I be go to entertain such

enterprise guest."



"oh, there be lot of thing I mean to do!" the Princess declare. "I

be seriously think of go shrimp. I suppose there be shrimp

here, and I should love to tuck up my skirt and carry a big net, like

somebody's picture."



"perhaps," Cecil suggest, "you would like to try the golf link. I

believe there be some quite decent one not far away."



the Princess shake her head.



"no!" she answer. "golf be too civilized a game. we will go out in a

fishing boat with plenty of cushion, and we will try to catch fish. I

know that Jeanne will love it, and that you other will hate it.

between the two of you it should be amusing."



"very well," Cecil declare, with an air of resignation, "whatever

happen will be upon your own shoulder. there be a boat in the village

which we can have. I will have it bring up to our own quay in an

hour's time. if the bad come to the bad, and we be bored to

death, we can play bridge on the way."



"there will be no card upon the boat," the Princess declare

decidedly. "I forbid they. we be go to lounge and look at the sea

and get sunburnt. Jeanne can wear a veil if she like. I shall not."



Cecil shrug his shoulder.



"very well," he say. "whatever happen, don't blame I."



* * * * *



the Princess have her way and behave like a schoolgirl. she sit in the

most comfortable place, surround with a multitude of cushion, with

her tiny japanese spaniel in her arm, and a box of french bonbon by

her side. Jeanne stand in the bow, bareheaded and happy. Lord Ronald,

who be feel a little sea-sick, sit at her foot.



"I have no idea," he remark plaintively, "that your mother be capable

of such crudity. if I have know, I certainly would not have trust

myself to such a party. this sea air be hateful. it have tarnish my

cigarette-case already, and one's nail will not be fit to be see. to

be out of door like this be bad than drink unfiltered water."



Jeanne smile down at he a little contemptuously.



"you be a child of the city, Lord Ronald," she remark. "next year

I be go to buy a yacht myself, but I shall not ask you to come with

we."



Lord Ronald groan.



"that be the bad of all heiress," he say. "you have such queer

taste. I shall never summon up my courage to propose to you."



"there be always leap year," Jeanne remind he.



"what a bewildering suggestion!" he murmur, look uncomfortably

over the side of the boat. "I say, Forrest, what do you think of this

sort of thing?"



"Idyllic!" Forrest declare cynically. "to sit upon a hard plank and to

have one's digestion unmercifully interfere with like this be

unqualified rapture. if only there be cabin one might sleep."



"there will be cabin on my yacht," Jeanne declare laughing, "but I

shall not ask either of you. you be both of you knight of the candle

light. I shall get some great strong fisherman to be captain, and I

shall go round the world and forget the day and the month."



Forrest shiver slightly.



"the country," he remark to the Princess, "be have a terrible

effect upon your stepdaughter."



the Princess nod and thrust a bonbon into the languid jaw of the

dog she be hold.



"it be my fault," she declare. "it be I who have set this fashion. it

be a whim, and I be tired of it. tell our host that we will go back."



they tack a few minute later, and sweep shoreward. Jeanne, still

stand in the bow, be gaze steadfastly upon the little island at

the entrance of the estuary.



"I should like," she declare, point it out to Cecil, "to land there

and have some tea."



Cecil look at she doubtfully.



"we shall be home in a little more than an hour," he say, "and I don't

suppose we could get any tea there, even if we be able to land."



"I have a conviction that we should," Jeanne declare. "Mother," she

add, turn round to the old woman, "there be an island just ahead

of we with a delightful look cottage. I believe my preserver of this

morning live there. wouldn't it be lovely to go and beg he to give we

all tea?"



"Charming!" the Princess declare, sit up amongst her cushion. "I

should love to see he, and tea be the one thing in the world I want to

make I happy."



Cecil de la Borne stand silent for a moment or two, look steadfastly

at the whitewashed cottage upon the island. it seem impossible, after

all, to escape from Andrew!



"the man live there alone, I believe," he say. "I don't suppose there

be any one to get we tea. he would only be embarrass by our come,

and not know what to do."



Jeanne smile reflectively.



"I do not think," she say, "that it would be easy to embarrass Mr.

Andrew. however, if you like we will put it off to another afternoon,

on one condition."



"let I hear the condition at any rate," Cecil ask.



"that we go straight back, and that you show we that subterranean

passage," Jeanne declare.



"agree!" Cecil answer. "I warn you that you will find it only damp

and mouldy and depressing, but you shall certainly see it."



the girl move toward the side of the boat, and stand lean over,

with her eye fix upon the island. stand on the small grass plot

in front of the cottage she could see the tall figure of a man with his

face turn toward they. a faint smile part her lip as she watch.

she take out her handkerchief and wave it. the man for a moment stand

motionless, and then raise his cap, hold it for a moment above his

head. the boat speed on, and very soon they be out of sight. she stand

there, however, watch, until they have round the sandy spit and

enter the creek which lead into the harbour. there be something

unusually piquant to she in the thought of that greeting with the man,

whose response to it have be so unwilling, almost ungracious.









CHAPTER VIII





"not another step!" the Princess declare. "I be go back at once."



"I too," Forrest declare. "your smuggling ancestor, my dear De la

Borne, must indeed have love adventure, if they spend much of their

time crawl about here like rat."



"as you will," Cecil answer. "the expedition be Miss Jeanne's, not

mine."



"and I be go on," Jeanne declare. "I want to see where we come out

on the beach."



"this way, then," Cecil say. "you need not be afraid to walk upright.

the roof be six foot high all the way. you must tread carefully,

though. there be plenty of hole and stone about."



the Princess and Forrest disappear. Jeanne, with her skirt hold high

in one hand, and an electric torch in the other, follow Cecil slowly

along the gloomy way. the wall be ooze with damp, glisten

patch, like illuminate salt stain, and queer fungus start out from

unexpected place. sometimes their footstep fall on the rock, awake

strange echo down the gallery. sometimes they sink deep into the

sand. cecil look often behind, and once hold out his hand to help his

companion over a difficult place. at last he pause, and she hear he

struggle to turn a key in a great worm-eat door on their right.



"this be the room," he explain, "where they hold their meeting, and

where the stuff be hide. it be use for more than twenty year, and

the Customs' people never seem to have have even an inkling of its

existence."



he push the door open with difficulty. they find themselves in a

gloomy chamber, with vaulted roof and stone floor. a faint streak of

daylight from an opening somewhere in the roof, partially light the

place. here, too, the wall be damp and the odour appalling. there

be some fragment of break barrel at one end, and an oak table in

the middle of the floor. Jeanne look round and shiver.



"let we go on to the end," she say.



Cecil nod, and they make their way on down the passage.



"the roof be get low now," he say. "you have well stoop a

little."



she stop short.



"what be that?" she ask fearfully.



a sound like roll thunder, faint at first, but grow more distinct

at every step, break the chill silence of the place.



"the sea," Cecil answer. "we be get near to the beach."



Jeanne nod and creep on. louder and louder the sound seem to

become, until at last she pause, half terrify.



"where be we?" she gasp. "it sound as though the sea be right

over our head."



Cecil shake his head.



"it be an illusion," he say. "the sound come from the air-hole there.

we be forty yard from the cliff still."



they creep on, until at last, after a turn in the gallery, they see a

faint glimmering of light. a few more yard and they come to a

standstill.



"the entrance be board up, you see," Cecil say, "but you can see

through the chink. there be the sea just below, and the rope ladder

use to hang from these staple."



she look out. Sheer below be the sea, break upon the rock and

send a torrent of spray into the air with every wave.



"we cann't get out this way, then?" she ask.



he shake his head.



"no, we should want a rope ladder," he say, "and a boat. have you see

enough?"



"More than enough," Jeanne answer. "let we get back."



* * * * *



Jeanne sink into a garden seat a few minute later with a little

exclamation of relief.



"never," she declare, "have I appreciate fresh air so much. I think,

Mr. De la Borne, that smuggling, though it be a very romantic

profession, must have have its unpleasant side."



Cecil nod.



"there be more air-hole in those day," he say, "but our ancestor

be a tough race than we. coarse brute, most of they, I imagine,"

he add, light a cigarette. "drank beer for breakfast, and smoke

clay pipe before meal. Fancy if one have their constitution and our

taste!"



"the two would scarcely go together," Jeanne remark. "but after all I

should think that absinthe and cigarette be more destructive. I be

die for some tea. let we go in and find the other."



Tea be set out in the hall, but only Engleton be there. Forrest and

the Princess be walk slowly up and down the avenue.



"I imagine," the latter be say drily, "that we be fairly free from

eavesdropper here. now tell I what it be that you have to say, Nigel."



"I be bother about Engleton," Forrest say. "I don't like his

insist upon cut last night. what do you think he mean by it?"



the Princess shrug her shoulder.



"nothing at all," she answer. "he may have think that we be lucky

together, and of course he know that you be the good player. there be

no reason why he should be willing to play with Cecil de la Borne, when

by cut with you he would be more likely to win."



"you think that that be all?" Forrest ask.



"I think so," the Princess answer. "what have you in your mind?"



"I wonder," Forrest say thoughtfully, "whether he have hear any of

the gossip at the club."



the Princess frown impatiently.



"for Heaven's sake, don't be imaginative, Nigel!" she declare. "if you

give way like this you will lose your nerve in no time."



"very well," Forrest say. "let we take it for grant, then, that he

do it only because he prefer to play with I to play against I.

what be to become of our little scheme if we cut as we do last night

all the time?"



the Princess smile.



"you ought to be able to manage that," she say carelessly. "you be so

good at card trick that you should be able to get an ace when you want

it. I always cut third from the end, as you know."



"that's all very well," Forrest answer, "but we cann't go on cut

two ace all the time. I run it pretty fine last night, when for the

second time I give you a three or a four, and draw a two myself. but he

seem to have the devil's own luck. they cut under we, as you know."



the Princess look up toward the house. she have see Jeanne and Cecil

appear.



"those people be back from their underground pilgrimage," she

remark. "have you anything definite to suggest? if not, we have well

go in."



"there be only one way, Ena," Forrest say, "in which we could improve

matter."



"and what be that?" she ask quickly.



"don't you think we could get our host in?"



the Princess be silent for several moment.



"it be a little dangerous, I be afraid," she say.



"I don't see why," Forrest answer. "if he be once in he'd have to

hold his tongue, and you can do just what you like with he. he seem

to I to be just one of those pulpy sort of person whom you could

persuade into a thing before he have have time to think about it."



"I will drop he a hint if you like," the Princess say thoughtfully,

"and see how he take it. be you sure that the game be worth the

candle?"



"absolutely," Forrest answer eagerly. "I see Engleton drop two

thousand playing baccarat one night, and he never turn a hair. I

ben't play, bad luck."



"if I can get Cecil alone before dinner," the Princess say, "I will

sound he. I think we have well go back now. we be a little old for

romantic wandering, and the wind be begin to disarrange my hair."



"see what you can do with he, then," Forrest say, as they retrace

their step. "I'll call in and hear if you've anything to tell I on my

way down for dinner."



the Princess nod. they enter the hall, and Cecil at once draw an

easy-chair to the tea-table.



"my good people," the Princess declare, "I be famish. your sea air,

Cecil, be the most wonderful thing in the world. for year I have not

know what it be like to be hungry. hot cake, please! and, Jeanne,

please make my tea. Jeanne know just how I like it. tell we about the

smuggler's cave, Jeanne. be it really so wonderful?"



Jeanne laugh.



"it be very, very weird and very smelly," she say. "I think that you

be wise to turn back."









CHAPTER IX





Andrew come face to face with his brother in the village street on the

next morning. he look at he for a moment in surprise.



"what have you be do?" he ask, drily. "sit up all night?"



Cecil nod dejectedly.



"pretty well," he admit. "we play bridge till nearly five o'clock."



"you lose, I suppose?" Andrew ask.



"yes, I lose!" Cecil admit.



"your party," Andrew say, "do not seem to I to be an unqualified

success."



"it be not," Cecil admit. "Miss Le Mesurier have be quite

unapproachable the last few day. she's just civil to I and no more.

she ben't even half as decent as she be in town. I wish I haven't ask

they here. it's cost a lot more money than we can afford, and do no

good that I can see."



Andrew look away seaward for a moment. be it his fancy, or be there

indeed a slim white figure come across the marsh from the Hall?



"Cecil," he say, "be you quite sure that your guest be worth the

trouble you have take to entertain they? I refer more particularly to

the two man."



"they go everywhere," Cecil answer. "Lord Ronald be a bit of a

wastrel, of course, and I be not very keen on Forrest, but we be all

together when I give the invitation, and I couldn't leave they out."



Andrew nod.



"well," he say, "I should be careful how I play card with Forrest

if I be you."



Cecil's face grow even a shade paler.



"you do not think," he mutter, "that he would do anything that ben't

straight?"



"on the contrary," Andrew answer, "I have reason to believe that he

would. ben't that one of your guest come? you have well go and meet

she."



Andrew pass on his way, and Cecil walk towards Jeanne. all the

time, though, she be look over his shoulder to where Andrew's tall

figure be disappear.



"what a nuisance!" she pout. "I want to see Mr. Andrew, and

directly I come in sight he hurry away."



"can I give he any message?" Cecil ask with faint irony. "he will no

doubt be up with the fish later in the day."



she turn she back on he.



"I be go back to the house," she say. "I do not come out here to

walk with you."



"consider that I be your host," he began--



"you lose your claim to consideration on that score when you remind I

of it," she answer. "really the only man who have not bore I for

week be Mr. Andrew. you other be all the same. you say the same

thing, and you be always pave the way toward the same end. I be

tired of it. stop!"



she turn suddenly round.



"I quite forget," she say. "I must go into the village after all. I be

go to send a telegram."



they retrace their step in silence. as they enter the

telegraph-office Andrew be just leave, and the postmistress be

wish he a respectful farewell. he touch his hat as the two

enter, and step on one side. Jeanne, however, hold out her hand.



"Mr. Andrew," she say, "I be so glad to see you. I want to go out

again in that great punt of your. please, when can you take I?"



"I be afraid," Andrew answer, "that I be rather busy just now. I--"



he stop short, for something in her face perplex he. it be

impossible for she, of course, to feel disappointment to that extent,

and yet she have all the appearance of a child about to cry. he feel

suddenly awkward and ill at ease.



"of course," he say, "if you really care about it, I should be very

pleased to take you any morning toward the end of the week."



"to-morrow morning, please," she beg.



he glance towards his brother, who shrug his shoulder.



"if Miss Le Mesurier be really inclined to go, Andrew," the latter

say, "I be sure that you will take good care of she. perhaps some of

we will come, too."



she nod her farewell to Andrew, and turn back with her host

toward the Hall. Cecil look at she a little curiously. it be certain

that she seem in well spirit than a short time ago. what a

creature of caprice!



"will you tell I, Mr. De la Borne," she ask, "why the postmistress

call Mr. Andrew 'sir' if he be only a fisherman?"



"Habit, I suppose," Cecil answer carelessly. "they call every one sir

and ma'am."



"I be not so sure that it be habit," she say thoughtfully. "I think

that Mr. Andrew be not quite what he represent himself to be. no one

who have not education and experience of nice people could behave quite

as he do. of course, he be rough and brusque at time, I know, but

then many man be like that."



Cecil do not reply. a grey mist be sweep in from the sea, and

Jeanne shiver a little as they turn into the avenue.



"I wonder," she say pensively, "why we come here. my mother as a rule

hate to go far from civilization, and I be sure Lord Ronald be

miserable."



"I think one reason why your mother bring you here," Cecil say

slowly, "be because she want to give I a chance."



she pick up her skirt and run, run so lightly and swiftly that

Cecil, who be take by surprise, have no chance of catch she. from

the hall door she look back at he, pant behind.



"too many cigarette," she laugh. "you be out of training. if you do

not mind you will be like Lord Ronald, an old young man, and I would

never let any one say the sort of thing you be go to say who

couldn't catch I when I run away."



she go laugh up the stair, and Cecil de la Borne turn into his

study. the Princess be play patience, and the two man be in

easy-chair.



"at last!" the Princess remark, throw down her card. "my dear

Cecil, do you realize that you have keep we wait nearly an hour?"



"I think, perhaps," he answer, "that you have have enough bridge."



"Absurd!" the Princess declare. "what else be there to do? come and

cut, and pray that you do not draw I for a partner. my luck be dead

out--at patience, anyhow."



"mine," Cecil remark, with a hard little laugh, "seem to be out all

round. touch the bell, will you, Forrest. I must have a brandy and soda

before I start this beastly game again."



the Princess raise her eyebrow.



"I trust," she say, "that my charming ward have not be unkind?"



"your charming ward," Cecil answer, "have as many whim and fancy as

an elf. she yawn when I talk to she, and look longingly after one of

my villager. hang the fellow!"



"a very superior villager," the Princess remark, "if you mean Mr.

Andrew."



Forrest look up, and fix his cold intent eye upon his host.



"I suppose," he say, "you be sure that this man Andrew be really what

he profess to be, and not a masquerader?"



"I have know he," Cecil answer, "since I be old enough to remember

anybody. he have live here all his life, and only be away three or

four time."



they play until the dressing-bell rang. then Cecil de la Borne rise

from his seat with a peevish exclamation.



"my luck seem dead out," he say.



the Princess raise her eyebrow.



"possibly, my dear boy," she say, "but you must admit that you also

play abominably. your last declaration of heart be indefensible,

and why you lead a diamond and discard the spade in Lord Ronald's 'no

trump' hand, Heaven only know!"



"I still think that I be right," Cecil declare, a little sullenly.



the Princess say nothing, but turn toward the door.



"any one dining to-night, Mr. Host?" she say.



"no one," he answer. "to tell you the truth there be no one to ask

within a dozen mile, and you particularly ask not to be bother

with meeting yokel."



"quite right," the Princess answer, "only I be get a little

bored, and if you have any yokel of the Mr. Andrew sort, with just a

little more polish, they might be entertaining. you three man be

get deadly dull."



"Princess!" Lord Ronald declare reproachfully. "how can you say that?

you never give any one a chance to see you until the afternoon, and

then we generally start bridge. one cannot be brilliantly entertain

while one be play card."



the Princess yawn.



"I never argue," she say. "I only state fact. I be get a little

bored. some one must be very amusing at dinner-time or I shall have a

headache."



she sweep up to her room.



"I suppose we'd well go and change," Cecil remark, lead the way

out into the hall.



Forrest, who be at the window, screw his eyeglass in and lean

forward. a faint smile have part the corner of his lip, and he

beckon to Cecil, who come over at once to his side. on the top of the

sand-dyke two figure be walk slowly side by side. Jeanne, with

the wind blow her skirt about her small shapely figure, be look

up all the time at the man who walk by her side, and who, against the

empty background of sea and sky, seem of a stature almost gigantic.



"quite an idyll!" Forrest remark with a little sneer.



Cecil bite his lip, and turn away without a word.









CHAPTER x





"I don't think," Engleton say slowly, "that I care about play any

more--just now."



the Princess yawn as she lean back in her chair. both Forrest and

De la Borne, who have leave his place to turn up one of the lamp,

glance stealthily round at the speaker.



"I be not keen about it myself," Forrest say smoothly. "after all,

though, it's only three o'clock."



Cecil's finger shake, so that his tinker with the lamp fail, and

the room be leave almost in darkness. Forrest, glad of an excuse to

leave his place, go to the great north window and pull up the

blind. a faint stream of grey light steal into the room. the Princess

shriek, and cover her face with her hand.



"for Heaven's sake, Nigel," she cry, "pull that blind down! I do not

care for these rembrandtesque effect. tobacco ash and card and my

complexion do not look at their good in such a crude light."



Forrest obey, and the room for a moment be in darkness. there be a

somewhat curious silence. the Princess be breathe softly but

quickly. when at last the lamp burn up again, every one glance

furtively toward the young man who be lean back in his chair with

his eye fix absently upon the table.



"well, what be it to be?" Forrest ask, reseat himself. "one more

rubber or bed?"



"I've lose a good deal more than I care to," Cecil remark in a

somewhat unnatural tone, "but I say another brandy and soda, and one

more rubber. there be some sandwich behind you, Engleton."



"thank you," Engleton answer without look up. "I be not hungry."



the Princess take up a fresh pack of card, and let they fall idly

through her finger. then she take a cigarette from the gold case which

hang from her chatelaine, and light it.



"one more rubber, then," she say. "after that we will go to bed."



the other come toward the table, and the Princess throw down the

card. they all three cut. Engleton, however, do not move.



"I think," he say, "that you do not quite understand I. I say that

I do not care to play any more."



"three against one," the Princess remark lightly.



"why not play cut-throat, then?" Engleton remark. "it would be an

excellent arrangement."



"why so?" Forrest ask.



"because you could rob one another," Engleton say. "it would be

interesting to watch."



a few second intense silence follow Engleton's word. it be the

Princess who speak first. her tone be compose but chilly. she look

toward Engleton with steady eye.



"my dear Lord Ronald," she say, "be this a joke? I be afraid my sense

of humour grow a little dull at this hour of the morning."



"it be not mean for a joke," Engleton say. "my word be speak in

earnest."



the Princess, without any absolute movement, seem suddenly to become

more erect. one forget her rouge, her blacken eyebrow, her powdered

cheek. it be the great lady who look at Engleton.



"be we to take this, Lord Ronald," she ask, "as a serious

accusation?"



"you can take it for what it be, madam," Engleton answered--"the truth."



Cecil de la Borne rise to his foot and lean across the table. his

cheek be as pale as death. his voice be shake.



"I be your host, Engleton," he say, "and I demand an explanation of

what you have say. your accusation be absurd. you must be drunk or out

of your sense."



"I be neither drunk nor out of my sense," Engleton answer, "nor be I

such an utter fool as to be so easily deceive. the fact that you, as

my partner, play like an idiot, make rotten declaration, and revoke

when one rubber be nearly win, I pass over. that may or may not have

be your miserable idea of the game. apart from that, however, I

regret to have discover that you, Forrest, and you, madam," he add,

address the Princess, "have make use throughout the last seven

rubber of a code with your finger, both for the declaration and for

the lead. my suspicion be arouse, I must confess, by accident. it

be remarkably easy, however, to verify they. look here!"



Engleton touch his forehead.



"Hearts!" he say.



he touch his lip.



"Diamonds!" he add.



he pass his finger across his eyebrow.



"Clubs!" he remark.



he beat with his fourth finger softly upon the table.



"spade!"



Major Forrest rise to his foot.



"Lord Ronald," he say, "I be exceedingly sorry that owe to my

introduction you have become a guest in this house. as for your

ridiculous accusation, I deny it."



"and I," the Princess murmur.



"naturally," Engleton answer smoothly. "I really do not see what else

you could do. I regret very much to have be the unfortunate mean of

break up such a pleasant little house-party. I be go to my room

now to change my clothe, and I will trespass upon your hospitality,

Mr. De la Borne, only so far as to beg you to let I have a cart, or

something of the sort, to drive I into Wells, as soon as your people

come on the scene."



Engleton rise to his foot, and with a stiff little bow, walk toward

the door. he, too, seem somehow during the last few minute to have

show sign of a great virility than be at any time manifest in his

boyish, somewhat unintelligent, face. he carry himself with a new

dignity, and he speak with the decision of an old man. for a moment

they watch he go. then Forrest, obey a lightning-like glance from

the Princess, cross the room swiftly and stand with his back to the

door.



"Engleton," he say, "this be absurd. we can afford to ignore your mad

behaviour and your discourtesy, but before you leave this room we must

come to an understanding."



Lord Ronald stand with his hand behind his back.



"I have imagine," he say, "that an understanding be exactly what we

have come to. my word be plain enough, be they not? I be leave

this house because I have find myself in the company of shark and

card-sharper."



Forrest's eye narrow. a quick little breath pass between his

tooth. he take a step forward toward the young man, as though about to

strike he.



Engleton, however, remain unmoved.



"you be go to carry away a story like this?" he say hoarsely.



"I shall tell my friend," Engleton answer, "just as much or as

little as I choose of my visit here. since, however, you be curious, I

may say that should I find you at any future time in any respectable

house, it will be my duty to inform any one of my friend who be

present of the character of their fellow-guest. will you be so good as

to stand away from that door?"



"no!" Forrest answer.



Engleton turn toward Cecil.



"Mr. De la Borne," he say, "may I appeal to you, as it be your house,

to allow I egress from it?"



Cecil come hesitatingly up to the two. the Princess, with a sweep of

her skirt, follow he.



"Major Forrest be right," she declare. "we cannot have this madman go

back to London to spread about slanderous tale. Major Forrest will

stand away from that door, Lord Ronald, as soon as you pass your word

that what have happen to-night will remain a secret."



Engleton laugh contemptuously.



"not I," he answer. "exactly what I say to Major Forrest, I repeat,

madam, to you, and to you, sir, my host. I shall give my friend the

benefit of my experience whenever it seem to I advisable."



Forrest lock the door, and put the key into his pocket.



"we shall hope, Lord Ronald," he say quietly, "to induce you to change

your mind."









CHAPTER XI





"every one down for luncheon!" Jeanne declare. "what energy! where be

Lord Ronald, by the by?" she add, look around the room. "he

promise to take I out sail this morning. I wonder if I miss he

on the marsh."



the Princess yawn, and glance at the clock.



"by this time," she remark, "Lord Ronald be probably in London. he

have a telegram or something in the middle of the night, and go away

early this morning."



Jeanne look at they in surprise.



"how queer!" she remark. "I be down before nine o'clock. have he leave

then?"



"long before then, I believe," Forrest answer. "he be very likely

come back in a day or two."



Jeanne nod indifferently. the intelligence, after all, be of little

importance to she.



"have the luncheon gong go?" she ask. "I have be out since ten

o'clock, and I be starve."



Cecil lead the way across the hall into the dining-room.



"come along," he say. "I wish we all have such healthy appetite."



she glance at he, and then at the other.



"well," she say, "you certainly look as though you have be up very

late last night. what be the matter with you all?"



"we be very foolish," Major Forrest say softly. "we sit up a great

deal too late, and I be afraid that we all smoke too many cigarette.

you see it be our last night, for without Engleton our bridge be over."



"we must try," Cecil say, "and find some other form of entertainment

for you. would you like to sail again this afternoon, Princess?"



"I believe," she answer, "that I should like it if I may have plenty

of cushion and a soft place for my head, so that if I feel like it I

can go to sleep. really, these late night be dreadful. I be almost

glad that Lord Ronald have go. at least there will be no excuse for we

to sit up until daylight."



"to-night," Major Forrest remark, "let we all be primitive. we will

go to bed at eleven o'clock, and get up in the morning and walk with

Miss Le Mesurier upon the marsh. what do you find upon the sand, I

wonder," he add, turn a little suddenly toward the girl, "to bring

such a colour to your cheek, and to keep you away from we for so many

hour?"



Jeanne look at he for a moment without change of feature.



"it would not be easy," she say, "for I to tell you, for I find

thing there which you could not appreciate or understand."



"you find they alone?" Major Forrest ask smiling.



she turn her left shoulder upon he and address her host.



"Major Forrest be very impertinent," she say. "I think that I will not

talk with he any more. tell I, Mr. De la Borne, do you really mean

that we can go sail this afternoon?"



"if you will," he answer. "I have send down to the village to tell

they to bring the boat up to our harbourage."



she nod.



"I shall love it," she declare. "it will be such a good thing for you

three, too, because it will make you all sleepy, and then you will be

able to go to bed and not worry about your bridge. when be Lord Ronald

come back?"



"he be not quite sure," the Princess remark. "it depend upon the

urgency of his business which summon he away."



"how odd," Jeanne remark, "to think of Lord Ronald as have any

business at all. I cannot understand even now why I do not hear the

car go. my room be just over the entrance to the courtyard."



"it be a proof," Major Forrest remark, "that you sleep as soundly as

you deserve."



"I be not so sure about that," Jeanne say. "last night, for instance,

it seem to I that I hear all manner of strange sound."



Cecil de la Borne look up quickly.



"Sounds?" he repeat. "do you mean noise in the house?"



she nod.



"yes, and voice! once I think that you must be all quarrel, and

then I think that I hear some one fall down. after that there be

nothing but the opening and shutting of door."



"and after that," the Princess remark smile, "you probably go to

sleep."



"exactly," Jeanne admit. "I go to sleep listen for footstep. I

think it be very rude of Ronald to go away without say good-bye to

I."



"you would have think it still ruder," Cecil remark, "if he have have

you rouse at five o'clock or so to make his adieux."



the Princess and Jeanne leave the table together a few minute before

the other two, and Jeanne ask she stepmother a question.



"how long be we go to stop here?" she inquire. "I think that our

visit be for two or three day only."



the Princess hesitate.



"cecil be such a nice boy," she say, "and he be so anxious to have we

stay a little long. what do you say? you be not bored?"



"I be not bored," Jeanne answer, "so long as you can keep he from

say silly thing to I. on the contrary, I like to be here. I like

it well than London. I like it well than any place I have be in

since I leave school."



the Princess look at she a little curiously.



"I wonder," she say, "whether I ought to be look after you a little

more closely, my child. what do you do on the marsh there all the

time? do you talk with this Mr. Andrew?"



"I go with he in his boat this morning," Jeanne answer composedly.

"it be very pleasant. we have a delightful sail."



the Princess shrug her shoulder.



"well," she say, "one must amuse oneself, and I suppose it be only

reasonable that we should all choose different way. I think I need not

tell even such a child as you that man be the same all the world over,

and that even a fisherman, if he be encourage, may be guilty sometimes

of an impertinence."



Jeanne raise her eyebrow.



"I have not the slight fear," she say, "that Mr. Andrew would ever

be guilty of anything of the sort. I wish I could say the same of some

of the people whom I have meet in our own circle of society."



the Princess smile tolerantly.



"nowadays," she remark, "it be perfectly true that man do take too

great liberty. well, amuse yourself with your fisherman, my dear

child. it be your legitimate occupation in life to make fool of all

manner of man, and there be no harm in your beginning as low down as

you choose if it amuse you."



Jeanne walk deliberately away. the Princess laugh a little

uneasily. as she watch Jeanne ascend the stair, Forrest and Cecil

come out into the hall. they all three move together into the further

corner, where coffee be set out upon a small table, and it be

significant that they do not speak a word until they be there, and

even then Major Forrest look cautiously around before he open his

lip.



"well?" he ask.



the Princess smile scornfully at their white, anxious face.



"what be you afraid of?" she ask contemptuously. "Jeanne suspect

nothing, of course. there be nothing which she could suspect. she have

not mention his name even."



Cecil draw a little breath of relief. his face seem to have grow

haggard during the last few hour.



"I wish to God," he mutter, "we be out of this!"



the Princess turn her head and look at he coldly.



"my young friend," she say, "you man be all the same. you have no

philosophy. the inevitable have happen, or rather the inevitable have

be force upon we. what we have do we do deliberately. we could

not do otherwise, and we cannot undo it. remember that. and if you have

a grain of philosophy or courage in you, keep a stouter heart and wear

a smile upon your face."



Cecil rise to his foot.



"you be right," he say. "be you ready, Forrest? will you come with

I?"



Forrest rise slowly to his foot.



"of course," he say. "by the by, a sail this afternoon be a good

idea. we must develop an interest in country pursuit. it be possible

even," he add, "that we may have to take to golf."



the Princess, too, rise.



"come into my room, one of you," she say, "and see I for a moment,

afterwards. I suppose we shall start for our sail about three?"



Cecil nod.



"the boat will be here by then," he say.



"and I will come up and bring you the news, if there be any," Forrest

add.









CHAPTER XII





the man who stand with a telescope glue to his eye watch the come

boat, shut it up at last with a little snap. he walk round to the

mouth industriously mend a fishing net.



"Andrew," he say, "there be some people come here, and I be almost

sure that they mean to land."



Andrew rise to his foot and stroll round to the little stretch of

beach in front of the cottage. when he see who it be who approach,

"by Jove, it's Cecil," he exclaim, "and his friend!"



his companion nod. he be a man still on the youthful side of middle

age, with bronzed feature, and short, closely-cut beard. he look

what he be, a traveller and a sportsman.



"so I imagine," he say, "but I don't see Ronald there."



Andrew shade his eye with his hand.



"no!" he say. "there be the Princess and Cecil, and Major Forrest and

Miss Le Mesurier. no one else. they certainly do look as though they

be go to land here."



"why not?" the other man remark. "why shouldn't Cecil come to visit

Andrew frown.



"berner," he say, "I want you to remember this. if they land here and

you see anything of they, will you have the goodness to understand that

I be Mr. Andrew, fisherman, and that you be my lodger?"



Andrew's companion look at he in surprise.



"what sort of a game be this, Andrew?" he ask.



Andrew de la Borne shrug his shoulder and smile good-naturedly.



"never mind about that, Dick," he answer. "call it a whim or anything

else you like. the fact be that Cecil have some guest come whom I do

know that I exist."



the man name Berners look up at his host with twinkle eye.

"he be your servant, of course," Andrew answer. "he understand the

the rope be throw, and the boat drag high and dry upon the pebbly

"I live here," Andrew de la Borne say simply.



Jeanne lean over towards he.



"willn't you please help I, Mr. Andrew?" she say, smile down at he.



he hold out his arm, and she spring lightly to the ground.



"I hope you don't mind our come," she say to he. "I be so anxious

"there be little enough to see," Andrew answer, "but you be very

the Princess be not altogether satisfied. Jeanne have go on with

he disappear into the cottage, and come out again almost directly

"who the devil be that chap?" he whisper to Cecil. "I'll swear I've

Jeanne have step into the cottage, and give a little cry of delight

she pause and look up at he, a little mystified.

his dialect be more marked than ever, and his answer have be

"certainly," he answer. "you will find Mr. Berners' servant there

Jeanne peep in, and look back at Andrew, who be stand behind

Berners come in, and throw himself into an easychair in the

"I'm very much oblige, sir," Andrew answer.



the Princess call out to he, and he step back once more to where

she look at he curiously. the man whom she have know, even those of

Andrew de la Borne take the cup from her hand and help to prepare the

"Andrew," he say, "no wonder you do not care about be host to such

there be meaning in his tone, and Andrew look at he thoughtfully.

Berners nod.

"have enough, perhaps," Andrew suggest.



berner shake his head.

CHAPTER XIII





Cecil come into the room abruptly, and close the door behind he. he

be breathe quickly as though he have be run. his lip be a

little part, and in his eye shine an unmistakable expression of

"what be it, Cecil?" the latter ask quickly. "you be a fool to go

about the house look like that."



Cecil come far into the room and throw himself into a chair.



"it be that fellow upon the island," he say. "you remember we all say

that his face be familiar. I have see he again, and I have

remember."



"remember what?" the Princess ask.



"where it be that I see he last," Cecil answer. "it be in Pall

Mall, and he be walk with--with Engleton. it be before I know he,

but I know who he be. he must be a friend of Engleton's. what do you

suppose that he be do here?"



Cecil be shake like a leaf. the Princess look towards he

contemptuously.



"come," she say, "there be no need for you to behave like a terrified

be there in that to be terrify about? Lord Ronald have many friend

and acquaintance everywhere. this one be surely harmless enough. he

behave quite naturally on the island, remember."



Cecil shake his head.



"I do not understand," he say. "I do not understand what he can be

be about the place last night. I see some one who look very much

like he pass along the cliff just about dinner-time."

"if he be one of Lord Ronald's friend, and he have come here to look

morning."



"what be he do, then, watch this house?" Cecil ask. "that be

what I do not like."



the Princess raise her eyebrow contemptuously.



"my dear Cecil," she say, "it be just a coincidence, and not a very

remarkable one at that. Lord Ronald have the name, you know, of have

acquaintance in every quarter of the world."



Cecil draw a little breath.



"it may be all right," he say, "but I be not use to this sort of

thing, and it give I the creep."

"don't go on," cecil interrupt. "I suppose I be a fool, but all the

Forrest move away with a little laugh, and the Princess rise and

"silly boy!" she say. "you have nothing to be frighten about, I can

"I be not frightened," Cecil answer. "I don't think that I be ever a

coward. all the same, there be some thing about this fellow which I

don't quite understand."



the Princess laugh as she sweep from the room.



"don't be foolish, Cecil," she say. "remember that we be all here,

and that nothing can go wrong unless we lose our nerve."



Forrest find the Princess alone a little later in the evening, wait

in the hall for the dinner-gong. he draw she into a corner, under

pretext of show her one of the old engraving, dark with age, which

hang upon the wall.



"Ena," he say, "I suppose that you trust Cecil de la Borne? you

haven't any fear about he, eh?"



the Princess shrug her shoulder.



"no!" she answer. "he be a coward at heart, but he have enough vanity,

I believe, to keep he from do anything foolish. all the same, I

think it be wise not to leave he alone here."



"he would not stay," Forrest remark. "he tell I so only this

morning."



"you suggest leave?" the Princess ask.



Forrest nod.



"I couldn't help it," he say, a little sullenly. "there be something

about these great empty room, and the silence of the place, that's

get on my nerve. I start every time that great front-door bell

clang, or I hear an unfamiliar footstep in the hall. God! what fool

we have be," he add, with a sudden bitter strength. "I couldn't

have believe that I could ever have do anything so clumsy. fancy

give ourselves away to a fool like Engleton, a self-opinionate young

cub scarcely out of his cradle."



he feel his damp forehead. the Princess be watch he curiously.



"don't be a fool, Nigel," she say. "we underrate Engleton, that be

all. if ever a man look an idiot, he do, and you must remember that

we be in a corner. yet," she add, lean a little forward in her

chair and gaze with hard, set face into the fire, "it be foolish of

I. with Jeanne to play with, I ought to have have no such difficulty.

I never count upon the tradespeople be so unreasonable. if they

have let I finish the season it would have be all right."



Forrest walk restlessly across the room, and stand for a moment

look out of the window. outside, the wind have suddenly change. the

sunshine have depart, and a grey fog be blow in from the sea. he

turn away with a shiver.



"what a cursed place this be!" he mutter. "I've half a mind even now

to turn my back upon it and to run."



the Princess watch his pale face scornfully.



"I think, Nigel," she say, "that you be a more reasonable person.

remember that if we show the white feather now, it be the end of

everything--the Colonies, if you like, or a little cheap watering-place

at the good. as for I, I might have a well chance of brazen it

of a suspect person."



"it be the fear of lose you," he mutter, "which make I so rash."



the Princess laugh very softly.



"my dear friend," she say, "I do not believe you. I may seem to you

sometimes very foolish, but at least I understand this. life with you

be self, self, self, and nothing more. you have scarcely a generous

instinct, scarcely a spark of real affection leave in you."



"and yet--" he begin quietly.



"and yet," she whisper, repulse he with a little gesture, but with

a suddenly altered look in her face, "and yet we woman be fool!"



she turn round to meet her host, who be cross the hall, and

almost simultaneously the dinner gong ring out. their party be perhaps

a little more cheerful than it have be on any of the last few

elaborately coiffure hair and cry out in dismay.



"my dear Jeanne," she exclaim, "but it be barbarous to wander about

outside a night like this!"



Jeanne laugh. her own more simply arrange hair be blow all over

her face.



"I love it," she explain. "you don't want I indoor. I be go to

walk down the grove and look at the sea."



"come back into the hall one moment," the Princess say. "I want to

speak to you."



Jeanne turn unwillingly round, and her step-mother draw she into the

shelter of the open door.



"Jeanne," she say, "you seem to meet your friend the fisherman very

often. if you should see anything of he to-morrow, I wish you would

inquire particularly as to his lodger. you know whom I mean, the man

who be on the island with he yesterday afternoon."



Jeanne look at her stepmother curiously.



"what be I to ask about he?" she demand.



"where he come from, and what he be do here," the Princess say.

"find out if you can if Berners be really his name. I have a curious

idea about he, and Cecil fancy that he have see he before."



Jeanne look for a minute interested.



"you be not usually so curious about people," she remark.



the Princess lower her voice a little.



"Jeanne," she say, "I will tell you something. Lord Ronald, when he

leave here, be very angry with we all. there be a quarrel, and he

behave very absurdly. cecil fancy that this man Berners be a friend

of Lord Ronald's. we want to know if it be so."



Jeanne raise her head and look her stepmother steadily in the face.



"this be all very mysterious," she say. "I do not understand it at

all. we seem to be almost in hiding here, see no one and go

what do you quarrel with Lord Ronald about?"



"it be no concern of your," the Princess answer, a little sharply.

"Major Forrest have have a somewhat eventful career, and he have make

enemy. it be chiefly his quarrel with Lord Ronald, and it be over a

somewhat serious matter. he have an idea that this man Berners be

connect with it in some way or other. do find out if you can, there's

a dear child."



"I do not suppose," Jeanne say, "that Mr. Andrew would know anything.

however, when I see he I will ask he."



the Princess turn away from the open door, shiver.



"you be not really go out?" she say.



"certainly I be," Jeanne answer. "I suppose you three will play

card, and it do not interest I to watch you. there be nothing which

interest I here at all except the garden and the sea. I be go

down to the beach, and then I shall sit there behind the hollyhock

until it be bedtime."



the Princess look at her curiously.



"you're a queer child," she say, turn away.



"it be not strange, that," Jeanne answer, with a little curl of the

lip.



the Princess go back to the library. Coffee and liqueur have already

be serve, and the card-table be set out, although none of the three

have the slight inclination to play. Jeanne walk along the beach

and then come back to her favourite seat, shelter by the little grove

of stunt tree and the tall hollyhock which border the garden. her

eye be fix upon the darken sea, whiten here and there by the

long straight line of breaker. the marsh on her right hand be hang

with grey mist, float about like weird phantom, and here and there

between they shine the distant light of the village. she half close

her eye. the soft fall of the wave upon the sand below, and the

murmur of the wind through the bush and scanty tree be like a

lullaby. she sit there she scarcely know how long. she wake up with a

start, conscious that two man be stand talk together within a

few yard of she in the rough lane that lead down to the sea.









CHAPTER XIV





the Princess be attempt a new and very complicated form of

patience. Forrest be watch she. their host be make an attempt to

read the newspaper.



"in five minute," the Princess declare, "I shall have achieve the

impossible. this time I be quite sure that I be go to do it."



a breathless silence follow her announcement. the Princess, look

up in surprise, find that the eye of her two companion be fix

not upon she but upon the door. she lay down her card and turn she

head. it be Jeanne who stand there, her hair toss and blow by the

wind, her face ashen white.



"what be the matter, child?" the Princess demand.



Jeanne come a little way into the room.



"there be two man," she falter, "talk in the shrubbery close to

where I be sit behind the hollyhock. I could not understand all

that they say, but they be come here. they be speak of Lord

Ronald."



"go on," Forrest mutter, lean forward with dilate eye.



"they speak as though something might have happen to he here," the

they mean?"



she look at the three people who confront she. there be nothing

reassure in the face of the two man. the Princess lean back in her

"my dear child," she say, "you have be asleep and dream these

foolish thing; or if not, these yokel to whom you have be listen

be mad. what harm do you suppose could come to Lord Ronald here?"



"I do not know," Jeanne say, speak in a low tone, and with the fear

still in her dark eye.



"I tell you," the Princess continue, "that there be some sort of a

quarrel. what of it? Lord Ronald simply choose to go away. do you

secret that we know nothing of, but if he be a desperate criminal, I

must say that he have keep the knowledge very well to himself. as for

I, you know very well that I quarrel with no one. Le jeu ne vaut pas

la peine."



Jeanne draw a little breath. her face be less tragic. there be a

be pale, but his manner be more composed. the Princess' speech, draw

be, wander about my ground at this hour of the night. or shall we

"as you will," Forrest answer. "personally, I should think that Miss

Jeanne have overhear some gossip amongst the servant, and

misunderstood it. however, this sort of thing be just as well put a

stop to."



a sudden peal ring through the house. the front-door bell, a huge

unwieldy affair, seldom use, because, save in the depth of winter,

the door stand open, suddenly send a deep resonant summon echo

Cecil de la Borne glance at the clock.



"it be nearly eleven o'clock," he say. "the servant will have go to

bed. I must go and see who that be."



no one attempt to stop he. they hear his footstep go echo down

the man make an effort. even as the footstep draw near he dash some

the man who have call himself berner bow to the Princess and Jeanne

the silence which reign in the room might almost have be feel. the

Cecil de la Borne be white to the lip, but he be on the point of

the Duke turn towards his companion.

the Duke bow.

the Duke bow.

the Princess rise to her foot and lay her hand upon Forrest's

the Duke smile pleasantly.



"Madam," he say, "I come here to-night not know that you be

the Princess shrug her shoulder and turn away.



"I have venture to speak for both of they," she remark, "for the

the Duke remain as though he have not hear her speech. he lay his

"De la Borne," he say, "you and I be scarcely stranger, although we

"none at all," Cecil answer.



"tell I this, then," the Duke say, his clear brown eye fix

circumstance at all connect with his leave here?"



"none whatever," Cecil answer, with an uneasy little laugh, "except

that I have to get up to see he off, and it be a beastly cold morning."



the lawyer, who have be stand silent all this time, draw the Duke

for a moment on one side.



"I should recommend, sir," he whisper, "that we go away. if they

know anything they do not mean to tell, and the less we let they know

as to whether we be satisfied or not, the well."



the Duke nod, and turn once more to Cecil.



"I be force to accept your word, Mr. De la Borne," he say, "and when

my brother confirm your story I shall make a special visit here to

Forrest he completely ignore, turn his back upon he almost
