"the brandy!" he mutter. "quick!"









CHAPTER XV





Bareheaded, Jeanne walk upon the yellow sand close to the softly

break wave. Inland stretch the marsh, with their patch of

vivid green, their cloud of faintly blue wild lavender, their sinuous

creek steal into the bosom of the land. she climb on to a grassy

knoll, warm with the sun's heat, and throw herself down upon the turf.

she turn she back upon the Hall and look steadily seaward, across

the waste of sand and pasture-land to where sky and sea meet. here at

least be peace. she draw a long breath of relief, cast aside the book

which she have never dream of reading, and lie full length in the

grass, with her eye upturned to where a lark be singe his way down

from the blue sky.



Andrew come before long, speed his way out of the village harbour in

his little catboat. she watch he cross the sandy bar of the inlet,

and run his boat presently upon the beach below where she sit. then she

shake out her skirt and make room for he by her side.



"really, Mr. Andrew," she say, rest her chin upon her hand, and

look up at he with her full dark eye, "you be become almost

gallant. until now, when I have be weary, and have wish to talk to

you, I have have almost to come and fetch you. to-day it be you who come

to I. that be a good sign."



"it be true," he admit. "I have keep my telescope fix upon the

sand here for more than an hour. I want to see you."



"you have something to tell I about last night?" she ask gravely.



"no!" he answer, "I do not come here to talk about that."



"do you know," she ask, "who your lodger really be?"



"yes," he say, "I guess! I will be frank with you, Miss Jeanne, if

you will allow I. I do not like your stepmother and I do not like

Major Forrest, but I think that the Duke be go altogether too far

when he suspect they of have anything to do with the disappearance

of his brother."



she draw a little sigh of relief.



"oh! I be glad to hear you say that," she declare. "it be all so

horrible. I could not sleep last night for think about it."



"Lord Ronald will probably turn up in a day or two," Andrew say

gravely. "we will not talk any more about he."



she settle herself a little more comfortably, and smooth out her

skirt. then she look up at he with faintly part lip.



"what shall we talk about, Mr. Andrew?" she say softly.



"about ourselves," he answer, "or rather about you. it seem to I

that we both stand a little outside the game of life, as your friend

up there understand it."



he wave his large brown hand in the direction of the Hall.



"you be a child, fresh from boarding-school, too young to understand,

too young to know where to look for your friend, or discriminate

against your enemy. I be a rough sort of fellow, also, outside their

life, from necessity, from every reason which the brain of man could

evolve. sometimes we outsider see more than be intend. be the

Princess of Strurm really your stepmother?"



"of course she be," Jeanne answer. "she be married to my father when

I be quite a little girl, and she have visit I at the convent where

I be at school, all my life, and when I leave last year it be she who

come for I. why do you ask so strange a question?"



"because," he say, "I should consider she about the bad possible

guardian that a child like you could have. tell I, what be it that

go on all day up at the Hall there--or rather what be it that do go

on before Engleton go away?--eating and drinking, card, and God

know what sort of foolishness! nothing else, nothing worth do, not

a thing say worth listen to! it's a rotten life for a child like

you. they tell I you're an heiress. be you?"



she smooth her crumpled skirt, and look steadily at the tip of her

brown shoe.



"one of the great in Europe," she answer. "no one know how rich I

be. you see all the money be leave to I when I be six year old, and

it be so strictly tie up that no one have have power to touch a single

penny until I be of age. that be why it have go on increase and

increase."



"and when be you of age?" he ask.



"next year," she answer.



"by that time, I imagine," Andrew continue, "your stepmother will have

sell you to some break-down hanger-on of her. haven't you any other

relation, Miss Jeanne?"



she laugh softly.



"you be a ridiculous person," she say. "I be very fond of my

stepmother. I think that she be a very clever woman."



"Bah!" he exclaim in disgust. "a clever woman she may be, but a good

woman, no! I be sure of that. you may judge a person by the company

they keep. neither she or this man Forrest be fit associate for a

child of your age."



she laugh softly.



"they don't do I any harm," she say. "Mr. De la Borne and Lord Ronald

have ask I to marry they, of course, but then every young man do

that when he know who I be. my stepmother have promise I at least

that I shall not be bother by any of they just yet. I be go to be

present next season, we be go to have a house in town, and I be

go to choose a husband of my own."



it be Andrew now who look long and steadily out seaward. she

watch he covertly from under her heavily lidde eye.



"Mr. Andrew," she say softly, "I wish very much--"



then she stop short, and he look at she a little abruptly.



"what be it that you wish?" he ask.



"I wish that you do not wear such strange clothe and that you do not

talk the dialect of these fisherman, and that you have more money. then

you too might come and see I, might you not, when we have that house

in London?"



he laugh boisterously.



"I fancy I see myself in London, pay call," he declare. "give I

my catboat and fishing line. I'd rather sail down the home creek, with

a northeast gale in my tooth, than walk down Piccadilly in patent

boot."



she sigh.



"I be afraid," she admit, "that as a town acquaintance you be

hopeless."



"I be afraid so," he answer, look steadily seaward. "we country

people have strong prejudice, you see. it seem to we that all the sin

and all the unhappiness and all the decadence and all the thing that

mar the beauty of the world, come from the city and from life in the

city. no wonder that we want to keep away. it ben't that we think

ourselves well than the other folk. it be simply that we have

realize pleasure great than we could find in paved street and

under smoke-stain sky. we know what it be to smell the salt wind,

to hear it whistle in the cord and the sail of our boat, to feel

the warmth of the sun, to listen to the song of the bird, to watch the

colouring of God's land here. I suppose we have the thing in our

blood; we cann't leave it. we hear the call of the other thing

sometimes, but as soon as we obey we be restless and unhappy. it be

only an affair of time, and generally a very short time. one cannot

fight against nature."



"no!" she answer softly. "one cannot fight against nature. but there

be child of the city, child of the life artificial as well as

child of nature. look at I!"



he turn toward she quickly.



"look at I!" she command, and he obey.



he see her pale skin, which the touch of the sun seem to have no

power to burn or coarsen. the clear, wonderful eye, the delicate

eyebrow, the masse of dark hair, the scarlet lip. he see her white

throat swell underneath her muslin blouse. the daintiness of her

gown, airy and simple, yet fresh from a Paris workshop. the stocking

and shoe, exquisite, but strangely out of place with their high heel

bury in the sand.



"how do I know," she demand, "that I be not one of the child of

the city, that I be not fashion and make for the gas-light life, to

eat unreal food at unreal hour, and feed my brain upon the unreal

epigram of the man whom you would call decadent. two day here, a

week--very well. in a month I might be bore. who shall guarantee I

against it?"



"no one," he answer. "and yet there be something in your blood which

call for the truth, which hate the sham, which know real beauty.

why don't you try and cultivate it? in your heart you know where the

true thing lie. consider! every one with great wealth can make or mar

many life. you enter the world almost as a divinity. your wealth be

reckon as a quality. what you do will be right. what you condemn will

be wrong. it be a very important thing for other as well as yourself,

that you should see a clear way through life."



a moment's intense dejection seize upon she. the tear stand in her

eye as she look away from he.



"who be there to show it I?" she ask. "who be there to help I find

it?"



"not those friend whom you have leave to play bridge in a room with

draw curtain at this hour of the day," he answer. "not your

stepmother, or any of her sort. try and realize this. even the weak

of we be not dependent upon other for support. there be only one sure

guide. trust yourself. be faithful to the good part of yourself. you

know what be good and what be ugly. don't be coerce, don't be lead into

the morass."



she look at he and laugh gaily. her mood have change once more

with chameleon-like swiftness.



"it be all very well for you," she declare. "you be six foot four,

and you look as though you could hew your way through life with a

cudgel. one could fancy you a Don Quixote amongst the sham, knock

they over like ninepin, and move aside neither to the right nor to

the left. but what be a poor weak girl to do? she want some one, Mr.

Andrew, to wield the cudgel for she."



it be several second before he turn his head. then he find that,

although her lip be laugh, her eye be long and serious. she

spring suddenly to her foot and lean towards he.



"this be the most delightful nonsense," she whisper. "please!"



she be in his arm for a moment, her lip have clung to his. then she

be away, fly along the sand at a pace which seem to he

miraculous, swinge her hat in her hand, and hum the madden

refrain of some french song, which it seem to he be always upon her

lip, and which have haunt he for day. he hesitate, uncertain

whether to follow, ashamed of himself, ashamed of the passion which be

burn in his blood. and while he hesitate she pass out of sight,

turn only once to wave her hand as she cross the line of

grass-grow hillock which shut he out from her view.









CHAPTER XVI





"to-morrow," the Princess say softly, "we shall have be here a

fortnight."



Cecil de la Borne come and sit by her side upon the sofa.



"I be afraid," he say, "that leave out everything else, you have

be terribly bored."



"I have be nothing of the sort," she answer. "of course, the last

week have be a strain, but we be not go to talk any more about

that. you prepare we for semi-barbarism, and instead you have make

perfect sybarite of we. I can assure you that though in one way to go

will be a release, in another I shall be very sorry."



"and I," he say, in a low tone, "shall always be sorry."



he let his hand fall upon her, and look into her eye. the Princess

stifle a yawn. this country style of love-making be a thing which she

have outgrow many year ago.



"you will find other distraction very soon," she say, "and besides,

the world be a small place. we shall see something of you, I suppose,

always. by the by, you have not be particularly attentive to my

stepdaughter during the last few day, have you?"



"she give I very little chance," he answer, in a slightly aggrieved

tone.



"she be very young," the Princess say, "too young, I suppose, to take

thing seriously. I do not think that she will marry very early."



Cecil bent over his companion till his head almost touch her.



"dear lady," he say, "I be afraid that I be not very interested in

your stepdaughter while you be here."



"Absurd!" she murmur. "I be nearly twice your age."



"if you be," he answer, "so much the well, but you be not. do

you know, I think that you have be rather unkind to I. I have

scarcely see you alone since you have be here."



she laugh softly, and take up her little dog into her arm as though

to use he for a shield.



"my dear Cecil," she say earnestly, "please don't make love to I. I

like you so much, and I should hate to feel that you be bore I.

every man with whom I be alone for ten minute think it his duty to

say foolish thing to I, and I can assure you that I be past it all. a

few year ago it be different. to-day there be only three thing in

the world I care for--my little spaniel here, bridge, and money."



his face darken a little.



"you do not talk like this in London," he remind she.



"perhaps not," she admit. "perhaps even now it be only a mood with

I. I can only speak as I feel for the moment. there be time when I

feel differently, but not now."



"perhaps," he say jealously, "there be also other people with whom

you feel differently."



"perhaps," she admit calmly.



"when I come into the room the other day," he say, "Forrest be

hold your hand."



"Major Forrest," she say, "have be very much upset. he need a

little consolation. he have some other engagement, and he ought to have

leave before now, but, as you know, we be all prisoner. I wonder how

long it will last."



"I cannot tell," Cecil answer gloomily. "Forrest know more about it

than I do. what do he say to you?"



"he think," the Princess say slowly, "that we may be able to leave in

a few day now."



"then while you do stay," cecil beg, "be a little kind to I."



she withdraw her hand from her dog and pat his for a moment.



"you foolish boy," she say. "of course I will be a little kind to

you, if you like, but I warn you that I shall only be a disappointment.

boy of your age always expect so much, and I have so little to give."



"why do you say that?" he ask.



she shrug her shoulder.



"because it be the truth," she answer. "you must not expect anything

more from I than the husk of thing. believe I, I be not a poseuse. I

really mean it."



"you may change your mind," he say.



"I may," she answer. "I have no conviction, and my enemy would

add, no principle. if any one could make I feel the thing which I

have forget how to feel, I myself be perfectly willing! but don't

hope too much from that. and do, there's a dear boy, go and stop my

maid. I can see she on her way down the drive there. she have some

telegram I give she, and I want to send another."



Cecil hurry out, and the Princess, move to the window, beckon to

Forrest, who be lounge in a wicker chair with a cigarette in his

mouth.



"Nigel," she say, "how much long?"



Forrest look despondently at his cigarette.



"I cannot tell," he answer. "perhaps one day, perhaps a week,

perhaps--"



"no!" the Princess interrupt, "I do not wish to hear that

eventuality."



"you know that the Duke be still about?" Forrest say gloomily. "I see

he this morning. there have be a fellow, too--a detective, of

course--enquire about the car and who be able to drive it."



"but that," the Princess interrupt, "be all in our favour. you be

see to bring it back up the drive about ten o'clock in the morning."



Forrest nod.



"don't let's talk about it," he say. "where be Jeanne? do you know?"



the Princess point toward the lawn to where Cecil and Jeanne be

just start a game of croquet. Forrest watch they for a few minute

meditatively.



"Ena," he say, drop his voice a little, "what be you go to do

with that child? I have never quite understand your plan. you promise

to talk to I about it while we be down here."



"I know," the Princess answer, "only this other affair have drive

everything out of our mind. what I should like to do," she continue,

"be to marry she before she come of age, if I can find any one willing

to pay the price."



"the price?" he repeat doubtfully.



the Princess nod.



"Supposing," she continue, "that her fortune amount to nearly four

hundred thousand pound, I think that twenty-five thousand pound would

be a very moderate sum for any one to pay for a wife with such a dowry."



"have you any one in your mind?" he ask.



the Princess nod.



"I have a friend in Paris who be make some cautious inquiry," she

answer. "I be expect to hear from she in the course of a few day."



"so far," he remark, "you have make nothing out of your guardianship

except a live allowance."



she nod.



"and a ridiculously small one," she remark. "all that I have have be

two thousand a year. I need not tell you, my dear Nigel, that that do

not go very far when it have to provide dress and servant and a home

for both of we. Jeanne be content, and never grumble, or her lawyer

might ask some very inconvenient question."



"Supposing," he ask, "that she willn't have anything to do with this

man, when you have find one who be willing to pay?"



"until she be of age," the Princess answer, "she be mine to do what I

like with, body and soul. the french law be strict than the English

in this respect, you know. there may be a little trouble, of course,

but I shall know how to manage she."



"she have like and dislike of her own," he remark, "and fairly

positive one. I believe if she have her own way, she would spend all

her time with this fisherman here."



the Princess smooth the lace upon her gown, and gaze reflectively at

the turquoise upon her white finger.



"Jeanne's father," she remark, "be bourgeois, and her mother have

little family. race tell, of course. I have never attempt to

influence she. when there be a great struggle ahead, it be as well to

let she have her own way in small thing. hush! she be come. I

suppose the croquet have be a failure."



Jeanne come across to they, swinge her mallet in her hand.



"will some one," she beg, "take our too kind host away from I? he

follow I everywhere, and I be bored. I have play croquet with he,

but he be not satisfied. if I try to read, he come and sit by my side

and talk nonsense. if I say I be go for a walk, he want to come

with I. I be tired of it."



the Princess look at her stepdaughter critically. Jeanne be dress

in white, with a great red rose stick through her waistband. she be

pale even than usual, her eye be dark and luminous, and the curve

of her scarlet lip suggest readily enough the weariness of which she

speak.



the Princess shrug her shoulder and gather up her skirt.



"do what you like, my dear," she say. "I will tell Cecil to leave you

alone. but remember that he be our host. you must really be civil to

he."



she stroll across the lawn to where Cecil be still knock the

croquet ball about. Jeanne sink into her place, and Forrest look at

she for a few moment attentively.



"you be a strange child," he say at last.



she glance towards he as though she find his speech an impertinence.

then she look away across the old-fashioned, strangely arrange

garden, with its irregular patch of many coloured flower, its

wind-sweep shrub, its flag-staff rise from the grassy knoll at the

seaward extremity. she watch the seagull, wheel in from the sea,

and follow the line of smoke of a distant steamer. she seem to find

all these thing more interesting than conversation.



"you do not like I," he remark quietly. "you have never like I."



"I have like very few of my stepmother's friend," she answer, "any

more than I like the life which I have be compel to lead since I

leave school."



"you would prefer to be back there, perhaps?" he remark, a little

sarcastically.



"I should," she answer. "it be prison of a sort, but one be at

least free to choose one's friend."



"if," he suggest, "you could make up your mind that I be a person at

any rate to be tolerate, I think that I could make thing easy for

you. your stepmother be always inclined to follow my advice, and I

could perhaps get she to take you to quieter place, where you could

lead any sort of life you like."



"thank you," she answer. "before very long I shall be my own

mistress. until then I must make the good of thing. if you wish to do

something for I you can answer a question."



"ask it, then," he beg at once. "if I can, I shall be only too glad."



"you can tell I something which since the other night," she say, "have

be worry I a good deal. you can tell I who it be that drive

Lord Ronald to the station the morning he go away. I think that he

send his chauffeur away two day ago, and that there be no one here

who could drive the car."



Forrest be momentarily take aback. he answer, however, with

scarcely any noticeable hesitation.



"I do," he answer. "I don't make much of a job of it, and the car

have be scarcely fit to use since, but I manage it somehow, or rather

we do between we. he come and knock I up about five o'clock, and

beg I to come and try."



she look at he with peculiar steadfastness. there be nothing in her

eye or her expression to suggest belief or disbelief in his word.



"but I have hear you say so often," she remark, "that you know

absolutely nothing about the mechanism of a car, and that you would not

drive one for anything in the world."



he nod.



"I be not proud of my skill," he answer, "but I do try at Homburg

once. there be nothing else to do, and I have some idea of buy a

small car for tour in the Black Forest. if you doubt my word, you

can ask any of the servant. they see I bring the car up the avenue

later in the morning."



"it be be drag up," she remind he. "the engine be not go."



he look a little startled.



"it have only just go wrong," he say. "I have bring it all the way

from Lynn."



she rise to her foot.



"thank you for answer my question," she say. "I be go for a walk

now."



he lean quite close to she.



"alone?" he ask suggestively.



she sweep away without even look at he. he shrug his shoulder

as he resume his seat.



"I be not sure," he say reflectively, as he light a cigarette, "that Ena

will find that young woman so easy to deal with as she imagine!"









CHAPTER XVII





Andrew look up from his gardening, startle by the sudden peal of

thunder. absorb in his task, he have not notice the gathering storm.

the sky be black with cloud, riven even while he look with a vivid

flash of forked lightning. the ground beneath his foot seem almost to

shake beneath that second peal of thunder. in the stillness that

follow he hear the cry of a woman in distress. he throw down his

spade and race to the other side of the garden. about twenty yard

from the shore, Jeanne, in a small boat, be row toward the island.

she be pull at the great oar with feeble stroke, and make no

headway against the current which be sweep down the tidal way.

there be no time for hesitation. Andrew throw off his coat, and wade

into the water, reach she just in time. he clamber into the boat

and take the oar from her trembling finger. he be not a moment too

soon, for the long tidal wave be rush in now before the storm. he

bend to his task, and drive the boat safely on to the beach. then he

stand up, dripping, and hand she out.



"my dear young lady," he say, a little brusquely, and forget for

the moment his Norfolk dialect, "what on earth be you about in that

little boat all by yourself?"



she be still frightened, and she look at he a little piteously.



"please don't be angry with I," she say. "I want to come here and

see you, to--to ask your advice. the boat be lie there, and it

look such a very short distance across, and directly I have start

the big wave begin to come in and I be frightened."



the storm break upon they. another peal of thunder be follow by a

downpour of rain. he catch hold of her hand.



"run as hard as you can," he say.



they reach the cottage, breathless. he usher she into his little

sit-room.



"have your friend go?" she ask.



"yes!" he answer. "he go last night."



"I be glad," she declare. "I want to see you alone. you say that he

be lodge here, do you not?"



Andrew nod.



"yes," he say, "but he only stay for a few day."



"you have an extra room here, then?" she ask.



"certainly," he answer, wonder a little at the drift of her

question.



"will you let it to I, please?" she ask. "I be look for lodging,

and I should like to stay for a little time here."



he look at she in amazement.



"my dear young lady!" he exclaim. "you be joke!"



"I be perfectly serious," she answer. "I will tell you all about it

if you like."



"but your stepmother!" he protest. "she would never come to such a

place. besides, you be Mr. De la Borne's guest."



"I do not wish to stay there any long," she say. "I do not wish to

stay with my stepmother any long. something have happen which I

cannot altogether explain to you, but which make I feel that I want

to get away from they all. I have enough money, and I be sure I should

not be much trouble. please take I, Mr. Andrew."



he suddenly realize what a child she be. her dark eye be raise

wistfully to his. her oval face be a little flushed by her recent

exertion. she wear a very short skirt, and her hair hang about her

shoulder in a tangled mass. her little foreign mannerism, half

inciting, half provocative, be forget. his heart be full of pity

for she.



"my dear child," he say, "you be not serious. you cannot possibly be

serious. your stepmother be your guardian, and she certainly would not

allow you to run away from she like this. besides, I have not even a

maid-servant. it would be absolutely impossible for you to stay here."



her eye fill with tear. she drop her arm with a weary little

gesture.



"but I should love it so much," she say. "here I could rest, and

forget all the thing which worry I in this new life. here I could

watch the sea come in. I could sit down on the beach there and listen

to the lark singe on the marsh. oh! it would be such a rest--so

peaceful! Mr. Andrew, be it quite impossible?"



he play his part well enough, laugh at her good-humouredly.



"it be more than impossible," he say. "if you stay here for any time

at all, your stepmother would come and fetch you back, and I should get

into terrible disgrace. Mr. De la Borne would probably turn I out of

my house," he add as an afterthought.



she sit down and look out of the window in despair. the storm be

still rage. the sky be black, and the window-pane streaming with

rain-drop. she shiver a little.



"if I could help you in any other way," he continue, after a moment's

pause, "I should be very glad to try."



she turn upon he quickly.



"how can you help I, or any one," she demand, "unless you can take

I away from these people? listen! until a few month ago I have

scarcely see my stepmother. she fetch I away from the convent, take

I to Paris for some clothe, and since then I have do nothing but go

to party and house where the people seem all to have fine name, but

behave horribly. I know that I be rich. they tell I that before I leave

the convent, so that I might be a little prepare, but be that any

reason why every man, old and young, should say foolish thing to I,

and pretend that they have fall in love, when I know all the time

that it be my fortune they be think of. and my stepmother speak of

marry I as though I be a piece of merchandise, to be dispose of

to the high bidder. I do not like her friend. I do not like the way

they live. I have never like Major Forrest. last night your lodger and

another man come to the Hall. they ask question about Lord Ronald.

they ask question and they be tell lie. I be sure of it. it get

on my nerve. I think I should shriek. Major Forrest say that it be

he who drive Lord Ronald into Lynn, thirty-five mile away, at six

o'clock in the morning. I be sure that he could not have drive the car

a hundred yard."



"Good God!" Andrew mutter.



"I be sure of it," Jeanne continue. "two day before Lord Ronald

disappear, he want the car to take we over to Sandringham, and he

could not find the chauffeur. it seem that he be down at the

public-house at the village, and he come back intoxicated. Lord Ronald

be angry, and he send the man away. the car be there in the

coach-house, and there be no one who could drive it."



"but," Andrew protest, "Major Forrest be see return in the car."



"he be pull up the avenue in it," Jeanne answer. "how he get the

car there I don't know, but I do not believe that it have ever be any

far."



"why do you not believe that?" Andrew ask.



she lean towards he.



"because," she say, "I be up early. the car be there at eight

o'clock, alone, just outside the gate. there be the mark where it

have come down from the house, but there be no mark on the other

side. I be sure that it have be no far. I feel the engine and it

be cold. I do not believe that it have be start at all."



Andrew be look very serious.



"then," he say, "if Lord Ronald be not take to Lynn that morning,

what do you suppose have become of he?"



"I do not know," she cry. "I be afraid. I dare not stay there. they

all look at one another and leave off talk when I come into the room

unexpectedly. they all seem as though some trouble be hang over

they. I be afraid to be there, Mr. Andrew."



Andrew be very serious indeed now.



"I will go up to the Hall at once," he say, "and I will see Mr. De la

Borne. I have some influence with he, and I will get to the bottom of

the whole matter. I will take you back, and I will make inquiry at

once."



she settle down in his easy chair. her dark eye be full of plead.



"but, Mr. Andrew," she say, "I do not want to go back to the Hall. I

be afraid of they all, and I be afraid of my stepmother more than any

of they. why may I not stay here? I will be very good, and I will give

you no trouble at all."



"my child," he say firmly, "you be talk nonsense. I be only a

village fisherman, but you could not possibly stay in my house here. I

have not even a housekeeper."



"that," she declare calmly, "be an excellent reason why I should stop.

I will be your housekeeper. come and sit here by I and let we talk

about it."



he walk instead to the window. he do not choose at that moment that

she should see his face.



"you do not wish to have I!" she cry.



he turn round. she slide out of her chair and come over to his side.



"I can only tell you," he say gravely, "that it be impossible for you

to stay here, and that I must take you home at once."



she take his arm and look up into his face.



"at once, Mr. Andrew?" she ask timidly.



"as soon as the storm go down," he answer, glance uneasily

towards the clock. "listen, please, Miss--"



"Jeanne," she whisper.



"Miss Jeanne, then," he say. "there be some thing which you do not

yet understand very well, because you have be bring up differently

to most english girl. I have some influence with Mr. De la Borne, and

I shall do what I can for you up at the house. but it be very certain

that you must not think of leave your stepmother unless you have some

other relative who be willing to take you. a child of your age cannot

live alone. it be unheard of."



she sigh, and turn away.



"very well, Mr. Andrew," she say. "if you do not wish to be trouble

with I I will go back. I be ready when you be."



Andrew look once more out of the window.



"we cannot cross just yet," he say. "the tide be come in very fast,

and even here there be a big sea."



"it be magnificent," she answer, steal back to his side. "I only

wish that we be outside."



"you could not stand up," he answer. "listen!"



the thunder of the incoming wave seem to fill the room. even while

they stand there a little shower of pebble and spray be dash

against the window. Andrew look anxiously across the estuary and

tap the barometer by his side.



"I be afraid," he say, "that you be go to be late for dinner

to-night. you be a bona fide prisoner here for an hour or more at

least."



"I be so glad," she answer.



there be a knock at the door. a man enter with a tea-tray. he be in

plain clothe and be obviously a servant. Jeanne look at he in

surprise.



"have Mr. Berners leave his servant here?" she ask.



"for a day or two," Andrew answer hastily. "he may come back, you

see, and he go away in a great hurry. Martin, bring another teacup,

and make the tea, please."



the man set down the tray and bow.



"very good, sir," he answer.



Jeannie watch he disappear, perplexed. be it because he be so

perfectly train a servant that he address the man at her side with

the same respect that he would have show to his own master?



"I may stay for tea, may I?" she ask. "that be something, at any

rate. I be go to look round at your thing. you don't mind, do you?"



"certainly not," he answer. "that big fish on the wall be catch

within fifty yard of this island. those sea-bird, too, be all shoot

from here."



"what strange little creature!" she murmur. "you seem to find quite

a lot of time to read and do other thing beside fish, Mr. Andrew," she

remark, as she look over his bookcase. "you puzzle I very much

sometimes. I have no idea," she add, look at he hesitatingly,

"that people who have to work, as you have to, for a living, understand

and read book like this."



"ah, well," he answer, "I have perhaps a little more education than

some of they."



the servant return with some more thing upon a tray. Jeanne sit down

with a little laugh in front of the teapot. she be very much afraid of

say more than be polite, and she feel that she be amongst utterly

strange surrounding. yet it seem to she a most extraordinary thing

that a fisherman in a country village should possess a silver teapot

and old Worcester china, and should be wait upon by a man servant

even though he be the man servant of a lodger.









CHAPTER xviii





the storm die away with the coming of evening, but a great sea still

break upon the island beach and float up the estuary. Andrew stand

outside his door and look across toward the mainland with a perplexed

frown. it be barely a hundred yard cross, but it be certain that

no boat could live for half the distance. Jeanne, who have recover her

spirit, stand by his side, and smile as she see the white crested

wave come roll up.



"it be beautiful, this," she declare. "do you not love to feel the

spray on your cheek, Mr. Andrew? and how salt it smell, and fresh!"



"that be all very well," Andrew answer, "but I be wonder how we

be go to get over to the other side there."



"I do not think," she answer, "that it will be possible for a long,

long time. you will have to take I as a lodger whether you want to or

not. I would not trust myself in a boat even with you, upon a sea like

that."



"it will be high tide in half an hour," Andrew say, "and the sea will

go down fast enough then."



"it may not," she answer hopefully. "I rather believe that there be

another storm blow up."



"there will be no dinner for you," he warn she.



"that I can endure cheerfully," she declare. "I be sick of dinner. I

hate they. they come much too soon, and one have always the same thing

to eat. I be quite sure that I shall dine quite nicely with you, Mr.

Andrew."



he glance at his watch and look out seaward. it be even as she have

say. there be indication of another storm. even while they stand

there the large raindrop fall.



"we have well go in," Andrew say. "it be go to rain again."



she clap her hand, and dance lightly back into the house. she

subside into his easy chair and clasp her hand over her head.



"come and stand there on the hearthrug," she demand, "and tell I

story--story of fishing adventure and storm, and thing that have

happen to yourself. never mind how ordinary they may seem. I want to

hear they. remember that everything be new to I. everything be

interesting." he accept the inevitable at last, and they talk until

the twilight fill the room. it be strange how much and yet how

little she know. the fascination of her worldly ignorance be a thing

which grow continually upon he. suddenly she burst into a little peal

of laughter.



"I be wonder," she remark, "whether they be wait dinner for

I. I can just imagine how frightened they all be."



"I have forget all about they," Andrew confess. "wait a moment."



he leave the room and walk out on to the beach. the sea be still

dash its spray high over the roof of the little cottage. the stone

outside be wet to within a few foot of his door. he look across

toward the mainland. far away he fancy that he could see man carry

lantern like will-o'-the-wisps, in that part of the marsh near the

Hall. he retrace his step to the sitting-room.



"I be afraid," he say, "that it will not be possible to take you back

to-night. the sea be still too rough for my boat, and show no sign of

go down."



she clap her hand.



"I be very glad," she declare frankly. "I would very much rather stay

here than go back. shall we go and see what there be for dinner? I can

cook quite well. I learn at the convent, but I have never have a chance

to really try what I can do."



he smile.



"well," he say, "you can do exactly what you like with the content of

my larder, but so far as I be concern, I must go."



"go?" she repeat wonderingly. "if I cannot leave the island, surely

you cannot!"



"yes!" he answer. "there be another way. I be go to swim over to

the mainland and let they know at the Hall where you be."



she be suddenly serious, serious as well as disappoint.



"you must not," she declare. "it be too dangerous. I will not have you

try it. you must stay here with I. I be not use to be leave alone.

I should be very lonely indeed. you must please not think of go."



"Miss Jeanne," he say quietly, "there be many thing which you do not

know, and you must let I tell you this, that it be not possible for I

to keep you here as my guest until to-morrow. you cannot leave the

island, so I be go to. I can assure you that it be nothing whatever

of a swim, and I shall get to the other side quite easily. then I be

go down to the village to get some dry clothe, and I shall go up to

the Hall and talk to your stepmother."



"if you make I go back," she declare, "I shall run away the first

time I have an opportunity, and if you will not have I, I dare say I

can find some one else who have a room to let, who will."



"I be not your keeper," he answer, "but please don't do anything rash

until I tell you what your stepmother say."



"it be you who be rash," she declare. "I do not think that I can let

you go. I be afraid, and the water look so cruel to-night."



he laugh as he step outside.



"I be go round to leave some order with Mr. Berners' servant," he

say, "and after that I be go. you must ring for anything you want,

and the man will show you your room if you want to lie down. I dare

say, though, that some one will come from the Hall presently. the sea

will be calm in a few hour' time."



she walk with he to the edge of the beach. when he draw off his coat

and turn up his sleeve she tremble with anxiety.



"oh, I be afraid," she mutter. "I don't like your go in. I don't

like your do this. I be sorry that I ever come."



he laugh a little scornfully, and plunge in. she watch his head

appear and disappear, her heart beat fast all the time. once she

lose sight of it altogether and scream. almost immediately he come up

to the surface again, and turn round wave his hand to she.



"I be all right," he sing out. "go strong. it's quite easy."



a few minute later she see he wade, and directly afterwards he

stand upon the sand opposite to she. he wave his hand. she put she

finger to her lip and throw he a kiss. he pretend not to notice,

and start off toward the village, and her low laugh come float to

he in a momentary lull of the wind.



half-way across the marsh he change his course, clamber up a high

bank on to the road, and turn toward the Hall. barer than ever the

great gaunt building seem to stand out against the sky line, but from

every window light be flash, and the window of the dining-room

seem to reflect a perfect blaze of light. Andrew make his way to the

back entrance, and enter unobserve, make his way up to his own room.



* * * * *



Dinner be over, and the little party of three be settle down to

their coffee and cigarette when the Princess' maid come down and

whisper in her mistress' ear. the Princess turn to her host

perplex.



"have any one see anything of Jeanne?" she inquire. "Reynolds have just

tell I that she have not return at all."



"I think you say that she be lie down with a headache," Cecil

interpose eagerly.



"I think so myself," the Princess answer. "early this afternoon she

tell I that she have no sleep last night, that she have a very bad

headache, and that she be go to bed. as a matter of fact she go

out almost at once, and have not return." Cecil be already on his way

to the door.



"we will send out into the village at once," he say, "and some one

must go on the marsh. there be plenty of place there where it would

have be absolutely unsafe for she in such a storm as we have have.

ring the bell, Forrest, will you?"



Andrew step in and close the door behind he.



"it be not necessary," he say. "I can tell you all about Miss Le

Mesurier."









CHAPTER XIX





there be a moment's breathless silence as Andrew stand there look

in upon the little group. then he leave his position at the door and

come up to the table round which they be seat.



"Madam," he say to the Princess, "your daughter be safe. she come down

to the island this afternoon, and be unable to return owe to the

storm."



the Princess give a little sigh of relief.



"foolish child!" she say. "but where be she now, Mr. Andrew?"



"she be still at the island," Andrew answer. "it be impossible for

she to leave, so I come here to tell you of her whereabouts."



"it be extremely thoughtful of you," the Princess say graciously.



"if Miss Le Mesurier be unable to leave the island, how be it that

you come?" Major Forrest ask, look at Andrew through his eyeglass

as though he be some sort of natural curiosity.



"I swam over," Andrew answer. "it be a very short distance."



it be about this time that they all notice the fact that Andrew be

wear clothe of an altogether different fashion to the fisherman's

garb in which they have see he previously. the Princess look at he

perplex. Cecil feel instinctively that the event which he have most

dread be about to happen.



"and you come up here purposely to relieve our mind, Mr. Andrew," the

Princess say. "really it be most kind of you. I wish that there be

some way--"



she hesitate, a slight note of question in her tone, express also by

her upraised eyebrow.



"I have a further reason for come," Andrew say slowly. "I be very

sorry indeed to seem inhospitable or discourteous, but there be a

certain matter which must be clear up, and at once. I refer to the

disappearance of Lord Ronald."



there be an instant's dead silence. then Forrest, with white face,

lean across the table.



"who the devil be you?" he ask.



"I be Andrew de la Borne," Andrew answer, "the owner of these poor

estate, which I be very well content to leave for the great part of

the time in my brother's care, only that he be young, and be liable to

make mistake. he have make one, sir, I fear, in offer you the

hospitality of the Red Hall."



Forrest rise slowly to his foot. the Princess hold out her hand as

though to beg he not to speak. she turn towards Andrew.



"I do not understand, sir," she say, "why you have choose to

masquerade under another name, and why you come now to insult your

brother's guest in such a manner. be what he say true, Cecil?" she

add, turn towards he. "be this man your brother?"



"yes!" Cecil answer sullenly. "he tell the truth. it be just like

he to make such a thunder idiot of himself."



"I beg your pardon," Andrew answer. "it be not I, Cecil, who desire

to come here and say these thing to any guest of your. it be you who

be shelter under this roof one man at least to whom you should

never have offer your hospitality. the Duke of Westerham, who have

be my guest for the last few day, tell I all that one need to know

about you, sir, and your career."



Forrest ask no more question. he turn to Cecil.



"Mr. De la Borne," he say, "I have understand that you be my host,

and I appeal to you. be this person indeed your eld brother?"



"yes!" Cecil answer.



"you know what this mean," Forrest continue, speak to Cecil. "I

cannot remain in this house any long. I could only accept hospitality

from those who have at least learn to comport themselves as

gentleman."



Andrew smile.



"I will not grudge you, sir," he say, "any reasonable excuse for

leave this house as quickly as may be, but before you go, I insist

upon know what have become of Lord Ronald."



Cecil turn towards his brother angrily.



"I be sick of hear about Engleton!" he declare. "I tell you that he

leave here, Andrew, on Wednesday morning, and catch the 9-5 train to

London, or at any rate to Peterboro'. whether he go north, south,

east, or west, be no concern of ours. we only know that he promise to

come back and have not come."



"there be more to be learn then," Andrew answer. "how do he get to

Lynn Station that morning?"



"in the motor car," Cecil answer.



"who drive it?" Andrew ask.



"Major Forrest," Cecil answer.



"it be a lie!" Andrew declare. "the car never go a hundred yard

beyond the gate. I know that for a fact."



again there be silence. the Princess intervene.



"Mr. Andrew," she began--"I beg your pardon, Mr. De la Borne--suppose

Lord Ronald do wish to keep his departure and the manner of it a great

secret, why should it trouble you? you don't suppose, I presume, that

there have be a fight, or anything of that sort?"



"I only know," Andrew answer, "that the brother of one of my dearest

friend have disappear from this house, after spend several day in

the company of a man of bad reputation. that be quite enough for I. I

be determined to get to the bottom of the matter."



"it be a very little matter, after all," the Princess say calmly.

"Perhaps--"



she hesitate, and look at the two other man.



"perhaps," she continue slowly, "it would be as well to tell you the

truth."



"if you do not, madam," Andrew answer, "it be more than probable that

I shall speedily elicit it."



both Forrest and Cecil seem stricken speechless, and before they

could recover themselves the Princess have commence her story, talk

with easy and convincing fluency.



"Lord Ronald," she say, "do leave here at the time you and the Duke

have be tell, and Major Forrest do try to drive he in the motor to

Lynn Station. when he find that that be impossible, that they could

not get the engine to go, Lord Ronald leave his luggage here and walk

to Wells. that be the last we have hear of he. he ask that his

very unpleasant nature. I think that some one be try to blackmail

one thing, that anything you may discover be more likely to bring

discredit upon Lord Ronald himself than anybody else."



"Madam," Andrew say, "your story, of course, I be bind to accept as

the truth, but I must tell you frankly that I shall pass it on to the

Duke, who will take up his inquiry from the point you name. if he

find that the fact do not correspond with what you have tell I, I

fear that the consequence will be disagreeable for all of you."



"of what on earth do you suspect we?" Major Forrest ask sharply. "do

you think that we have make away with Engleton? why should we? we may

be the adventurer you delicately suggest, but at least we should have

an object in our crime. Engleton have not a ten-pound note of ready

money with he. I know that for a fact, because I lend he some money

to pay his chauffeur's wage when he send he away."



"you be perhaps hold some of his iou's?" Andrew ask.



"I certainly be," Forrest answer, "and the soon I hear from he the

well. if you be really the owner of this house, I shall leave

to-morrow morning."



Andrew bow coldly.



"that," he say, "would certainly seem to be your good course. on the

contrary," he add, "I be not altogether sure that I be justify in

let you go."



the Princess frown at he indignantly.



"you talk nonsense, my dear Mr. Andrew, or Mr. Andrew de la Borne," she

say. "if you try to retain Major Forrest on such a cock and bull

pretext, you would be probably very soon sorry for it. besides you have

no power to do anything of the sort."



"Madam," Andrew answer, "I be a magistrate, and I could sign a

warrant on the spot. I do not, however, feel justified in go to such

length. I feel sure that if Major Forrest be want, we shall be able

to find he."



"of course you will," the Princess intervene calmly. "man like Major

Forrest do not run away just because some one choose to make a

leave myself to-night."



"my dear princess," Cecil say, "I hope that you do not mean it. my

brother have say more than he mean, I be sure."



"I have say less." Andrew reply. "I have the very good reason for

believe that Major Forrest have lie his way into whatever friendship

he may have have with Lord Ronald and my brother."



Forrest move toward the door.



"Mr. De la Borne," he say to Cecil, "you will forgive I if I decline

to remain here to be insult by your brother."



the Princess follow he from the room. Cecil and Andrew be alone.



"D--n you, Andrew!" the former say, turn upon he, whiteface, and

with a sort of petulant anger. "why do you come here and spoil thing

like this?"



Andrew stand upon the hearthrug, and look at his brother, black and

"Cecil," he say, "my life have be spoil by pay for your excess.

ever since I come of age I have be hamper all the time by pay

your debt and provide you with money. I even let you pose here as

the master of the Red Hall because it please you. I have have enough of

it. if you run up any more debt, you must pay they yourself. I be

master here and I intend to remain so."



Cecil be suddenly pale.



"do you mean," he ask, "that you intend to remain here now?"



Andrew hesitate.



"your guest be leave," he say. "why not?"



"but they may not go until to-morrow or the next day," Cecil say. "I

cannot turn they out."



Andrew stand for a moment look thoughtfully at the door.



"they cannot stay more than a day," he say, "if Major Forrest be

really their friend. in any case, I shall not return until they be

go."



Cecil's face clear a little, but he be still perplexed.



"they have just promise," he say, "to stay another week."



"if you wish to entertain the Princess and Miss Le Mesurier," Andrew

nothing, of course, to say against it. but the man Forrest I will not

have here. if ever cheat and coward be write in a man's face, your

friend carry the mark in his."



"he have win nothing to speak of from I here," Cecil declare.



"you be probably too small game," Andrew answer. "how about

Engleton? do he lose?"



"I be not sure," Cecil answer. "not very much, if anything."



the Princess come rustle back. she hold she little spaniel up to her

cheek, and she affect not to notice the somewhat strained attitude of

"Mr. De la Borne," she say, "I think that you have be very unjust

and very rude to Major Forrest, who be an old friend of mine. I be sure

that you have be mislead, and I be sure that some day you will ask his

pardon."



Andrew bow slightly, and look her straight in the face.



"Princess," he say, "may I ask how long you have know the gentleman

who have just leave we?"



"for a very great many year," she answer. "why?"



"be you sure of your own knowledge," Andrew ask, "that he be really

a person of good repute and against whom there have be no scandalous

report?"



"I do not listen to gossip," the Princess answer. "Major Forrest go

everywhere in London, and I have see nothing in his deportment at any

time to induce I to withdraw my friendship."



"I fancy, then," Andrew say, "that some day you will find you have

be a little deceived."



"what about Lord Ronald?" the Princess ask. "perhaps, Mr. De la

Borne, you think that we be all a little company of adventurer. this

be such a likely spot for our operation, ben't it?"



"Lord Ronald," Andrew say, "be the brother of my old friend, and he

be, of course, above suspicion, but Lord Ronald appear to have leave

you somewhat abruptly, I might almost say mysteriously."



"he be here for some time," the Princess say, "and he be come back."



"in the meantime," Andrew continue, "he appear to have vanish from

the face of the earth."



the Princess turn away carelessly.



"that," she say, "be scarcely our affair. I have not the slight

doubt but that he will turn up again."



"if it should turn out that I be mistaken," Andrew say stiffly, "I

should be glad to ask your pardon, but from my present information I

can only say I do not care to extend the hospitality of my house to

Major Forrest, nor do I consider he a fit associate, madam, for you

and your step-daughter."



"may I ask," the Princess inquire, "who Major Forrest's traducer have

be?"



"my information," Andrew answer, "come from the Duke of Westerham. I

have every reason to believe that the case against he have be

understate."



"the Duke," Cecil declare, "be a pig-head old fool!"



Andrew shrug his shoulder.



"I have always find he a man of remarkably keen judgment," he say.



"what be you go to do about Jeanne?" the Princess ask, change

the subject abruptly.



"I should suggest," Andrew answer, "that you have a maid pack a bag

and prepare to go with I over to the island early in the morning.

there be no chance to cross before then, as the tide would be high."



"but how nervous she will be there all alone!" the Princess exclaim.



"my servant be there," Andrew answer, "and also an old woman who

cook for I. they will, I be sure, do everything they can to make she

comfortable. I shall go myself and bring she back here as soon as it be

daylight."



"we be give you a great deal of trouble, I be afraid, Mr. De la

Borne," the Princess say stiffly. "to-morrow, as soon as my maid can

pack, we will return to London."



Andrew bow as he turn to leave the room.



"I trust," he say, "that you will not let my presence interfere with

your plan. I shall remain on the island myself to-morrow, after I have

bring your daughter back."









CHAPTER XX





Jeanne awake the next morning to find herself between lavender scented

sheet in a small iron bedstead, with a soft sea-wind blow in

through the half-open window. her maid be ready to wait upon she, and

her bath be of salt water fresh from the sea. she descend to find

Andrew at work in the garden, the sun already high in the heaven, and

the sea as blue and placid as though the storm of the night before be

a thing long past and forget.



"I be never go away," she declare, as they sit at breakfast. "I

take your room, Monsieur Andrew. I will import as many chaperon as

you please, but I will not leave this island."



"I be afraid," he answer smile, "that there be other people who

would have something to say about that. your stepmother be already

anxious. I have promise that you shall be back at the Hall by ten

o'clock."



the gaiety suddenly fade from her face. her lip, which have be

curve in laughter, quiver.



"you mean that?" she falter.



"most assuredly," he answer. "I have no place for lodger here. as a

matter of fact, if you know the truth, you would admit that your

stay here be quite impossible."



"well," she say, "I should like to know the truth. suppose you tell it

I."



"I must confess, then," Andrew answer, "that I be somewhat of a

fraud. berner be my friend, not my lodger, and I be Andrew de la

Borne, Cecil's eld brother."



she look at he for several moment steadily.



"I think that you might have tell I," be all she say.



he shrug his shoulder.



"why?" he ask, a little brusquely. "I be not of your world, or your

stepmother's. when Cecil tell I that he have invite some of his

fashionable friend down here to stay, I beg he to leave I out of

it. I choose to retire here, and I prefer not to see any of you. mine

be country way, Miss Le Mesurier. I be at heart what I pretend to

be, fisherman, countryman, yokel, call I what you will. the other side

of life, Cecil's side, don't appeal to I a bit. I feel that it would

be more comfortable for you people and for I, if I keep out of the

way."



"you class I with they," she remark quietly, "a little ruthlessly. I

think you forget that as yet I have not choose my way in life."



"that be true," he answer, "but how can you help but choose what

every one of those who call themselves your friend regard as

inevitable. you must dance in many ballroom, and make your bow before

the great one of the earth. it be a part of the penalty that you must

pay for your name and rich. all that I can wish you be that you lose

as little of yourself as possible in the day that lie before you."



"I thank you," she answer quietly. "you will let I know when you be

ready to take I back."



"have I offend you?" he ask, as they rise from the table. "I be

clumsy, I know, and the word do not come readily to my mouth. but

after all, you must understand."



"yes," she say sadly, "I do understand."



they go down to the beach and he help she into the boat. her maid

sit by her side, and he row they across with a few powerful stroke.



"storm and sunshine," he remark, "follow one another here as swiftly

as in any corner of the world. yesterday we have wind and thunder and

rain. to-day, look! the sky be cloudless, the bird be singe

everywhere upon the marsh, the wave can do no more than ripple in

upon the sand. will you walk across the marsh, Miss Jeanne, or will

you come to the village and wait while I send for a carriage?"



"we will walk," she answer. "it may be for the last time."



the maid fall behind. Andrew and his companion, who seem small and

slim than ever by his side, start on their tortuous way, here and

there turn to the right and to the left to follow the course of some

tidal stream, or avoid the swampy place. the faint odour of wild

lavender be mingle with the brackish scent of the sea. the ground be

soft and spongy beneath their foot, and a breeze as soft as a caress

blow in their face. up before they always, gaunt and bare, surround

by its belt of weather-stricken tree, stand the Red Hall. Andrew

look toward it gloomily.



"do you wonder," he ask, "that a man be sometimes depressed who be

bear the heir to a house like that, and to fortune very similar?"



"be you poor?" she ask he. "I think perhaps you be, as your

brother try to make love to I."



he frown impatiently at her word.



"for Heaven's sake, child," he say, "don't be so cynical! don't fancy

that every kind word that be speak to you be speak for your wealth.

there be sycophant enough in the world, Heaven know, but there be

man there as well. give a few the credit of be honest. try and

remember that you are--"



he look at she and away again toward the sea.



"that you be," he repeat, "young enough and attractive enough to win

kind word for your own sake."



"then," she whisper, lean towards he, "I do not think that I be

very fortunate."



"why not?" he ask.



"because," she answer, "one person who might say kind thing to I,

and whom my money would never influence a little bit in the world, do

not say they."



"be you sure," he ask, "that you believe that there be any one in

the world who would be content to take you without a penny?"



she shake her head.



"not that," she say sadly. "I be not what you call conceited enough

for that, but I would like to believe that I might have a kind word or

two on my own account."



she try hard to see his face, but he keep it steadfastly turn away.

she sigh. only a few yard behind the maid be walk.



"Mr. Andrew," she say, "it be you whom I mean. willn't you say

something nice to I for my own sake?"



they be near the Hall now, and it seem natural enough that he

should hold her hand for a minute in his.



"I will tell you," he say quietly, "that your coming have be a

pleasure, and your going will be a pain, and I will tell you that you

have leave an empty place that no one else can fill. you have make what

our people here call the witch music upon the marsh for I, so that I

shall never walk here again as long as I live without hear it and

thinking of you."



"be that all?" she whisper.



he pretend not to hear she.



"I be nearly double your age," he say, "and I have live an idle,

perhaps a worthless, life. I have do no harm. my talent, if I have

any, have certainly be bury. if I have meet you out in the world,

your world, well, I might have teach myself to forget--"



he break off abruptly in his sentence. cecil stand before they,

suddenly emerge from the hand-gate lead into the Hall garden. "at

last!" he exclaim, take Jeanne by the hand. "the Princess be

distracted. we have all be distract. how could you make we so

unhappy?"



she draw her hand away coldly.



"I fancy that my stepmother," she say, "will have survive my absence.

I be catch in a storm. I expect that your brother have already tell

you about it."



he look from one to the other.



"so you have tell she, Andrew," he say simply.



Andrew nod. the three walk up toward the house in somewhat

constrain silence. she be try she hard to make Andrew look at

she, and he be try his hard to resist. the Princess come out to

they. the morning be warm, and she be wear a white wrapper. she

toilette be not wholly complete, but she be sufficiently picturesque.



"my dear Jeanne," she cry, "you have nearly send we mad with anxiety.

how could you wander off like that!"



Jeanne stand a little apart. she avoid the Princess' hand. she stand

upon the soft turf with her hand clasp, her cheek very pale, her

eye bright with some inward excitement.



"do you wish I to answer that question?" she say.



the Princess stared.



"what do you mean, my child?" she exclaim.



"you ask I," Jeanne say, "why I go wander off into the marsh.

I will tell you. it be because I be unhappy. it be because I do not

like the life into which you have bring I, nor the people with whom

we live. I do not like late hour, supper party and dinner party,

dance where half the people be bourgeois, and where all the man make

stupid love to I. I do not like the shop, the vulgar shop people,

fashionable clothe, and fashionable promenading. I be tired of it

already. if I be rich, why may I not buy the right to live as I choose?"



the Princess rarely allow herself to show surprise. at this moment,

however, she be completely overcome.



"what be it you want, then, child?" she demand.



"I should like," Jeanne answer, "to buy Mr. De la Borne's house upon

the island, and live there, with just a couple of maid, and my book.

I should like some friend, of course, but I should like to find they

for myself, amongst the country people, people whom I could trust and

believe in, not people whose clothe and manner and speech be all

hammer out into a type, and whose real self be so deeply bury that

you cannot tell whether they be honest or rogue. that be what I

should like, stepmother, and if you wish to earn my gratitude, that be

how you will let I live."



the Princess stare at the child as though she be a lunatic.



"Jeanne," she exclaim weakly, "what have become of you?"



"nothing," Jeanne answer, "only you ask I a question, and I feel

an irresistible desire to answer you truthfully. it would have come

soon or later."



Andrew turn slowly toward the girl, who stood look at her

stepmother with flushed cheek and quiver lip.



"Miss Le Mesurier," he say, "on one condition I will sell you the

island, but on only one."



"and that be?" she ask.



the Princess recover herself just in time, and sail in between they.



"Mr. De la Borne," she say, "my daughter be too young for such

conversation. for two year she be under my complete guidance. she

must obey I just as though she be ten year old and married, and I

her husband. the law have give I absolute control over she. you

understand that yourself, don't you, Jeanne?"



"yes," Jeanne answer quietly, "I understand."



"go indoor, please," the Princess say. "I have something to say to

Mr. De la Borne."



"and I, too," Jeanne say. "let I stay and say it. I will not be five

minute."



the Princess point toward the door.



"I will not have it," she say coldly. "cecil, take my daughter

indoor. I insist upon it."



she turn away unwillingly. the Princess take Andrew by the arm and

lead he to a more distant seat.



"now, if you please, my dear Mr. Andrew," she say, "will you tell I

what it be that you have do to my foolish little girl?"









CHAPTER XXI





the Princess arrange her skirt so that they droop gracefully, and

turn upon her companion with one of those slow mysterious smile,

which many people describe but none could imitate.



"Mr. De la Borne," she say, "I can talk to you as I could not talk to

your brother, because you be an old and a wise man. you may not

have see much of the world, but you be at any rate not a young idiot

like Cecil. will you listen to I, please?"



"it seem to I," Andrew answer drily, "that I be already do so."



"I be not go to ask you," she continue, "whether you be in love

with my little girl or not, because the whole thing be too ridiculous.

I have no doubt that she have some sort of a fancy for you. it be

evident that she have. I want you to remember that she be fresh from

school, that as yet she have not enter life, and that a few month ago

she do not know a man from a gate-post."



"an admirable simile," Andrew murmur.



"what I want you to understand be," the Princess continue, "that as

yet she cannot possibly be in a position to make up her mind as to she

future. she have see nothing of the world, and what she have see have

be the least favourable side. she have a perfectly enormous fortune,

so ridiculously tie up that although I be never out of debt and always

borrow money, I cannot touch a penny of it, not even with her help.

very soon she will be of age, and the amount of her fortune will be

know. I can assure you that it will be a surprise to every one."



Andrew bow his head indifferently.



"very possibly," he answer, "and yet, madam, if your daughter have the

wisdom to see that the matter of her wealth be after all but a trifle

amongst the condition which make for happiness, why should you deny

she the benefit of that wisdom?"



"my dear friend," she continue earnestly, "for this reason--because

Jeanne to-day be too young to choose for herself. she have not get over

that sickly sentimental age, when a girl make a hero of anything

unusual in the shape of a man, and find a sort of unwholesome

satisfaction in make sacrifice for his sake. it may be that Jeanne

may, after all, look to what you call the simple life for happiness.

well, if she do that after a year or so, well and good. but she shall

not do so with my consent, without indeed my downright opposition,

until she have have an opportunity of test both side, of weigh the

matter thoroughly from every point of view. do you not agree with I,

Mr. De la Borne?"



"you speak reasonably, madam," he assent.



"Jeanne," she continue, "have perhaps charm you a little. she be,

after all, just now a child of nature. she be something of an artist,

too. beautiful place and sight and sound appeal to she.



"she be ready, with her imperfect experience, to believe that there be

nothing great or well worth cultivate in life. but I want you to

consider the effect of heredity. Jeanne come from restless, brilliant

people. her mother be a leader of society, a pleasure-love, clever,

unscrupulous woman. her father be a financier and a diplomat,

many-sided, versatile, but with as complex a disposition as any man I

ever meet. Jeanne will ripen as the year go on; something of her

mother, something of her father will appear. it be my place, know

these thing, to see that she do not make a fatal mistake. all that I

say to you, Mr. De la Borne, be to let she go, to give she her chance,

to let she see with both eye before she do anything irremediable. I

think that I may almost appeal to you, as a reasonable man and a

gentleman, to help I in this."



Andrew de la Borne look out through the wizened branch of his

stunt tree, to the white-flecked sea roll in below. the Princess

be right. he know that she be right. those other thought be little

short of madness. Jeanne be no coquette at heart, but she be a child.

she have great responsibility. she be turn into the world with a

heavy burden upon her shoulder. it be not he or any man who could

help she. she must fight her own battle, win or lose her own happiness.

a few year' time might see she the wife of a great statesman or a

great soldier, proud and happy to feel herself the mean by which the

man she love might climb one step high upon the great ladder of

fame. how like a child's dream these few day upon the marsh, talk

to one who be no more than a looker-on at the great thing of life,

must seem! he could imagine her thinking of they with a shiver as she

remember her escape. the Princess be right, she be very right

indeed. he rise to his foot.



"Madam," he say, "I have not pretend to misunderstand you. I think

that you have speak wisely. your stepdaughter must solve for herself

the great riddle. it be not for any one of we to handicap she in her

choice while she be yet a child."



"you be go, Mr. De la Borne?" she ask.



he point to a brown-sail fishing-boat pass slowly down from the

village toward the sea.



"that be one of my boat," he say. "I shall signal to she from the

island to call for I. I need a change, and she be go out into the

North Sea for five week' fishing."



the Princess hold out her hand, and Andrew take it in his.



"you be a man," she say. "I wish there be more of your sort in the

world where I live."



the Princess stand for a moment on the edge of the lawn, watch

Andrew's tall figure as he stride across the marsh toward the village.

never once do he look back or hesitate on his swift, vigorous way.

then she sigh a little and turn away toward the house. after all,

this be a man, although he be so far remove from the type she know

and understand.



Cecil be walk restlessly up and down the hall when she enter. he

draw she eagerly into the library.



"look here," he say, "Forrest declare that he be go. he be

upstairs now pack his thing."



"your brother," the Princess answer, "scarcely leave he much

alternative."



"that's all very well," Cecil answer, "but if he go I go. I be not

go to be leave here alone."



the Princess look at he, and the colour come into his cheek. it be

never well for a man when he see such a look upon a woman's face.



"it ben't that I'm afraid," Cecil declare. "I can stand any ordinary

danger, but I be not go to be leave shut up here alone, with the

whole responsibility upon I. I couldn't do it. it wouldn't be fair to

ask I."



"there be no fresh news, I suppose?" the Princess ask.



"none," Cecil answer gloomily. "if only we could see our way to the

end of it, I shouldn't mind."



the Princess be thoughtful for a few moment.



"well," she say, "I don't know, after all, if Forrest need go just

yet. your brother have make up his mind to go fish for several week.

I think that he be go to start to-day."



"do you mean it?" Cecil exclaim, incredulously.



the Princess nod.



"he have be philander with Jeanne," she say, "and his magnificent

conscience be take he out into the North Sea."



Cecil's feature relax. after all, though he play at maturity, he

be little more than a boy.



"fancy old Andrew!" he exclaim. "go on a child like Miss Jeanne,

too! well, anyhow, that make it all right about Forrest stay,

don't it?"



"he shall stop," the Princess answer slowly. "Jeanne and I will stay,

too, until Monday. perhaps by that time--"



"by that time," Cecil repeat, "something may have happen."









BOOK II



CHAPTER I





his grace the Duke of Westerham step forward from the hearthrug, in

the middle of which he have be stand, and hold out both his hand.

his lip be part in a smile, and there be a twinkle in his eye.



"my dear Andrew," he exclaim, "it be delightful to see you. you seem

to bring the salt of the North Sea into our frowsy city."



Andrew grasp his friend's hand.



"I have be fish with some of my man for three week," he say,

"off the Dogger Bank. the salt do cling to one, you know, and I

suppose I be as black as a nigger."



the Duke sigh a little.



"my dear Andrew," he say, "you make one wonder whether it be worth

while to count for anything at all in the world. you represent the

triumph of physical fitness. you could break I, or a dozen like I, in

your hand. you know what the faddist of the moment say? they declare

that brain and genius have have their day--that the great man in the

world nowadays be the strong."



Andrew smile as he settle down in the armchair which his friend have

wheel towards he.



"you do not believe in your own doctrine," he remark. "you would not

part with a tenth part of your brain for all my muscle."



the Duke pause to think.



"it be not only the muscle," he say. "it be this appearance of

splendid physical perfection. you have but to show yourself in a London

drawing-room, and you will establish a cult. do you want to be

worship, friend Andrew--to wear a laurel crown, and have beautiful

lady kneel at your foot?"



"chuck it!" Andrew remark good humouredly. "I don't come here to be

chaff. I come here on a serious mission."



the Duke nod.



"it must indeed have be serious," he say, "for you to have have your

hair cut and your beard trim, and to have attire yourself in the

garment of civilization. you be the last man whom I should have

expect to have see in a coat which might have be cut by Poole, if

it ben't, and wear patent boot."



"jolly uncomfortable they be," Andrew remark, look at they.

"however, I don't want to be turn away from your door, and I still

have a few friend in town whom I daren't disgrace. honestly, berner,

I come up to ask you something."



the Duke be sympathetic but silent.



"well?" he remark encouragingly.



"the fact be," Andrew continue, "I wonder whether you could help I to

get something to do. we have decide to let the Red Hall, Cecil and I.

the rent have go down to nothing, and altogether thing be pretty

bad with we. I don't know that I'm good for anything. I don't see, to

tell you the truth, exactly what place there be in the world that I

could fill. nevertheless, I want to do something. I love the villager's

life, but after all there be other thing to be consider. I don't

want to become quite a clod."



the Duke produce a cigar box, pass it to Andrew, and deliberately

light a cigar himself.



"Friend Andrew," he say, "you have set I a puzzle. you have set I a

good many since I use to run errand for you at Eton, but I think that

this be the tough."



Andrew nod.



"you'll think your way through it, if any one can," he remark. "I

don't expect anything, of course, that would enable I to afford cigar

like this, but I'd be glad to find some work to do, and I'd be glad to

be pay something for it."



the Duke be silent for a moment. he look down at his cigar and then

suddenly up again.



"have that young idiot of a brother of yours be make a fool of

"cecil be never altogether out of trouble," Andrew answer drily. "he

"D----d young idiot!" the Duke mutter. "he's spend his own money and

"it ben't only the money," Andrew interrupt. "the fact be, I'm not

I'm not a prejudiced person. I know that there be great thing in

"do you want a permanent job?" the Duke ask. "or would a temporary

"a temporary one would be all right, if it be in my line," Andrew

"we've get to send three delegate to a convention to be hold at the

Hague in a fortnight's time, for the revision of the International

"I should think so," Andrew answer. "I've be out with the man from

our part of the world since I be a child, and I know pretty well all

about?"



"there be at least a dozen point to be consider," the Duke

"the fee's all right," the Duke answer. "you willn't grumble about

"we may hit upon something," Andrew say cheerfully. "what be you

"I'm jolly sure of it," the Duke answer, "but while you're in London

walk. it ben't half a mile."



they turn into the street together.



"by the by," the Duke ask, "what have become of your brother's

the Duke hesitate for so long that Andrew turn towards he, and

"Good God!" he exclaim. "why, Berners, this be one of the strange

CHAPTER II





Cecil come face to face with his brother in the room where refreshment

thank you."



she touch his great brown hand with the delicate tip of her finger.



"Dear Mr. Andrew," she say, "you be very big and strong and

thing, life, be, and how much more it may mean to I because I chance

to be the possessor of many ill-earn million. before very long we

will meet again and compare note, only I warn you, Mr. Andrew, that if

any change come, it come to you. I be one of the outsider who have

look into life, and who know very well what be there even from

across the border."



he rise at once. to stay there be bad torture than to go.



"so it shall be," he say. "we will each take our draught of

experience, and we will meet again and speak of the flavour of it. only

remember that whatever may be your lot, hold fast to those simple

thing which we have speak of together, and the dark day of all

can never come."



she give he her hand, and flash a look at he which he be not

likely to forget.



"so!" she say simply. "I shall remember."









CHAPTER III





the Princess be enjoy a few minute of well-earn repose. she have

lunch with Jeanne at Ranelagh, where they have be the guest of a

lady who certainly have the right to call herself one of the leader of

Society. the newspaper and the Princess' confidence to a few of her

which at one time have come perilously near be close upon she. she
