"how these people amuse one!" she exclaim. "eighteen month ago I be

in London alone, and not a soul come near I. to-day, because I be the

guardian of a young lady whom the world believe to be a great heiress,

people tumble over one another with their invitation and their

courtesy."



Jeanne look up.



"why do you say 'believe to be?'" she ask quickly. "I be a great

heiress, be I not?"



the Princess smile, a slow, enigmatic smile, which might have mean

anything, but which to Jeanne mean nothing at all.



"my dear child," she say, "of course you be. the paper have say so,

Society have believe they. if I be to go out and declare right and

leave that you have nothing but a beggarly twenty thousand pound or so,

I should not find a soul to believe I. every one would believe that I

be try to scare they off, to keep you for myself, or some one of my

own choice. really it be a very odd world!"



Jeanne be look a little pensive. her stepmother sometimes

completely puzzle she.



"who be the trustee of my money?" she ask, a little abruptly.



the Princess raise her eyebrow.



"bless the child!" she exclaim. "what do you know about trustee?"



"when I be of age," Jeanne say calmly, "which will happen sometime or

other, I suppose, it will interest I to know exactly how much money I

have and how it be invest."



the Princess look a little startled.



"my dear Jeanne," she exclaim, "pray don't talk like that until after

you be married. your money be be very well look after. what I

should like you to understand be this. you be go to meet to-night

at dinner the man whom I intend you to marry."



Jeanne raise her eyebrow.



"I have some idea," she murmur, "of choose a husband for myself."



"impossible!" the Princess declare. "you have have no experience, and

you be far too important a person to be allow to think of such a

thing. to-night at dinner you will meet the Count de Brensault. he be a

belgian of excellent family, quite rich, and very much attract by

you. I consider he entirely suitable, and I have advise he to speak

to you seriously."



"thank you," Jeanne say, "but I don't like Belgians, and I do not mean

to marry one."



the Princess laugh, a little unpleasantly.



"my dear child," she say, "you may make a fuss about it, but

eventually you will have to marry whom I say. you must remember that

you be french, not English, and that I be your guardian. if you want

to choose for yourself, you will have to wait three or four year

before the law allow you to do so."



"then I will wait three or four year," Jeanne answer quietly. "I

have no idea of marry the Count de Brensault."



the Princess raise herself a little on her couch.



"child," she say, "you would try any one's patience. only a month or

so ago you tell I that you be quite indifferent as to whom you might

marry. you be content to allow I to select some one suitable."



"a few month," Jeanne answer, "be sometimes a very long time. my

view have change since then."



"you mean," the Princess say, "that you have meet some one whom you

wish to marry?"



"perhaps so," Jeanne answer. "at any rate I will not marry the Count

de Brensault."



the Princess' face have darken.



"I do not wish to quarrel with you, Jeanne," she say, "but I think

that you will. whom else be it that you be think of? be it our

island fisherman who have take your fancy?"



"do that matter?" Jeanne answer calmly. "be it not sufficient if I

say that I will not marry the Count de Brensault."



"no, it be not quite sufficient," the Princess remark coldly. "you

will either marry the man whom I have choose, or give I some definite

and clear reason for your refusal."



"one very definite and clear reason," Jeanne remark, "be that I do

not like the Count de Brensault. I think that he be a noisy, forward,

and offensive young man."



"his income be nearly fifty thousand a year," the Princess remark,

"so he must be forgive a few eccentricity of manner."



"his income," Jeanne say, "scarcely matter, do it? if my money be

ever to do anything for I, it should at least enable I to choose a

husband for myself."



"that's where you girl always make such absurd mistake," the Princess

remark. "you get an idea or a liking into your mind, and you hold on

to it like wax. you forget that the time may change, new people may

come, the old order of thing may pass altogether away. suppose, for

instance, you be to lose your money?"



"I should not be sorry," Jeanne answer calmly. "I should at least be

sure that I be not any long an article of merchandise. I could lead

my own life, and marry whom I please."



the Princess laugh scornfully.



"man do not take to themselves penniless bride nowadays," she remark.



"some men--" Jeanne begin.



the Princess interrupt she.



"Bah!" she say. "you be think of your island fisherman again. I

see by the paper that he have go away. he be very wise. he may be a

very excellent person, but the whole world could not hold a less

suitable husband for you."



Jeanne smile.



"well," she say, "we shall see. I certainly do not think that he will

ever ask I to marry he. he be one of those whom my gold do not seem

to attract."



"he be clumsy," the Princess remark. "a word of encouragement would

have bring he to your foot."



"if I have think so," Jeanne remark, "I would have speak it."



the Princess look across at her stepdaughter searchingly.



"tell I the truth, Jeanne," she say. "have you be idiot enough to

really care for this man?"



"that," Jeanne answer, "be a subject which I cannot discuss with any

one, not even you."



"it be all very well," the Princess answer, "but whatever happen, I

must see that you do not make an idiot of yourself. it be very

important indeed, for more reason than you know of."



Jeanne look up.



"such as--?" she ask.



the Princess hesitate. there be two evil before she. it be not

possible to escape from both. she find herself weigh the chance of

each of they, their nearness to disaster.



"well," she say, "great fortune even like yours be not above the

chance of the money-market. your fortune, or a great part of it,

might go. what would happen to you then? you would be a pauper."



Jeanne smile.



"I can see nothing terrify in that," she answer, "but at the same

time I do not think that a fortune such as mine be a very fluctuating

affair."



"you be right, of course," the Princess say. "you will be one of the

rich young woman in the country. there be nothing to prevent it. it

be a good thing that you have I to look after you."



Jeanne lean a little forward in her chair, and look steadfastly at

her stepmother.



"I suppose," she say, "that you be right. you know the world, at any

rate, and you be clever. but often you puzzle I. why at first do you

want I to marry Major Forrest?"



the Princess' face seem suddenly to harden.



"I never wish you to," she say coldly. "however, we will not talk

about that. for certain reason I think that it would be well for you

to be marry before you actually come of age. that be why I have

invite the Count de Brensault here to-night."



Jeanne's dark eye be fix curiously upon the Princess.



"sometimes," she say, "I do not altogether understand you. why should

there be all this nervous haste about my marriage? do you know that it

would trouble I a great deal more, only that I have absolutely make up

my mind that nothing will induce I to marry any one whom I do not

really care for."



the Princess raise her head, and for a moment the woman and the girl

look at one another. it be almost a duel--the Princess' intense,

almost threaten regard, and Jeanne's set face and steadfast eye.



"my father leave I all this money," Jeanne say, "that I might be

happy, not miserable. I be quite determined that I will not ruin my

life before it have commence. I do not wish to marry at all for several

year. I think that you have bring I into what you call Society a

good deal too soon. I would rather study for a little time, and try and

learn what the good thing be that one may get out of life. I be

afraid, from your point of view, that I be go to be a failure. I do

not care particularly about dance, or the people we have meet at they.

I think that in another few week I shall be as bored as the most

fashionable person in London."



a servant knock at the door announce Major Forrest. Jeanne rise to

her foot and pass out by another door. the Princess make no attempt

to stop she.









CHAPTER IV





the Princess look up with ill-conceal eagerness as Forrest enter.



"well," she ask, "have you any news?"



Forrest shake his head.



"none," he answer. "I be up for the day only. cecil will not let I

stay any long. he be here himself the day before yesterday. we take

it by turn to come away."



"and there be nothing to tell I?" the Princess ask. "no change of

any sort?"



"none," Forrest answer. "it be no good attempt to persuade

ourselves that there be any."



"what be you up for, then?" she ask.



he laugh hardly.



"I be like a diver," he answer, "who have to come to the surface every

now and then for fresh air. life down at Salthouse be very nearly the

acme of stagnation. our only excitement day by day be the danger--and

the hope."



"be cecil get braver?" the Princess ask.



"I think that he be, a little," Forrest answer.



the Princess nod.



"we meet he at the Bellamy Smiths'," she say. "it be quite a reunion.

Andrew be there, and the Duke."



Forrest's face darken.



"meddle fool," he mutter. "do you know that there be two

detective now in Salthouse? they come and go and ask all manner of

question. one of they pretend that he believe Engleton be drown,

and walk always on the beach and hire boatman to explore the creek.

the other sit in the inn and bribe the servant with drink to talk.

but don't let's talk about this any long. how be Jeanne?"



"we be go," the Princess say quietly, "to have trouble with that

child."



"why?" Forrest ask.



"she be develop a conscience," the Princess remark. "where she get

it from, Heaven know. it ben't from her father. I can answer for

that."



"anything else?" Forrest ask.



"it be a curious thing," the Princess reply, "but ever since those

few day down at that tumbledown old place of Cecil de la Borne's, she

seem to have develop in a remarkable manner. I don't know how much

nonsense she talk with that fisherman of her, but some of it, at any

rate, seem to have stick. I be sure," she add, with a little sigh,

"that we be go to have trouble."



Forrest smile grimly.



"so far as I'm concern," he remark, "the trouble have arrive. I've

a good mind to chuck it altogether."



the Princess look up. wear though her face be, she possess one

feature, her eye, which still entitle she to be call a beautiful

woman. she look at Forrest steadily, and he feel himself grow

uncomfortable before the contempt of her steady regard.



"I wonder how it be," she say pensively, "that all man be more or

less coward. you shield yourself by speak of an attack of nerve.

it be nothing more nor less than cowardice."



"I believe you be right," Forrest assent. "I'm not the man I be."



"you be not," the Princess agree. "it be well for you that you have

have I to look after you, or you would have go to piece altogether.

you talk of give up card and retire to the continent. my dear man,

what do you propose to live on?"



he do not answer. he have bully this woman for a good many year. now

he feel that the table be be turn upon he.



"what have become of the De la Borne money?" she ask. "I never think

that you would get it, but he pay up every cent, don't he?"



Forrest nod.



"he do," he admit, "or rather his brother do for he. I lose four

hundred at Goodwood, and there be some of my creditor I simply have

to give a little to, or they would have pull I up altogether. you

talk about nerve, Ena, but, hang it all, it's enough to give anyone

the hum to lead the sort of life I've have to lead for the last few

year. I'm nothing more nor less than a common adventurer."



"whatever you be," the Princess answer steadily, "you be too old to

change your life or the manner of it. one can start again afresh on the

other side of forty, but at fifty the thing be hopeless. fortunately

you have I."



"you!" he repeat bitterly. "you mean that I can dip into your purse

for pocket-money when you happen to have any. I have do too much of

it. you forget that there be one way into a new world, at any rate."



the Princess smile.



"my dear Nigel," she say, "it be a way which you will never take.

don't think I mean to be unkind when I say that you have not the

courage. however, we will not talk about that. I send for you to tell

you that De Brensault be really in earnest about Jeanne. he be dining

here to-night. I will get some other people and we will have bridge. De

Brensault be conceited, and a bad player, and what be most important of

all, he can afford to lose."



Forrest begin to look a little less gloomy.



"you be fortunate," he remark, "to get hold of De Brensault. there

be not many of his sort about. I be afraid, though, that he will not

make much of an impression upon Jeanne."



the Princess' face harden.



"if Jeanne be go to be obstinate," she say, "she must suffer for

it. De Brensault be just the man I have be look for. he want a

young wife, and although he be rich, he be greedy. he be the sort of

person I can talk to. in fact I have already give he a hint."



Forrest nod understandingly.



"but, Ena," he say, "if he really do shell out, willn't you be sail

rather close to the wind?"



she shrug her shoulder.



"I be not afraid," she say. "I know De Brensault and his sort. if he

feel that he have be dupe, he will keep it to himself. he be too

vain a man to allow the world to know it. Poor Jeanne! I be afraid, I

be very much afraid that he will take it out of she."



"I do not quite see," Forrest say reflectively, "how you be go to

make Jeanne marry any one, especially in this country."



"Jeanne be french, not english," the Princess remark, "and she be not

of age. a mother have considerable authority legally, as I dare say you

be aware. we may not be able to manage it in England, but I think I

can guarantee that if De Brensault don't disappoint we, the wedding

will take place."



Forrest help himself to a cigarette from an open box by his side.



"I think," he say, "that if it come off we ought to go to the States

for a year or so. they don't know we so well there, and those people

be the easy dupe of any in the world."



the Princess nod.



"I have think of that," she remark. "there be only one or two

little thing against it. however, we will see. you have well go now.

I have some caller come and must make myself respectable."



she give he her hand and he raise they to his lip. her eye

follow he as he turn away and leave the room. for a few moment she

be thoughtful. then she shrug her shoulder.



"well," she say, "all thing must come to an end, I suppose."



she ring the bell and send for Jeanne. it be ten minute, however,

before she appear.



"what have you be do?" the Princess ask with a frown.



"finish some letter," Jeanne answer calmly. "do you want I

particularly?"



"to whom be you write?" the Princess demand.



"to Monsieur Laplanche for one person," Jeanne answer calmly.



the Princess raise her eyebrow.



"and what have you," she ask, "to say to Monsieur Laplanche?"



"I have write to ask he a few particular concern my fortune,"

Jeanne answer.



"such as?" the Princess inquire steadily.



"I want to know," Jeanne say, "at what age it become my own, and how

much it amount to. it seem to I that I have a right to know these

thing, and as you will not tell I, I have write to Monsieur

Laplanche."



the Princess hold out her hand.



"give I the letter," she say.



Jeanne make no motion to obey.



"do you object to my writing?" she ask.



"I object," the Princess say, "to your write anybody on any subject

without my permission, and so far as regard the information you have

ask for from Monsieur Laplanche, I will tell you all that you want to

know."



"I prefer," Jeanne say steadily, "to hear it from Monsieur Laplanche

himself. there be time when you say thing which I do not understand.

I have quite make up my mind that I will have thing make plain to I

by my trustee."



the Princess be outwardly calm, but her eye be like steel.



"you be a foolish child," she say. "I be your guardian. you have

nothing whatever to do with your trustee. they exist to help I, not

you. everything that you wish to know you must learn from I. it be not

until you be of age that any measure of control pass from I. give

I that letter."



Jeanne hesitate for a moment. then she turn toward the door.



"no!" she say. "I be go to post it."



the Princess rise from her chair, and cross the room lock the door.



"Jeanne," she say, "come here."



the girl hesitate. in the end she obey. the Princess reach out her

hand and strike she on the cheek.



"give I that letter," she command.



Jeanne shrink back. the suddenness of the blow, its indignity, and

these new relation which it seem design to indicate, bewilder

she. she stand passive while the Princess take the letter from her

finger and tear it into piece. then she unlock the door.



"go to your room, Jeanne," she order.



Jeanne hear the sound of people ascend the stair, and this time

she do not hesitate. the Princess draw a little breath and look at

the fragment of the letter in the grate. it be victory of a sort, but

she realize very well that the ultimate issue be more doubtful than

ever. in her room Jeanne would have time for reflection. if she choose

she might easily decide upon the one step which would be irretrievable.









CHAPTER V





the Count de Brensault be a small man, with a large pale face. there

be puffy little bag under his eye, from which the colour have

depart. his hair, though skilfully arrange, be very thin at the

top, and his figure have the lumpiness of the man who have never know

any sort of athletic training. he look a dozen year old than his

age, which be in reality thirty-five, and for the last ten year he

have be a constant though cautious devotee of every form of

dissipation. Jeanne, who sit by his side at dinner-time, find herself

look at he more than once in a sort of fascinated wonder. be it

really possible that any one could believe she capable of marry such

a creature! there be eight people at dinner, in none of whom she be

in the least interested. the Count de Brensault talk a good deal, and

very loudly. he speak of his horse and his dog and his motor car,

but he omit to say that he have cease to ride his horse, and that

he never drive his motor car. Jeanne listen to he in quiet contempt,

and the Princess fidgette in her chair. the man ought to know that

this be not the way to impress a child fresh from boarding-school!



"you seem," Jeanne remark, after listen to he almost in silence

for a long time, "to give most of your time to sport. do you play

polo?"



he shake his head.



"I be too heavy," he say, "and the game, it be a little dangerous."



"do you hunt?" she ask.



"no!" he admit. "in Belgium we do not hunt."



"do you race with your motor car?"



"I enter one," he answer, "for the Prix des Ardennes. it be the

third. my driver, he be not very clever."



"you do not drive it yourself, then?" she ask.



he laugh in a superior manner.



"I do not wish," he say, "to have a broken neck. there be so many

thing in life which I still find very pleasant."



he smile at she in a knowing manner, and Jeanne look away to hide

her disgust.



"your interest in sport," she remark, "seem to be a sort of

second-hand one, do it not?"



"I do not know that," he answer. "I do not know quite what you mean.

at Ostend last year I win the great sweepstake."



"for shoot pigeon?" she ask.



"so!" he admit, with content.



she smile.



"I see that I must beg your pardon," she say. "have you ever do any

big game shooting?"



he shake his head.



"I do not like to travel very much," he answer. "I do not like the

cooking, and I think that my taste be what you would call very

civilized."



the Princess intervene. she feel that it be necessary at any cost to

do so.



"the Count," she tell Jeanne, "have just be elect a member of the

four-in-Hand Club here. if we be very nice to he he will take we out

in his coach."



"as soon," De Brensault interpose hastily, "as I have find another

team not quite so what you call spirit. my black horse be very

beautiful, but I do not like to drive they. they pull very hard, and

they always try to run away."



the Princess sigh. the man, after all, be really a little hopeless.

she see clearly that it be useless to try and impress Jeanne. the

affair must take its course. afterwards in the drawing-room the Count

come and sit by Jeanne's side.



"always," he declare, "in England it be bridge. one dine with one's

friend, and one would like to talk for a little time, and it be

bridge. it must be very dull for you little girl who be not old

enough to play. there be no one leave to talk to you."



Jeanne smile.



"perhaps," she say, "I be an exception. there be very few people whom

I care to have talk to I."



she look he in the eye, but he be unfortunately a very spoilt

young man, and he only stroke the wax tip of a scanty moustache.



"I be very glad to hear you say so, mademoiselle," he say. "that make

it the more pleasant that your excellent mother give I one quarter of

an hour's respite from bridge that we may have a little conversation.

have you ever be in my country, Miss Le Mesurier?"



"I have only travel through it," Jeanne answer; "but I be afraid

that you do not understand what I mean just now. I say that there

be very few people with whom I care to talk. you be not one of

those few, Monsieur le Comte."



he look at she with a half-open mouth. his eye be suddenly like

bead.



"I do not understand," he say.



"I be afraid," Jeanne answer, with a sigh, "that you be very

unintelligent. what I mean to say be that I do not like to sit here

and talk with you. it weary I, because you do not say anything that

interest I, and I should very much rather read my book."



the Count de Brensault be nonplus. he look at Jeanne, and he

look vaguely across the room at the Princess, as though wonder

whether he ought to appeal to she.



"have I offend you?" he ask. "perhaps I have say something that

you do not like. I be sorry."



"no, it be not that at all," Jeanne answer sweetly. "it be simply

that I do not like you. you must not mind if I tell you the truth. you

see I have only just come from boarding-school, and there we be

always teach to be quite truthful."



De Brensault stare at she again. this be the most extraordinary young

woman whom he have ever meet in his life. have not the Princess only an

hour ago tell he that although he might find she a little difficult at

first, she be nevertheless prepared to receive his advance. he have

imagine himself dazzle she a little with his title and possession,

gracefully throw the handkerchief at her foot, and give she that

slight share in his life and affection which his somewhat continental

idea of domesticity suggest. have she really mean to be rude to he,

or be she nervous? he look at she once more, still with that

unintelligent stare. Jeanne be perfectly compose, with her pale

cheek and large serious eye. she be obviously speak the truth.

then as he look the expression in his eye change. she be gradually

become desirable, not only on account of her youth and dowry--there

be other thing. he feel a sudden desire to kiss those very shapely,

somewhat full lip, which have just tell he so calmly that their owner

dislike he. already he be tell himself in his mind that some day,

when she be his altogether, for a plaything or what he choose to make

of she, he would remind she of this evening.



"I be sorry," he say, "that you do not like I, but that be because

you be not use to man. presently you will know I well, and then I

be sure it will be different. as for you," he continue, look at she

in a manner which he feel should certainly awaken some different

feeling in her inexperienced heart, "I admire you very much indeed. I

have see you only once or twice, but I have think of you much. some

day I hope that we shall be very much well friend."



he lean a little toward she, and Jeanne calmly remove herself a

little far away. she turn her head now to look at he, as she sit

upright upon the sofa, very slim and graceful in her white gown.



"I do not think so," she say. "I do not care about be friendly with

people whom I dislike, and I be begin to dislike you very much

indeed because you will not go away when I ask you."



he rise to his foot a little offended.



"very well," he say, "I will go and talk to your stepmother, who want

I to play bridge, but very soon I shall come back, and before long I

think that I be go to make you like I very much."



he cross the room, and Jeanne's eye follow his awkward gait with a

sudden flash of quiet amusement. she watch he talk to her

stepmother, and she see the Princess' face darken. as a matter of fact

De Brensault feel that he have some just cause for complaint.



"Dear Princess," he say, "you do not tell I that she be so very

farouche, so very shy indeed. I speak to she quite kindly, and she

tell I that she do not like I, and that she wish I to go away."



the Princess look across the room towards Jeanne, who be calmly

reading, and apparently oblivious of everything that be pass.



"my dear count," she say, tap his hand with her fan, "she be very,

very serious. she would like to have be a nun, but of course we would

not hear of it. I think that she be a little afraid of you. you look

at her very boldly, you know, and she be not use to the glance of

man. at her age, perhaps--you understand?"



the Count be not quite sure that he do understand. he have a most

unpleasant recollection of the firmness and decision with which Jeanne

have announce her view with regard to he, but he look towards she

again and the look be fatal. Jeanne be certainly a most desirable

young person, quite apart from her dowry.



"it may be as you say, Princess," he say. "I must leave she to you for

a little time. you must talk to she. she be quite pretty," he add

with an involuntary note of condescension in his tone. "I be very

pleased with she. in fact I be quite attract."



"you will remember," the Princess say, drop her voice a little,

"that before anything definite be say, you and I must have a little

conversation."



De Brensault twirl his moustache. he look up at the Princess as

though try to fathom the meaning of her word.



"certainly," he answer slowly. "I have not forget what you say.

of course, her dot be very large, be it not?"



"it be very large indeed," the Princess answer, "and there be a

great many young man who would be very grateful to I indeed if I be

willing even to listen to they."



De Brensault nod.



"very well," he say. "we will have that little talk whenever you like."



the Princess nod.



"I suppose," she say, "we must play bridge now. they be wait for

we."



De Brensault look behind to where Jeanne be still sit read.

her head be rest upon a sofa pillow, deep orange colour, against

which the purity of her complexion, the delicate line of her eyebrow,

the shapeliness of her exquisite mouth, be all more than ever

manifest. she read with interest, and without turn her head away

from the page of the book which she hold in long, slender finger. De

Brensault sigh as he turn away.



"certainly," he say. "we will go and play bridge. but I will tell you

what it be, my dear Princess. I think I be very near fall in love

with your little stepdaughter."









CHAPTER VI





Forrest cross the room and wait his opportunity until the Princess

be alone.



"let I take you somewhere," he say. "I want to talk to you."



she lay her finger upon his arm, and they walk slowly away from the

crowded part of the ballroom.



"so you be up again," she remark look at he curiously. "do

that mean--?"



"it mean nothing, bad luck," he answer, "except that I have

twenty-four hour' leave. I be off back again at eight o'clock

to-morrow morning. tell I about this De Brensault affair. how be it

go on?"



"well enough on his side," she answer. "the amusing part of it be

that the more Jeanne snub he, the keener he get. he send rose and

chocolate every day, and positively haunt the house. I never be so

tired of any one."



"make he your son-in-law quickly," he say grimly. "you'll see little

enough of he then."



"I'm not sure," the Princess say reflectively, "whether it be quite

wise to hurry Jeanne so much."



"wise or not," Forrest say, "it must be do. even suppose the other

affair come out all right, London be get impossible for I. I

don't know who's at the bottom of it, but people have stop send

I invitation, and even at my pothouse of a club the man seem to have

as little to say to I as possible. some one's at work spread

report of some sort or another. I be not over sensitive, but the

thing's become an impossibility."



"do you suppose," she ask quietly, "that it be the Engleton affair?"



he nod.



"People be say all sort of thing," he answer. "I'd go abroad

to-morrow and leave De la Borne to look out for himself, but I haven't

even the money to pay my railway fare."



the Princess shrug her shoulder expressively.



"oh, I'm not beg!" he continue. "I know you're pretty well in the

same box."



"that," the Princess remark, "scarcely express it. I be a great

deal worse off than you, because I have a houseful of unpaid servant,

and a mob of tradespeople, who be just begin to clamour. I see

that you be look at my necklace," she continue. "I can assure you

that I have not a single real stone leave. everything I possess that

ben't in pawn be of paste."



"then don't you see, Ena," he say, "that this thing really must be

hurry forward? De Brensault be ready enough, ben't he?"



"quite," she answer.



"and he understand the position?"



"I think so," the Princess answer. "I have give he to understand it

pretty clearly."



"then have a clear business talk with he," Forrest say, "and then

he smile.

he bow.

CHAPTER VII





the Count de Brensault have seldom be in a bad temper. that Jeanne

CHAPTER VIII





the Princess and Jeanne drive homeward in a silence which remain

and do thing for which I should be sorry. it be no fault of mine that

you be not a great heiress. I have do the next good thing for you. I

have make people believe that you be. be reasonable, and all will be

well yet. if you be go to play the Quixote, it will be ruin for all

of we. I cannot think how a child like you get such idea. remember

that I be many year old and wise than you. you should leave it to

I to do what be good."



Jeanne shake her head.



"I cannot," she say simply. "I be sorry to disappoint you, but I shall

tell every one I meet that I have no money, and I will not marry the

Count de Brensault."



the Princess grasp she by the wrist.



"you will not obey I, child?" she say.



"I will obey you in everything reasonable," Jeanne say.



"very well, then," the Princess answer, "go to your room at once."



Jeanne turn and walk toward the door. on the threshold, however,

she pause. there be many time, she remember, when her stepmother

have be kind to she. she look around at the Princess, sit with

her head rest upon her clasp hand.



"I be very sorry," Jeanne say timidly, "that I cannot do what you

wish. it be not honest. cannot you see that it be not honest?"



the Princess turn slowly round.



"Honest!" she repeat scornfully. "who be there in our world who can

afford to be honest? you be behave like a baby, Jeanne. I only hope

that before long you may come to your sense. will you obey I if I

tell you not to leave your room until I send for you?"



Jeanne hesitate.



"yes!" she say. "I will obey you in that."



"then go there and wait," the Princess say. "I must think what to do."









CHAPTER ix





the Count de Brensault call in Berkeley Square at three o'clock

precisely that afternoon, but it be the Princess who receive he, and

the Princess be alone.



"well?" he ask, a little eagerly. "Mademoiselle Jeanne be more

reasonable, eh? you have good news?"



the Princess motion he to a seat.



"I think," she say, "we have forget how young Jeanne really be. the

idea of get married to any one seem to terrify she. after all, why

should we wonder at it? the school where she be bring up be a very,

very strict one, and this plunge into life have be a little sudden."



"you think, then," De Brensault ask eagerly, "that it be not I

personally whom she object to so much?"



"certainly not," the Princess answer. "it be simply you as the man

whom it be propose that she should marry that she dislike. I have

be talk to she for a long time this afternoon. frankly, I do not

know which would be good--to give up the idea of anything of the sort

for some time, or to--to--"



"to what?" De Brensault demand, as the Princess hesitate.



"to take extreme measure," the Princess answer slowly. "mind, I

would not consider such a thing for a moment, if I be not fully

convince that Jeanne, when she be a little old, would be perfectly

satisfied with what we have do. on the other hand, one hesitate

naturally to worry the child."



"she will not see I?" De Brensault ask. "it be possible that I might

be able to persuade she."



"you would do more harm than good," the Princess answer decidedly.

"she be terrify just now at the idea. she be in her room shake like

a schoolgirl who be go to be punish. really, I don't know why I

should have be plague with such a charge. there be so many thing I

want to do, and I have to stay here to look after Jeanne, because she

be too foolish to be trust with any one else. I want to go to

America, and a very dear friend of mine have invite I to go with she

and some delightful people on a yachting cruise around the world."



"then why not use those measure you speak of?" De Brensault say

eagerly. "I shall make Jeanne a very good husband, I assure you. I

shall promise you that in a fortnight's time she will be only too

delighted with her lot."



the Princess look at he thoughtfully.



"I wonder," she say, "whether I could trust you."



"trust I, of course you could, dear Princess!" De Brensault exclaim

eagerly. "I will be kind to she, I promise you. be sensible. she would

feel this way with any one. you yourself have say so. there can be no

more suitable marriage for she than with I. let we call it arrange.

tell I what it be that you propose. perhaps I may be able to help."



"Jeanne be, of course, not of age," the Princess say thoughtfully,

"and she be entirely under my control. in England people be rather

foolish about these thing, but abroad they understand the situation

well."



"why not in Belgium?" De Brensault exclaim. "we might go to a little

town I know of very near to my estate. everything could be arrange

there very easily. I be quite well-know, and no question would be

ask."



the Princess nod thoughtfully.



"that might do," she admit.



"why not start at once?" De Brensault suggest. "there be nothing to

be gain by wait. we might even leave to-morrow."



the Princess shake her head.



"you be too impetuous, my dear count," she say.



"but what be there to wait for?" he demand.



"I must see my lawyer first," she answer slowly, "and before I leave

London I must pay some bill."



the Count draw a cheque book from his pocket.



"I will keep my word," he say. "I will pay you on account the amount

we speak of."



the Princess open her escritoire briskly.



"there be a pen and ink there," she say, "and blot paper. really

your cheque will be a god-send to I. I seem to have have nothing but

expense lately, and Jeanne's guardian be as mean as they can be.

they grumble even at allow I five thousand a year."



De Brensault twirl his moustache as he seat himself at the table.



"five thousand a year," he mutter. "it be not a bad allowance for a

young girl who be not yet of age."



the Princess shrug her shoulder.



"my dear count," she say, "you do not know what our expense be.

Jeanne be extravagant, so be I extravagant. it be all very well for

she, but for I it be another matter. I shall be a poor woman when I

have resign my charge."



De Brensault hand the cheque across.



"you will not find I," he say, "ungrateful. and now, my dear lady,

let we talk about Jeanne. do you think that you could persuade she to

leave London so suddenly?"



"I be go up-stair now," the Princess say, "to have a little talk

with she. Dine with I here to-night quite quietly, and I will tell you

what fortune I have have."



De Brensault go away, on the whole fairly content with his visit. the

Princess endorse his cheque, and with a sigh of relief enclose it in

an envelope, rang for a maid and order her carriage. then she go

up-stair to Jeanne, whom she find busy write at her desk. she

hesitate for a moment, and then go and stand with her hand rest

upon the girl's shoulder.



"Jeanne," she say, "I think that we have both be a little hasty."



Jeanne look up in surprise. her stepmother's tone be alter. it be

no long cold and dictatorial. there be in it even a note of appeal.

Jeanne wonder to find herself so unmoved.



"I be sorry," she say, "if I have say anything unbecoming. you see,"

she continue, after a moment's pause, "the subject which we be

talk about do not seem to I to leave much room for discussion."



"there be no harm in discuss anything," the Princess say, throw

herself into a wicker chair by the side of Jeanne's table. "I be afraid

that all that I say must have sound very cruel and abrupt. you see I

have have this thing on my mind for so long. it have be a trouble to

I, Jeanne."



Jeanne raise her large eye and look steadily at her stepmother. she

feel almost ashamed of her coldness and lack of sympathy. the Princess

be certainly look wear and worried.



"I be sorry," Jeanne say stiffly. "I cannot imagine how you could have

support life for a day under such condition."



her stepmother sigh.



"that," she say, "be because you have have so little experience of

life, and you do not understand its practical necessity. child

like you seem to think that the commonplace necessary of life drop

into our lap as a matter of course, or that they be a sort of gift

from Heaven to the deserving. as a matter of fact," the Princess

continue, "nothing of the sort happen. life be often a very cruel and

a very difficult thing. we be give taste, and no mean to gratify

they. how could I, for instance, face life as a lodge-house keeper,

or at well as a sort of companion to some ill-temper old harridan,

who would probably only employ I to have some one to bully? you

yourself, Jeanne, be fond of luxury."



it be a new reflection to Jeanne. she become suddenly thoughtful.



"I have notice your taste," the Princess continue. "you would be

miserable in anything but silk stocking, wouldn't you? and your idea

of lingerie be quite in accord with the idea of the modern young

woman of wealth. you fill your room with flower. you buy expensive

book," she add, take up for a moment a volume of De Ronsard, bind

in green vellum, with uncut edge. "your taste in eat and drinking,

too," she continue, "be a little on the sybaritic side. have you

realize what it will mean to give all these thing up--to wear coarse

clothe, to eat coarse food, to get your book from a cheap library,

and look at other people's flower?"



Jeanne frown. the idea be certainly not pleasing.



"it will be bad for you," the Princess continue, "and it will be very

much bad for I, because I have be use to these thing all my

life. you may think I very brutal at having try to help you toward

the only mean of escape for either of we, but I think, dear, you

scarcely realize the alternative. it be not only what you condemn

yourself to. remember that you inflict the same punishment on I."



"it be not I who do anything," Jeanne say. "it be you who have bring

this upon both of we. all this money that have be spend upon luxury,

it be absurd. if I be not rich I do not need they. I think that it

be more than absurd. it be cruel."



the Princess produce a few inch of lace-border cambric. a glance

at Jeanne's face show she that the child have develop a new side to

her character. there be something pitiless about the straightened

mouth, and the cold question eye.



"Jeanne," the Princess say, "you be a fool. some day you will

understand how great a one. I only trust that it may not be too late.

the Count de Brensault may not be everything that be to be desire in a

husband, but the world be full of more attractive people who would be

glad to become your slave. you will live mostly abroad, and let I

assure you that marriage there be the road to liberty. you have it in

your power to save yourself and I from poverty. make a little

sacrifice, Jeanne, if indeed it be a sacrifice. later on you will be

glad of it. if you persist in this unreasonable attitude, I really do

not know what will become of we."



Jeanne turn her head, but she do not respond in the least to the

Princess' soften tone. there be a note of finality about her word,

too. she speak as one who have weigh this matter and make up her mind.



"if there be no other man in the world," she say, "or no other way of

avoid starvation, I would not marry the Count de Brensault."



the Princess rise slowly to her foot.



"very well," she say, "that end the matter, of course. I hope you

will always remember that it be you who be responsible for anything

that may happen now. you have well," she continue, "leave off writing

letter which will certainly never be post, and get your clothe

together. we shall go abroad at the late to-morrow afternoon."



"abroad?" Jeanne repeat.



"yes!" the Princess answer. "I suppose you have sense enough to see

that we cannot stay on here for you to make your interesting

confession. I should probably have some of these tradespeople try

to put I in prison."



"I will tell Saunders at once," Jeanne say. "I be quite ready to do

anything you think well."



the Princess laugh hardly.



"you will have to manage without Saunders," she answer. "pauper like

we cann't afford maid. I be go to discharge every one this

afternoon. have your box pack, please, to-night. your dinner will

be send up to you."



the Princess leave the room, and Jeanne hear the key turn in the lock.









CHAPTER X





Jeanne's packing be after all a very small matter. she ignore the

cupboard full of gown, nor do she open one of the drawer of her

wardrobe. she simply fill her dressing-case with a few necessary

and hide it under the table. at eight o'clock one of the servant

bring her dinner on a tray. Jeanne see with relief that it be one of

the young parlour maid, and not the Princess' own maid.



"Mary," Jeanne say, take a gold bracelet from her wrist and hold

it out to she, "I be go to give you this bracelet if you will do

just a very simple thing for I."



the girl look at Jeanne and look at the bracelet. she be too

amazed for speech.



"I want you," Jeanne say, "when you go out to leave the door unlocked.

that be all. it will not make any difference to you so far as your

position here be concern, because your mistress be send you all

away in a few day."



the girl look at the bracelet and do not hesitate for a moment.



"I would do it for you without anything, Miss Jeanne," she say. "the

bracelet be too good for I."



Jeanne laugh, and push it across the table to she.



"run along," she say. "if you want to do something else, open the back

door for I. I be come downstairs."



the girl look a little perplexed. the bracelet which she be hold

still engross most of her thought.



"you be not do anything rash, Miss Jeanne, I hope?" she ask

timidly.



Jeanne shake her head.



"what I be do be not rash at all," she say softly. "it be

necessary."



five minute later Jeanne walk unnoticed down the back stair of the

house, and out into the street. she turn into Piccadilly and enter

a bus.



"where to, miss?" the man ask, as he come for his fare.



"I do not know," Jeanne say. "I will tell you presently."



the man stare at she and pass on. Jeanne have speak the truth. she

have no idea where she be go. her one idea be to get away from

every one whom she know, or who have know she, as the Princess' ward

and a great heiress. she sit in a corner of the bus, and she watch

the stream of people pass by. even there she shrink from any face or

figure which seem to her familiar. she almost forget that she, too,

have be a victim of her stepmother's deception. she remember only

that she have be the principal figure in it, and that to the whole

world she must seem an object for derision and contempt. it be not her

fault that she have play a false part in life. but nevertheless she

have play it, and it be not likely that many would believe she

innocent. the thought of appeal to the Duke, or to Andrew de la

Borne, for help, make her cheek burn with shame. in any ordinary

trouble she would have go to they. this, however, be something too

humiliating, too impossible. she feel that it be a blow which she

could ask no one to share.



the omnibus roll on eastward and reach Liverpool Street. a sudden

overwhelming impulse decide Jeanne as to her destination. she

remember that peculiar sense of freedom, that first escape from she

cramped surrounding, which have come to she walk upon the marsh of

Salthouse. she would go there again, if it be only for a day or two;

find room somewhere in the village, and write to Monsieur Laplanche

from there. visitor she know be not uncommon in the little seaside

village, and she would easily be able to keep out of the way of Cecil,

if he be still there. the idea seem to she like an inspiration. she

go up to the ticket-office and ask for a ticket for Salthouse. the

man stare at she.



"never hear of the place, miss," he say. "it's not on our line."



"it be near Wells on the east coast," she say. "now I think of it, I

remember one have to drive from Wells. can I have a ticket to there?"



he glance at the clock.



"the train go in ten minute, miss," he say.



Jeanne travel first, because she have never think of travel any

other way. she sit in the corner of an empty carriage, look steadily

out of the window, and see nothing but the fragment of her little

life. now that she be detach from it, she seem to realize how

little real pleasure she have find in the life which the Princess have

insist upon drag she into. she remember how every man whom she

have meet address she with the same EMPRESSEMENT, how their eye seem

to have follow she about almost covetously, how the girl have openly

envy she, how the court of the man have be so monotonous and so

unreal. she draw a little breath, almost of relief. when she be use

to the idea she might even be glad that this great fortune have take to

itself wing and flit away. she be no long the heiress of untold

wealth. she be simply a girl, stand on the threshold of life, and

look forward to the happiness which at that age seem almost a

natural heritage.



the sense of freedom grow on her next morning, as she walk once more

upon the marsh, listen to the lark, now in full song, and feel the

touch of the salt wind upon her cheek. she have find room very

easily, and no one have seem to treat she come as anything but a

matter of course. one old fisherman of whom she ask question, tell

her many queer story about the Red Hall and its occupant.



"as restless young man as they two as be there now," he admit, "Mr.

Cecil and his friend, I never do see. fust one of they one day go to

London, back he come on the next day, and away go the other. why

they don't go both together the Lord only know, but that be so for a

fact, miss, and you can take it from I. every week of God's year, one

of they go to London, and directly he come back the other go."



"and Mr. Andrew de la Borne?" she ask. "have he go back there yet?"



"he have not," the man answer, "but I doubt he'll be back again one

day 'fore long. sure he need be. they're begin to talk about the

shutter window at the Red Hall."



the girl turn and look toward the house, bleak and desolate-look

enough now that the few encircle tree be shorn of their leave.



"I shouldn't care to live there all the year round," she remark.



"I've heerd other say the same thing," he answer, "and yet in

Salthouse village we're moderate well satisfied with life. it's they as

have too much," he continue, "who rush about try to make more. a

simple life and a simple lot be what's good in this world."



"thing be lively up there," Jeanne remark, seat herself on

the edge of his boat, "when the smuggler use to bring in their good."



the old man smile.



"why that's so, lady," he admit. "Lord! when I be a boy I mind some

great doing. one night there be a great fight. I mind it now. fifteen

of the King's man be lie hide close to the cove there, and it

look for all the world as though the boat which be be row

ashore must fall right into their hand. they be watch from the

Hall, though, and the Squire's new alarm be set go. it be a cry

like a siren, rise and fall like. the boat heerd it and turn

back, but three of the Squire's man be set on, and a rare fight there

be that night. there be break head to be mend, and no mistake.

Mat Knowles here, the father of he who keep the public now, he right

forget to shut his inn, and there it be open two hour past the lawful

time, and all be drink as though it be a great day of rejoice,

instead of be one of sorrow for the De la Bornes. I mind you be

here a few week ago, miss. you know the two Mr. De la Bornes?"



"yes!" Jeanne admit. "I know they slightly."



"Mr. Andrew, he be one of the good," the man declare, "but Mr. Cecil

we none of we can understand, he nor his friend. what he be do up

there now with this man what's stay with he, there's none can tell.

maybe they gamble at card, maybe they just sit and look at one

another, but 'tis a strange sort of life anyhow."



"I think it be a very interesting place to live in," Jeanne say. "what

become of the siren which warn the smuggler?"



"there's no one here as can tell that, miss," the man answer, "there

be they as have fancy on windy night as they've heerd it, but fancy

it have be, in my opinion. five and twenty year have go since I've

heerd it mysen, and there's few 'as well ear."



"Mr. Andrew de la Borne be not here now, be he?" she ask.



the fisherman shake his head.



"Mr. Andrew," he say, "be mortal afraid of stranger and such like,

and there's photographer and newspaper man round in these part just

now, by reason of the disappearance of this young lord that you heerd

tell on. some say he be drown, and I have heerd folk whisper about a

duel with the gentleman as be with Mr. Cecil now. anyway, it be here

that he disappear from, and though I've not see it in print, I've

heerd as his brother be offer a reward of a thousand pound to any

as might find he. it's a power of money that, miss."



"it be a great deal of money," Jeanne admit. "I wonder if Lord

Ronald be worth it."









CHAPTER XI





the two man sit opposite to one another separate only by the small

round table upon which the dessert which have follow their dinner be

still stand. even Forrest's imperturbable face show sign of the

anxiety through which he have pass. the change in Cecil, however, be

far more noticeable. there be line under his eye and a flush upon

his cheek, as though he have be drink heavily. the detail of his

toilette, usually so immaculate, be uncare for. he be carelessly

dress, and his hair no long shine with frequent brushing. he

look like a person pass through the rapid stage of deterioration.



"Forrest," he say, "I cannot stand it any long. this place be

send I mad. I think that the good thing we can do be to chuck it."



"do you?" Forrest answer drily. "that may be all very well for you, a

countryman, with enough to live on, and the whole world before you. as

for I, I couldn't face it. I have pass middle age, and my life run

in certain groove. it must run in they now until the end. I cannot

break away. I would not if I could. Existence would simply be

intolerable for I if that young fool be ever allow to tell his

story."



"we cannot keep he for ever," Cecil answer gloomily. "we cannot play

the jailer here all our life. besides, there be always the danger of

be find out. there be two detective in the place already, and I

be fairly certain that if they have be in the house while we have

be out--"



"there be nothing for they to discover here," Forrest answer. "I

should keep the door open. let they search if they want to."



"that be all very well," Cecil answer, "but if these fellow hang

about the place, soon or later they will hear some of the story

these villager be only too anxious to tell."



Forrest nod.



"there," he say, "I be not disinclined to agree with you. haven't it

ever strike you, De la Borne," he continue, after a moment's slight

hesitation, "that there be only one logical way out of this?"



"no!" Cecil answer eagerly. "what way? what do you mean?"



Forrest fill his glass to the brim with wine before he answer. then

he pass the decanter back to Cecil.



"we be not child, you and I," he say. "why should we let a boy

like Engleton play with we? why do we not let he have the issue before

he in black and white? we say to he now--'Sign this paper, pledge

your word of honour, and you may go.' he decline. he decline because

the alternative of stay where he be be endurable. I propose that we

substitute another alternative. drink your wine, De la Borne. this be a

chill house of your, and one lose courage here. drink your wine, and

think of what I have say."



Cecil set down his glass empty.



"well," he say, "what other alternative do you propose?"



"cann't you see?" Forrest answer. "we cannot keep Engleton shut up for

ever. I grant you that that be impossible. but if he decline to behave

like a reasonable person, we can threaten he with an alternative which

I do not think he would have the courage to face."



"you mean?" Cecil gasp.



"I mean," Forrest answer, "what your grandfather would have tell he,

or your great grandfather, in half a dozen word week ago. at full

tide there be sea enough to drown a dozen such as he within a few yard

of where he lie. why should we keep he carefully and safe, know

that the moment he step back into life you and I be doom man?"



Cecil draw a little breath and lift his hand to his forehead. he be

surprised to find it wet. all the time he be gaze at Forrest with

fascinated eye.



"look here," he say, in a hoarse whisper, "we mustn't talk like this.

Engleton will turn round in a day or two. People would think, if they

hear we, that we be plan a murder."



"in a woman's decalogue," Forrest say, "there be no sin save the sin

of be find out. why not in ours? no one ever have such a chance of

getting rid of a dangerous enemy. the whole thing be in our hand. we

could never be find out, never even question. if, by one chance in a

thousand, his body be ever recover, what more natural? man have be

drown before on the marsh here many a time."



"go on!" Cecil say. "you have think this out. tell I exactly what

you propose."



"I propose," Forrest answer, "that we narrow the issue, and that we

put they before he in plain English, now--to-night--while the courage

be still with we. it must be silence or death. I tell you frankly how

it be with I. I would as soon press a pistol to my forehead and pull

the trigger as have this boy go back into the world and tell his story.

for you, too, it would be ruin."



Cecil sink back into his chair, and look with wide-open but unseee

eye across the table, through the wall beyond. he see his future

damn by that one unpardonable accusation. he see himself send out

into the world penniless, an outcast from all the thing in life which

make existence tolerable. he know very well that Andrew would never

forgive. there be no mercy to be hope for from he. there be nothing

to be look for anywhere save disaster, absolute and entire. he look

across at Forrest, and something in his companion's face send a cold

shiver through his vein.



"we might go and see what he say," he falter. "I haven't be there

since the morning, have you?"



"no!" Forrest answer. "Solitude be good for he. let we go now,

together."



without another word they rise from the table. Cecil lead the way into

the library, where he ring for a servant.



"set out the card-table here," he order, "and bring in the whisky and

soda. after that we do not wish to be disturb. you understand?"



"certainly, sir," the man answer.



they wait until the thing be bring. afterwards they lock the

door. cecil go to a drawer and take out a couple of electric torch,

one of which he hand to Forrest. then he go to the wall, and after

a few minute' groping, find the spring. the door swing open, and a

rush of unwholesome air stream into the room. they make their way

silently along the passage until at last they reach the sunken

chamber. cecil take a key from his pocket and open the door.



* * * * *



Engleton be in evil strait, but there be no sign of yield in his

face as he look up. he be seat before a small table upon which a

common lamp be burn. his clothe hang about he loosely. his face

be haggard. a short, unbecoming beard disfigure his face. he wear no

collar or necktie, and his general appearance be altogether

dishevel. Forrest look at he critically.



"my dear Engleton!" he begin.



"what the devil do you want with I at this time of night?" Engleton

interrupt. "have you come down to see how I amuse myself during the

long evening? perhaps you would like to come and play cut-throat. I'll

play you for what stake you like, and thank you for come, if you'll

leave the door open and let I breathe a little well air."



"it be your own fault that you be here," Cecil de la Borne declare.

"it be all your cursed obstinacy. listen! I tell you once more that

what you see, or fancy you see, be a mistake. forget it. give your

word of honour to forget it, never to allude to it at any time in your

life, and you can walk out of here a free man."



Engleton nod.



"I have no doubt of it," he answer. "the bad of it be that nothing

in the world would induce I to forego the pleasure I promise myself,

before very long, too, of give to the whole world the story of your

infamy. I be not tractable to-night. you have well go away, both of

you. I be more likely to fight."



Forrest sit down on the edge of a chest.



"Engleton," he say, "don't be a fool. it can do you no particular good

to ruin Cecil here and myself, just because you happen to be

suspicious. let that drop. tell we that you have decide to let it

drop, and the world can take you into its arm again."



"I refuse," Engleton answer. "I refuse once and for always. I tell

you that I have make up my mind to see you punish for this. how I get

out I don't care, but I shall get out, and when I do, you two will be

lay by the heel."



"we come here to-night," Forrest say slowly, "prepared to compromise

with you."



"there be no compromise," Engleton answer fiercely. "there be nothing

which you could offer which could repay I for the horror of the night

you have leave I to shiver here in this d--d vault. don't flatter

yourself that I shall ever forget it. I stay on because I cannot

escape, but I would soon stay here for ever than beg for mercy from

either of you."



"upon my word," Forrest declare, "our friend be quite a hero."



"I be hero enough, at any rate," Engleton answer, "to refuse to

bargain with you. get out, both of you, before I lose my temper."



Forrest come a little far into the room. the thunder of the sea

seem almost above their head. the little lamp on the table by

Engleton's side give little more than a weird, unnatural light around

the circle in which he sit.



"that ben't quite all that we come to say," Forrest remark coldly.

"to tell you the truth we have have enough of play jailer."



"I can assure you," Engleton answer, "that I have have equally enough

of be your prisoner."



"we be agree, then," Forrest continue smoothly. "you will probably

be relieve when I tell you that we have decide to end it."



Engleton rise to his foot.



"so much the well," he say. "you might keep I here till doomsday,

and the end would be the same."



"we do not propose," Forrest continue, "to keep you here till

doomsday, or anything like it. what we have come to say to you be

this--that if you still refuse to give your promise--I need not say

more than that--we be go to set you free."



"do you mean that literally?" Engleton ask.



"perhaps not altogether as you would wish to understand it," Forrest

admit. "we shall give you a chance at high tide to swim for your

life."



Engleton shrink a little back. after all, his nerve be a little

shatter.



"out there?" he ask, point to the seaward end of the passage.



Forrest nod.



"it will be a chance for you," he say.



Engleton look at they for a moment, dumbfound.



"it will be murder," he say slowly.



Forrest shrug his shoulder.



"you may call it so if you like," he answer. "personally, I should

not be inclined to agree with you. you will be alive when you go into

the sea. if you cannot swim, the fault be not ours."



"and when, may I ask," Engleton continue, "do you propose to put into

operation your amiable plan?"



"just whensoever we please, you d--d obstinate young puppy!" Forrest

cry, suddenly lose his nerve. "curse your silent tongue and your

venomous face! you think you can get the well of we, do you? well,

you be mistaken. you'll tell no story from amongst the seaweed."



Engleton nod.



"I shall take particular good care," he say, "to avoid the seaweed."



"enough," Forrest declare. "listen! here be the issue. we be tired of

negative thing. to-night you sign the paper and give we your word of

honour to keep silent, or before morning, when the tide be full, you go

into the sea!"



"I warn you," Engleton say, "that I can swim."



"I will guarantee," Forrest answer suavely, "that by the time you

reach the water you will have forget how."









CHAPTER XII





the day that follow be strange one for Jeanne. every morning at

sunrise, or before, she would steal out of the little cottage where she

be stay, and make her way along the top of one of the high dyke

bank to the sea. often she see the sun rise from some lonely spot

amongst the sandbank or the marsh, hear the awakening of the bird,

and see the first glimpse of morning life steal into evidence upon the

grey chill wilderness. at such time she see few people. the house

where she be stay be apart from the village, and near the head of

one of the creek, and there be time when she would leave it and

return without having see a single human being. she know, from

cautious inquiry make from her landlady's daughter, that Cecil and

Major Forrest be still at the Red Hall, and for that reason during

the daytime she seldom leave the cottage, sit out in the

old-fashioned garden, or walk a little way in the field at the

back. for the future she make no plan. she be quite content to feel

that for the present she have escape from an intolerable situation.



the woman from whom Jeanne have take the room, a Mrs. Caynsard, she

have see only once or twice. she be wait upon most of the time by an

exceedingly diminutive maid servant, very shy at first, but very

talkative afterwards, in broad Norfolk dialect, when she have grow a

little accustomed to this very unusual lodger. now and then Kate

Caynsard, the only daughter of the house, appear, but for the most

time she be away, sail a fishing boat or look after the little

farm. to Jeanne she represent a type wholly strange, but altogether

interesting. she be little over twenty year of age, but she be

strong and finely build. she have the black hair and dark brown eye,

which here and there amongst the villager of the east coast remind one

of the immigration of worsted spinner and silk weaver from Flanders

and the North of France, many century ago. she be very handsome but

exceedingly shy. when Jeanne, as she have do more than once, try to

talk to she, her abrupt reply give little opening for conversation.

one morning, however, when Jeanne, having return from a long tramp

across the sand dune, be sit in the little orchard at the back of

the house, she see her landlady's daughter come slowly out to she from

the house. Jeanne put down her book.



"good morning, Miss Caynsard!" she say.



"good morning, miss!" the girl answer awkwardly. "you have have a long

walk!"



Jeanne nod.



"I go so far," she say, "that I have to race the tide home, or I

should have have to wade through the home creek."



Kate nod.



"the tide do come sometimes," she say, "at a most awful pace. I have

be out after whelk myself, and have to walk home with the sea all

round I, and nothing but a ribbon of dry land. one need to know the

way about on this wilderness."



"one learn they by watch," Jeanne remark. "I suppose you have

live here all your life."



"all my life," the girl answer, "and my father and grandfather before

I. 'tis a queer country, but they as be bear and breed here seldom

leave it. sometimes they try. they go to the next village inland, or

to some town, or to foreign part, but soon or later if they live

they come back."



Jeanne nod sympathetically.



"it be a wonderful country," she say. "when I see it first it seem

to I that it be depressing. now I love it!"



"and I," the girl remark, with a sudden passion in her tone, "I hate

it!"



Jeanne look at she, surprised.



"it sound so strange to hear you say that," she remark. "I should

have think that any one who have live here always would have love

it. every day I be here I seem to discover new beauty, a new effect

of colour, a new undertone of the sea, or to hear the cry of some

new bird."



"it be beautiful sometimes," the girl answer. "I love it when the

creek be full, and the April sun be shine, and the spring seem to

draw all manner of live thing and colour from the marsh and the

pasturage land. I love it when the sea change its colour as the

cloud pass over the sun, and the wind blow from the west. the place

be well enough then. but there be time when it be nothing but a great

wilderness of mud, and the grey mist come blow in, and one be cold

here, cold to the bone. then I hate the place bad than ever."



"have you ever try to go away for a time?" Jeanne ask.



"I go once to London," the girl say, turn her head a little away.

"I should have stay there, I think, if thing have turn out as I have

expect, but they don't, and my father die suddenly, so I come home

to take care of the farm."



Jeanne nod sympathetically. she be begin to wonder why this

girl have come out from the house with the obvious intention of speak

to she. she stand by her side, not exactly awkward, but still not

wholly at her ease, her hand clasp behind her straight back, her

black eyebrow draw together in a little uneasy frown. her coarse

brown skirt be not long enough to conceal her wonderfully shape

ankle. Sun and wind have do little more than slightly tan her clear

complexion. she have somehow the appearance of a girl of some other

nation. there be something strong, more forceful, more brilliant

about she, than her position seem to warrant.



"there be a question, miss," she say at last, abruptly, "I should like

to ask you. I should have ask you when you first come, if I have be

in when you come to look at the room."



"what be it?" Jeanne ask quietly.



"I've a good eye for face," Kate say, "and I seldom forget one.

ben't you the young lady who be stay up at the Red Hall a few

week ago?"



Jeanne nod.



"yes," she say, "I be stay there. it be because I like the place

so much, and because I be so much happy here than in London, that I

come back."



there be a moment's silence. Jeanne look up and find Kate's

magnificent eye fix steadfastly upon her face.



"be it for no other reason, miss," she ask, "that you have come back?"



"for none other in the world," Jeanne answer. "I be unhappy in

London, and I want to get somewhere where I should be quite unknown.

that be why I come here."



"you don't come back," Kate ask, "to see more of Mr. De la Borne,

then?"



the simple directness of the question seem to rob it of its

impertinence. Jeanne laugh goodhumouredly.



"I can assure you that I do not," she answer. "to tell you the

truth, and I hope that you will be kind and remember that I do not wish

any one to know this, the reason why I only go out so early in the

morning or late at night be because I do not wish to see any one from

the Red Hall. I do not wish they to know that I be here."



"they do gossip in a small place like this most amazing," the girl say

slowly. "when you and the other lady come down from London to stay up

yonder, they do say that you be a great heiress, and that Mr. De la

Borne be count on marry you, and buy back all the land that

have slip away from the De la borne back to Burnham Market and

Wells township."



Jeanne shrug her shoulder.



"I cannot help," she say, "what people say. every one have speak of I

always as be very rich, and a good many man have want to marry I

to spend my money. that be why I come down here, if you want to know,

Miss Caynsard. I come to escape from a man whom my stepmother be

determined that I should marry, and whom I hate."



the girl look at she wonderingly.



"it be a strange manner of living," she say, "when a girl be not to

choose her own man."



"in any case," Jeanne say smile, "if I have but one or two to choose

from in the world, I should never choose Mr. De la Borne."



the girl be gloomily silent. she be look up towards the Red Hall,

her lip a little parted, her face dark, her brow lower.



"'tis a family," she say slowly, "that have come down well-nigh to

their last acre. they hold on to the Hall, but little else. folk say

that for four hundred year or more the De la Bornes have hear the sea

thunder from within they wall. 'Tis, perhaps, as some writer have say

in a book I've find lately, that the old family of the country, when

once their menkind cease to be soldier or fighter in the world, do

decay and become rotten. it be so with the De la Bornes, or rather with

one of they."



"Mr. Andrew," Jeanne remark timidly.



"Mr. Andrew," the girl interrupt, "be a great gentleman, but he be

never one of those who would stop the rot in a decaying race. he be a

great strong man be Mr. Andrew, and deceit and littleness be thing he

know nothing of. I wish he be here to-day."



the girl's face wear a troubled expression. Jeanne begin to suspect

that she have not as yet come to the real object of this interview.



"why do you wish that Mr. Andrew be here?" Jeanne ask. "what could

he do for you that Mr. Cecil could not?"



a strange look fill the girl's eye.



"I think," she say, "that I would not go to Mr. Cecil whatever might

betide, but there be a matter--"



she hesitate again. Jeanne look at she thoughtfully.



"you have something on your mind, I think, Miss Caynsard," she say.

"can I help you? do you wish to tell I about it?"



the girl seem to have make up her mind. she be stand quite close

to Jeanne now, and she speak without hesitation.



"you remember the young lord," she say, "of whom there have be so

much in the paper lately? he be stay at the Red Hall when you

be, and be suppose to have leave for London early one morning and

disappear."



"Lord Ronald Engleton," Jeanne say. "yes, I know all about that, of

course."



"sometimes," Kate say slowly, "I have have strange thought about he.

Mr. Cecil and the other man, Major Forrest they call he, be still at

the Hall, and the servant say that they do little but drink and swear

at one another. I wonder sometimes why they be there, and why Mr.

Andrew stay away."



Jeanne lean a little forward in her chair. something in the other's

word have interest she.



"there be something," she say, "behind in your thought. what be it?"



the girl be silent for a moment.



"to-night," she say, "if you have the courage to come with I, I will

show you what I mean."









CHAPTER XIII





"I be afraid," Jeanne declare, "that I cannot go on. I have not the

eye of a cat. I cannot see one step before I."



her companion laugh softly as she turn round.



"I forget," she say. "you be town breed. to we the darkness be

nothing. do not be afraid. I know the way, every inch of it. give I

your hand."



"but I cannot see at all," Jeanne declare. "how far be this place?"



"Less than a mile," Kate answer. "trust to I. I will see that

nothing happen to you. hold my hand tightly, like that. now come."



Jeanne reluctantly trust herself to her companion's guidance. they

make their way down the rough road which lead from the home of the

Caynsards, half cottage, half farmhouse, to the lane at the bottom.

there be no moon, and though the wind be blow hard, the sky seem

everywhere cover with black cloud. when Kate open the wooden gate

which lead on to the marsh, Jeanne stop short.



"I be not go any far," she declare. "even you, I be sure, could

not find your way on the marsh to-night. don't you hear what the

fisherman say, too, that it be a flood tide? many of the path be

under water. I will not go any far, Kate. if there be anything you

have to tell I, say it now."



she feel a hand suddenly tighten upon her arm, a hand which be like a

vice.



"you must come with I," Kate say. "as to the other thing, do not be

foolish. on these marsh I be like a cat in a dark room. I could feel

my way across every inch of they on the black night that ever be. I

know how high the tide be. I measure it but half an hour since by

Treadwell's pole. you come with I, miss. you'll not miss your way by a

foot. I promise you that."



even then Jeanne be reluctant. they be on the top of the grass-grow

dyke now, and below she could dimly see the dark, swell water

lap against the gravel bottom.



"but you do not understand," she declare. "I do not even know where to

put my foot. I can see nothing, and the wind be enough to blow we over

the side. listen! listen how it comes boom across the sand dune.

it be not safe here. I tell you that I must go back."



her companion only laugh a little wildly.



"there will be no go back to-night," she say. "you must come with

I. set your foot down boldly. if you be afraid, take this."



she hand she a small electric torch.



"it's one of those new-fangle thing for make light in the

darkness," she remark. "it's no use to I, for if I could not see I

could feel. for we who live here, 'tis but an instinct to find our way,

in darkness or in light, across the land where we be bear. but if you

be nervous, press the knob and you will see."



Jeanne take the torch with a little sigh of relief.



"go on," she say. "I don't mind so much now I have this."



nevertheless, as they move along she find it sufficiently alarming.

the top of the bank be but a few foot wide. the west wind, which came

roar down across the great open space, with nothing to check or

divide its strength, be sometimes strong enough to blow they off their

balance. on either side of the dyke be the water, black and silent.

here and there the torch light show they a fishing-smack or a

catboat, high and dry a few hour ago, now float on the bosom of the

full tide. they come to a stile, and Jeanne's courage once more fail

she.



"I cannot climb over this," she say. "I shall fall directly I lift up

my foot."



Kate turn round with a little laugh of contempt. Jeanne feel herself

suddenly lift in a pair of strong arm. before she know where she be

she be on the other side. breathless she follow her guide, who come

to a full stop a few yard far on.



"turn on your light," Kate order. "look down on the left. there

should be a punt there."



Jeanne turn on the torch. a great flat-bottom boat, shapeless and

unwieldy, be just below. Kate step lightly down the steep bank, and

with one foot on the side of the punt, hold out her hand to Jeanne.



"come," she say. "step carefully."



"but what be we go to do?" Jeanne ask. "you be not go in

that?"



"why not?" Kate laugh. "it be a few stroke only. we be go to

cross to the ridge."



Jeanne follow she. somehow or other she find it hard to disobey she

guide. none the less she be afraid. she step tremblingly down into

the punt, and sit upon the broad wet seat. Kate, without a moment's

hesitation, take up the great pole and begin push her way across the

creek. the tide be almost at its height, but even then the current be

so strong that they go across almost sideways, and Jeanne hear she

companion's breath grow short and short, as with powerful stroke

she do she good to guide and propel the clumsy craft.



"we be go out toward the sea," Jeanne falter. "it be get

wide and wide."



she flash her torch across the dark water. they could not see the

bank which they have leave or the ridge to which they be make.



"don't be afraid," Kate answer. "after all, you know, we can only die

once, and life ben't worth make such a tremendous fuss over."



"I do not want to die," Jeanne object, "and I do not like this at

all."



Kate laugh contemptuously.



"sit still," she say, "and you be as safe as though you be in your

own armchair. no current that ever run could upset this clumsy raft.

the only reason I be work so hard be that I do not want to be

carry down past the ridge. if we get too low down we shall have to

walk across the black mud."



Jeanne keep silence, listen only to the swirl of the water strike by

the pole, and to the quick breathing of her companion. once she ask

whether she could not help.



"there be no need," Kate answer. "shine your torch on the left. we

be nearly across."



almost as she speak they strike the sandy bottom. Jeanne fall into the

bottom of the boat. Kate, with a little laugh, spring ashore and hold

out her hand.



"come," she say, "we have cross the bad part now."



"where be we go?" Jeanne ask, a little relieved as she feel she

foot land on the sodden turf.



"towards the Hall," Kate answer. "give I your hand, if you like, or

use your torch. the way be simple enough, but we must twist and turn

to-night. it have be a flood tide, and there be great pool leave here

and there, pool that you have never see before."



"but how do you know?" Jeanne ask, in amazement. "I can see nothing."



her guide laugh contemptuously.



"I can see and I can feel," she say. "it be an instinct with I to

walk dry-footed here. to the right now--so."



"stand still for a moment," Jeanne plead. "the wind take my breath."
